Preface
This thesis is submitted as the ﬁnal project of a M.Sc degree in civil engineering at
the department of civil engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The
work has been conducted the spring semester, from January 30th to the 1st of July.
The thesis has an extent of 60 ESTC credits, divided equally between the two partic-
ipants.
Both participants had a Bachelor in civil engineering as a background prior the
current work, and both had followed the study line Bridges, Pavements and Large
Structures at DTU.
We would like to extend our sincere gratitude to all our supervisors. Michael
Joachim Andreassen for giving us the opportunity to participate in such a interest-
ing research ﬁled, in addition to general guidance in all aspects of the thesis. The
guidance of Christian Ammitzbøll Madsen has been a key factor in constructing
the FE model in Abaqus, especially the knowledge of AWI was crucial. He has also
been important to the development of the thesis, in regards of guidance into relevant
research areas. The contributions from Jens Henrik Nielsen has greatly improved
many aspects of the modelling process in Abaqus.
As the method used in the experimental work of the current thesis, is a fairly
new method, non of the technicians involved in the work had any experience with
the execution of it. Therefore we would like to extend a special thanks to Morten Røn
Andersen, the operator of the coordinate measuring machine and Henrik Petersen,
the operator of the electrical discharge machine. Their professionalism was admired,
and their solution orientation was greatly appreciated.
During the work, there were some challenges with python scripting in relation to
Abaqus. In this challenge, Lars Pilgaard Mikkelsen from the Wind Institute at DTU
gave guidance in connection us to Stergios Goutianos, whom developed a python
script of great importance to the current work. We would like to thank them both
for their willingness to help and their efﬁciency in doing so.
We would also like to thank our fellow students whom have worked in the same
ofﬁce as us, for the successful collaboration.
Finally, we would like to thank family and friends for their support during our
studies. Especially, their endurance throughout these last months.
Kongens Lyngby, July 4, 2017
Ida Lea Andreassen
Richard Hetlevik

v
Abstract
With the growing focus on green energy, monopiles have become an important part
of the offshore wind turbine industry. With a higher demand, the research interest
in regards to both increasing the feasible length of the monopiles and reducing the
can thickness and thereby reducing costs, are increased. Today, there are large un-
certainties in relation to the fatigue life of monopiles, especially where the cans are
joined by welding. The residual stresses that manifest during the welding reduces
the fatigue life of the monopiles.
This thesis has the objective of investigating the above-mentioned welding resid-
ual stresses in a 40 mm thick plate, by the means of the contour method. If the
stresses were found to be lower than the yield limit, one could either increase the
fatigue life of the monopiles or reduce the wall thickness.
In the present work a numerical simulation of the welding process was built in
Abaqus for a welded plate of steel quality S355ML. The thermal properties were
veriﬁed by experimental work conducted prior to the current work, in terms of tem-
perature measurements. The mechanical properties were to some extent present, as
temperature elevated monotonic and cyclic tests had been conducted for the steel
type used in the current work. Yielding both an isotopic and a kinematic material
model. This way, experimental values for modelling plasticity were present. The
temperature dependence of the other mechanical properties were gathered from a
PhD using the same steel type.
Knowing that a lot of the parameters used for creating the numerical model were
present, it remained to experimentally investigate the residual stress in a welded
plate. The contour method was the chosen investigation method. It is a fairly new
method, that relies on the relaxation principles of steel upon cutting. In contrast to
other investigation methods, the contour method has the ability to map the residual
stress across the entire cross section. As no one involved in the project had any
experience with the method, a key feature of the present work lies in the mapping
of the procedure and identifying challenges related to the execution.
The test specimen was a 45 mm thick plate which were welded together by sub-
merged arc welding in 17 passes. One cut was executed in the transverse plane, re-
leasing longitudinal stresses. Both of the cut surfaces were measured by a coordinate
measuring machine. The data was processed in Matlab, and the stress calculations
were executed in Abaqus.
The numerical simulation matched the experimental temperature measurements
with 9% and it was concluded that the thermal properties implemented in the model
were close to the experimental conditions. However, a small change in the thermal
conductivity might have given a higher correspondence in the cooling periods.
Experimentally, it was found that peak longitudinal stresses were 857 ± 17MPa
and 954 ± 32MPa, depending on the chosen knot density in the data processing.
From the numerical model, the same value was found to be 723MPa.

1
Chapter 1
Introduction
Numerous types of foundations are used for offshore wind turbines. One of the main
conﬁguration used are supersized monopiles. These monopiles are constructed in
various dimension, governed by the placement depth, size of the turbine and sur-
rounding environment. When designing these offshore wind turbines, the govern-
ing design criteria, are in most cases the fatigue life of the foundation structure. The
residual stresses which manifest in the welded joints, due to the localised heat in-
put, is known to be the main factor in estimating their fatigue life. The magnitude,
distribution and mechanisms regarding the residual stresses in welded joints of su-
persized monopiles are at the present time at a research stage, as this subject is prone
to optimization.
It is therefore, in the present work, conducted an experiment to investigate the
distribution and magnitude of the residual stresses in a welded plate. A numerical
simulation of the known mechanism which take place in such a weld is constructed,
and validate by the ﬁndings in the experimental work. The experiment is carried out
using the contour method. This is a fairly new method, implementing the well es-
tablished principles of relaxation measurement methods, such as the slitting method
and hole drilling method.
In the following section, a description of monopiles and the general design prac-
tises concerning them, will be given. Following this a section concerning the residual
stress, and the link to fatigue life is described, together with the usage of the contour
method. Thereafter, will the objective of this thesis be presented, followed by the
last section in this introduction, scope of work.
1.1
Monopiles - Embodiment and General Design
In the last decades a considerable increase in demand for clean energy have arisen
due to the green shift which have, and is, taking place around the world. This in-
crease in demand have contributed to larger investments and focus on the devel-
opment of larger and more efﬁcient offshore wind turbines. It was reported, that
the new investments in the offshore wind farms in Europe, for 2016, have continued
to grow strongly. In 2016, eleven projects had reached Final Investment Decision
(FID), which had a cumulative investment value of 18.2bn Euro’s [7]. This have
contributed to a large increase in commissioned new projects, were some are push-
ing the boundaries both in scale of the turbines and the depths of which they are
placed. In Figure 1.1, one can see wind farms which are online, under construction,
consented and where application are submitted in relation to the water depth and
distance from shore.

The foundations which are used for offshore wind turbines comes in numerous

The monopile foundation, is a vary widely used conﬁguration, due to the sim-

a rather simple instalment procedure, where the monopile is pile-driven deep into
==PAGE:17==

the seabed. In simple terms, the monopile is a long hollow column, which is com-
posed of several steel plate that are rolled into an circler shape, such that it resemble
a can shape. These cans are thereafter stacked and welded together. A typical hight
dimension for these cans are 3m. See Figure 1.3 for composition concept.

The averaged depth of installed monopile was in 2016, 29.2m. Monopiles which
are in this range and above, is the ones which are regarded as supersized monopile[7].
As the instalment depth increase, the diameter and material thickness also increase
accordingly. This yields an increased moment, due to the self-weight and externally
applied loads. Almost all the load subjected to the monopile is of a cyclic fashion,
resulting in the fatigue life being the governing design criteria.
In the following section, the link between the residual stresses and fatigue will
be stated.
1.2
Residual Stresses and Fatigue
All structures subjected to cyclic loads must be assessed in regard of fatigue. Fatigue
cyclic cracks manifest typically in high stress areas and weak points. Such areas are
often located in joints and especially in welded joints.
One of the reasons why welded joints are so exposed to developing cyclic cracks,
is due to the formation of welding induced residual stresses in, and around the ﬁller
material. These stresses occur due to the intense and localised heat input from the
welding processes. The welding residual stresses are deﬁned as stresses that remains
after all external loads are relieved from the material. In the present work, the load
is the thermal cycles induced by the welding. Welding residual stresses are in most
cases in tension in the welding are and the magnitude is assumed to be close to the
yield limit. Structures where welding residual stresses are present and is subjected to
cyclic loads have therefore a larger likelihood of surpassing the yield limit, resulting
in plastic deformation, thus, making it possible for initiation of crack formation.
The magnitude of residual stresses depends on several parameter; boundary
conditions, weld grove geometry and the welding procedure[20]. The numerous
==PAGE:18==
4
Chapter 1. Introduction
parameter which affect the residual stress level in a specimen, leads to difﬁculties in
measuring and numerically simulate them. Currently, there are several method of
measuring residual stresses, all with their limitations and beneﬁts. Some of the most
established method are listed below.
• Hole drilling method
• Ring core method
• Section Method
• X-ray diffraction method
• Neutron diffraction method
• Ultrasonic method
• Megnatic method
In the present work, non of the above mentioned methods are applied. It is
here been utilize a rather new method called the contour method. This method im-
plements the principles of relaxation measurement, which also is used in the hole
drilling method and sectioning method. The contour method is therefore of a de-
structive type. The results obtained from this method can potentially provide a
whole contour map of the residual stresses, manifested in a specimen throughout a
cut plane, with large uncertainties near the edges of the contour. Thus, supplement-
ing the results obtained from the hole drilling methods and section method, where
only surface stresses and shallow depth stresses can be measured. However, the con-
tour method is not portable, as it is in need of electrical discharge machining(EDM)
and coordinate controlled measuring equipment. This equipment is expensive, both
in the manner of operation and purchase. The method is also highly destructive,
where it needs to cut trough the whole specimen to expose the measuring surface.
These last mentioned statements, supports the statement that the contour method is
only relevant for research purpose.
In the assessment of fatigue, the assumption that the magnitude of residual stresses
are in range of the yield limit is implemented in standards regarding design of
offshore structures. Nevertheless, this assumption can be evaded, if the residual
stresses can be proven to be below lower. The obvious beneﬁts of this is that mate-
rial can be saved in such cases. Even small percentages of material savage, can result
in substantial savings in relation to the wind farm projects.

The work executed in this thesis is a further development of earlier performed projects,
under the supervision of Michael Joachim Andreassen. There are to main thesis un-
derlying the present work; The thesis of Christian Ammitzbøll Madsen and Daniel
Sommerlund Pedersen from 2015 [1] and the thesis of Lasse Schmidt Jessen form
2016 [39]. The former thesis, developed a welding simulation utilizing computa-
tional welding mechanics(CWM). Here, a tremendous amount of groundwork was
done, where the manner of how one constructs such a FE-model was map. How-
ever, the thermo-mechanical parameter used in this work was not extracted form

experimental work. Leading to the need of further investigation of these parame-

1.4
Scope of Work
The work done throughout this master thesis consists of three main ﬁelds; theoret-
ical study of the problem, numerical welding simulations and experimental work.
The structure of the thesis is therefore governed by the terms of these three ﬁeld.
Firstly a chapter is presented containing an overview of the methodology used
in the present work. Following, will the underlying theory of the problem at hand
be elaborated. In Chapter 3 and 4 a general description of the welding process and
residual stresses is presented. Further a detailed description of the contour method
and the numerical modelling is elaborated. The latter, must be seen in conjunction
with the last two chapter in the the theoretical part of thesis. Which contains the
thermo-mechanical analysis and numerical approach. A chapter containing the val-
idation of the welding simulation is presented in Chapter 9, which is prior to the
part containing the experimental work.
Following the theoretical part, the experimental work done in this thesis is pre-
sented. This, is sectioned into two parts; Preliminary experiment and the main ex-
periment. As the method utilized, is of the destructive type and only one specimen
was available for this thesis, it was concluded early on that a preliminary experiment
was needed. This was to acquire a good overview of the method and to eliminate
as many potential error sources as possible. A dummy specimen was here made, by
welding together two plates of thickness 15mm. The whole execution of the contour
method was thereafter conducted, reported and analysed. The preliminary work
yielded a good understanding of the method and several alteration was made before
the main experiment was initiated. The main experiment consisted of the testing of a
steel plate of type S355ML, with thickness 45mm. This plate was welded using sub-
merged arc welding and was the result of a special course conducted by Andersen

Chapter 2
Methodology/ Research approach
In this thesis the main objective is to investigate welding residual stresses by the
means of the contour method. To this end, a thorough review of the current avail-
able research was conducted to ensure the credibility of the present work. This re-
view included both research in relation to the numerical model, being the Abaqus
simulation of the weldig and the experimental work conducted by implementing
the contour method.
2.1
Numerical modelling
The numerical model was built on the basis of guidance from Christian Ammitzbøll
Madsen, as he had previously created a model of the same character. In addition,
some books regarding computational welding mechanics (CWM) were review:
• Computational Welding Mechanics by J.A. Goldak and M. Akhlaghi (2005)
• Heat Effects of Welding - Temperature Field, Residaul Stress, Distortion by D. Radaj
(1992)
• Welding Metallurgy by S. Kou (2003)
These were the main literatures used to create the numerical model and write
the theory behind the model. These are all well known book, and considered to be
a valid sources of information. In addition, there was a PhD that was frequently
mentioned in the above literature:
• Numerical Modelling of Welding Induced Stresses by J.L Hansen (2003)
Especially, the work Hansen did with material properties was considered to be
of relevance to the present work.
2.2

amount of data available. Before starting the experimental work a documentary
analysis was necessary to map the available information as a part of the qualitative
research.
The research approach for the experimental work needed to be different from
the approach for the numerical model, as the contour method is a fairly new method
and the available information is not as widely acknowledged. In the present work
it was chosen to start with the source of the method, Michael B. Prime of the Los

Chapter 3
Welding process
The main objective in the current work is the investigation of residual stresses from
welding. To fully understand how these stresses develop, one need to have an un-
derstanding of the welding process in it self. Which welding method is used, what
does the welding terms means and how can one describe the various zones of the
weld. With this chapter, one aims to answer these questions and create a clear basis
for the continuing chapters.
3.1
Fusion welding
One category within welding is the fusion weld, which is the process of joining ma-
terial by introducing heat. Fusion welds often involve metal, and two different ma-
terials are often used; A base material which is the material that is to be joined, and
a ﬁller material with the purpose of creating a melting pool that will bind the base
material together upon cooling.
There are many welding techniques, each one with different execution proce-
dures and distinctive end results. The main difference between the end results of
the methods lies in the heat that is used to create the weld pool. There is also a great
variation in the shape of the weld pools, this mainly depends on the power density
[W/mm2] of the welding method.
The three main fusion welding categories are gas welding, arc welding and high-
energy beam welding, were there are several welding methods for each category.
Some main characteristics regarding the heat sources for the three categories are
presented in Figure 3.1.
In Figure 3.1 one can see that the arc welding has high heat input to the work-
piece and low power density. This will produce a weld bead that is rather shallow
and wide. On the other hand, there is the high-energy beam welding. This can for
instance can be laser welding, which have exactly the opposite features; low heat
input and high power density. This kind of weld-method creates a narrow and deep
bead.
In the present work, a piece that is welded using the submerged arc welding
method is investigated. On that account, only this method will be further discussed.
3.2
Submerged arc welding
Submerged arc welding (SAW) is a fusion welding process in which merging of met-
als occurs by heating an arc between a consumable electrode and a work piece. This
process takes place under a layer of granular fusible ﬂux that protects both the arc
and the weld. In this sense, the process is submerged. The granular ﬂux holds
multiple features that are beneﬁcial for the process. For one, the ﬂux protects the
==PAGE:24==
10
Chapter 3. Welding process
FIGURE 3.1: [14].
welding against atmospheric contamination. It also aids in stabilizing the arc dur-
ing the welding. Finally, it suppresses splatter and sparks, in addition to radiation
and fumes from the process [14].
The overall process is presented in Figure 3.2, where one can see the arc melting
the base material and the wire electrode in the metal pool. Then, when cooled down
the weld metal is solid and there is a layer of solidiﬁed slag covering the weld. At
the very top of the soldiﬁed slag there are some remains of the granular ﬂux, which
were not molten. These remains can be reused if not contaminated [14].
There are different types of welding currents, and the most common one in SAW
is the direct current positive. This means that the electrode polarity is positive and
constant in one direction. When the electrode polarity is positive the heat balance
between the work-end and the electrode-end is approximately 30 % and 70 %, re-
spectively. The low power density of the heat source for arc welding yields a shallow
weld penetration [14].
Alloying elements can be used as additives to the granular ﬂux with the purpose
of better controlling the composition of the weld material. Another additive is metal
powder with the feature of expediting the deposition rate. Other ways of increasing
the deposition rate is to increase the current [14].
3.3
Terminology
3.3.1
Welding Geometry
In joints made using fusion welding, the area in and around the weld have distinct
microstructural regions. The two main zones are called fusion zone and Heat Af-
fected Zone(HAZ). Prior to the 1960s, these two zones were believed to be the only
microstructure zones of importance in a fusion weld. During extensive studies, done
by W.F. Savage and his students in the 1960s and 1970s, it was found that both the
fusion zones and HAZ could be subdivided further in to smaller regions[19, p.11].
These subregions will be elaborated on later in this section. The fusion zone is asso-
ciated with melting and solidiﬁcation, in contrast to the HAZ, which is the transition
==PAGE:25==
3.3. Terminology
11
FIGURE 3.2: (a) - The overall setup for SAW, (b) - Detailed sketch of
welding area [14].
zone from the fusion zone to the unaffected base material. This area is not associated
with melting or solidiﬁcation and have at all times mechanical properties to a certain
extent. Figure 3.3 illustrate the two zones used prior to 1960s.
FIGURE
3.3:
Illustration
of
fusion
zone
and
Heat
Affected
Zone(HAZ)[19, p.11].
As mentioned, did Savage et al. perform studies concerning the two zones shown
in Figure 3.3 during the 1960s and 1970s. In 1976, they proposed several changes to
the terminology which were to describing the found subregions of the microstruc-
ture in fusion welds. The established subregions were as shown in Figure 3.4.
==PAGE:26==
12
Chapter 3. Welding process
FIGURE 3.4: [19, p.11].
In the studies of Savage et al., the fusion zone is divided into two subregions;
the composite region and the unmixed zone(UMZ). In the composite region melted
ﬁller- and base material mix together into a "composite” material. The intermedi-
ate unmixed zone(UMZ) between the composite region and the fusion boundary,
consists of re-solidiﬁed and melted base material which do not mix with the ﬁller
material. It was discovered that the properties of this two zones could be severely
different, especially when the ﬁller material is dissimilar in relation to the base ma-
terial.
Same as for the fusion zone, were the HAZ divided into two subregions; the Par-
tially Melted Zone(PMZ) and the True Heat-Affected Zone(T-HAZ). In fusion welds
a transition form 100% liquid to 100% solid must occur across the fusion border,
therefore will a PMZ always be present in fusion welds. The T-HAZ establish the
region where all metallurgical reactions occur in the solid state, thus, no melting
and/or liquation occurs.
Post 1976, very little have changed in the terminology of heterogeneous fusion
welds. Although, many studies have been conducted, most of them have only con-
tributed with the fact that these regions actually occur in different types of alloys.
The only change which has remained, is the adding of a transition zone(TZ) in some
alloy systems with heterogeneous fusion welds and the subdividing of the T-HAZ
in steel to coarse-grained HAZ (CGHAZ), the ﬁne-grained HAZ (FGHAZ) and the
intercritical HAZ (ICHAZ) regions. The TZ is the transition zone between the com-
posite zone and the UMZ, which may occur in some alloy systems[19, p.12]. The
most modern schematic of the terminology is illustrated in Figure 3.5. In the next
subsection, a brief deﬁnition of the three types of fusion zone si given.
Fusion Zones
It is importer to distinguish between the three types of fusion zones which have been
deﬁned by Lindgren [19], as the welding geometry for the three is not necessarily
identical, as mentions in 3.3.1. The three types of fusion zones are as following; au-
togeneous, homogeneous and heterogeneous. This categorization is based upon whether
or not a ﬁller is used and if the ﬁller is of a similar metal in respect to the base metal.
Autogeneous fusion zone is generated in the case where no ﬁller is added to the
weld. Therefore, is the fusion zone comprised of solidiﬁed base material. This type
of fusion zone is common where the section is of small thickness, where penetration
is efﬁciently accomplish. The fusion zone is approximately the same as the base
==PAGE:27==
3.4. S355ML
13
FIGURE 3.5: Schematic presentation of the different zones in a fusion
weld [19, p.12].
material, only minimal changes to the composition can occur due to evaporation or
pickup of gases from the shielding atmosphere.
Homogeneous fusion zones occurs when the ﬁller material is almost identical to
the base material. This type of weld is often used in cases where it is needed to
have the same material properties throughout the weld and base material, e.g. such
properties can be corrosion properties and/or heat treatment response.
Heterogeneous fusion zone arises in the situation where the ﬁller material is dif-
ferent to the composition of the base material. In many cases the base metal is of
a type which possess undesirable welding properties, therefore, a dissimilar ﬁller
metal is needed to be able to achieve acceptable properties or service performance
in the weld.
3.4
S355ML
The most commonly used steel in offshore constructions is of type S355ML. This steel
is a thermomechanically hot rolled, low carbon, low alloyed, ﬁne-grained structural
steel and is produced according to European standard DS/EN 10025-4. This steel
possesses several material properties that are desirable for offshore constructions,
e.g. great weldability and high strength properties at low temperature[3]. The low
carbon content in S355ML leads to a lower likelihood of cold creaking compared to
S355NL(Normalized rolled) and also increases the toughness in the HAZ. The com-
position of steel S355ML is tabulated in Table 3.1 and the steel inspection certiﬁcate
can be viewed in [39, p.178].

Chapter 4
Residual stresses
Knowing how the welding process takes place, one has better understanding of the
residual stresses that arises. Now, the concept in it self will be elaborated on. First,
by a short introduction to the deﬁnition and classiﬁcation of residual stresses that
are adapted in the current work is presented. Then, the welding aspect will be in
focus, in terms of growth of residual stresses in steel when affected by heat. Finally,
decisive factors for the magnitude and distribution of the stresses are discussed.
4.1
Residual stresses Deﬁnition and classiﬁcation
Sindo Kou [14] deﬁnes residual stresses as stresses that would exist in a body if all
external loads were removed.
This statement is the purest form of the deﬁnition of residual stresses and is
widely excepted. These type of stresses are also referred to as "locked-in" stresses
and internal stresses. Residual stresses are self-equilibrating in the sense that all of
the residual stresses in a body sum up to zero force and zero moment, yielding a
balanced state between tension and compression.
Residual stresses develop in practically all manufacturing processes and there-
fore nearly all material possesses these stresses. The main mechanisms for generat-
ing residual stresses according to Schajer and Ruud [38] are:
• Non-uniform plastic deformations
• Surface modiﬁcations
• Material phase and/or density change, often in presents of large thermal gra-
dients
Non-uniform plastic deformations are often a product of manufacturing pro-
cesses were the shape of the material is altered in some way. Examples include
forging, rolling, bending, drawing and extrusion. The second mechanism listed is
the surface modiﬁcations, which is also related to the manufacturing process. Typi-
cal processes that induce residual stresses are machining, grinding, plating, peening
and carburizing. Finally, there is the material phase change and density change.
Exemplary processes are welding, casting, quenching and phase transformation in
metals and ceramics. [38].
Later on in section 6.3, the importance of classiﬁcation in regards to residual
stress will be clariﬁed. A common way to classify this type of stress is by the length
which the stresses equalizes in relation to tension and compression. Figure 4.1 shows
an illustration of the levels of which the stresses are categorized by.
The ﬁrst order of residual stresses, σ1, averages over a macroscopic area, from
a millimeter perspective and upwards. This includes several material grains. Here,
==PAGE:30==
16
Chapter 4. Residual stresses
thermo-mechanics are cause of the stresses in terms of temperature, stress/strain
and displacements. It is within this range the effect of peening, bending and weld-
ing are measured. Second order residual stresses averages over a microscopic level
(10−3 to 10−5), meaning within each grain and between adjoining grains. The third
order of residual stresses averages at an even smaller microscopic level, as far as
micrometers (10−5 to 10−9). This corresponds to inter-atomic mechanism, as dislo-
cations and imperfections inside the crystallites [1].
FIGURE 4.1: Classiﬁcation of the ﬁrst, second and third order of resid-
ual stresses with corresponding mechanisms. The principles are from
D. Radaj [35] and the illustration is by J.L. Hansen [15].
4.2
Residual stresses from welding
When welding, high temperatures are discharged on a relative small area, creating
a local molten region surrounded by cold material. When heated, the welding area
wants to expand, but is restricted by the adjacent cold material. If only heated within
the elastic region, the material would return to its initial state when the non-uniform
temperature was removed. Also, if the material was subjected to a uniform temper-
ature change, meaning that the whole part was heated and cooled down at the same
rate, no residual stresses would remain in the part from this exposure. However,
some plastic deformations are bound to set in during the welding and obviously the
whole part is not uniformly heated, just the welding area. This means that there are
residual stresses in the welded part.
The three bar arrangement by Kou [14] is used to explain the thermal effects on
the specimen during the course of the welding. Here considering three phases as
presented in Figure 4.2, initial conditions, heating of the middle bar and the cooled
down state. In this arrangement the bars are restrained from movement in the lon-
gitudinal direction, rather than by the rigid connection between the bars as is more
representative for a plate. First, all the bars have room temperature, then, the middle
bar is heated until the compressive yield stress is reached. The increase in tempera-
ture causes the middle bar to expand, but the restraints in the longitudinal direction
prevents it from doing so, leaving the middle bar in compression. If the material is
==PAGE:31==
4.2. Residual stresses from welding
17
compressed within the elastic region, the ﬁnal state will reﬂect the initial state, how-
ever if plastic compressive stresses are achieved the middle bar will want to shrink
beyond the initial length and cause tension as the temperature in the material drops.
As residual stresses are self-equilibrating the outer bars will have to balance the mid-
dle one at all times and therefore end up with compression equal half the tension in
the middle bar.
FIGURE 4.2: Sketch inspired by Sindo Kou [14] showing the three
stages of thermal induced stresses
The situation explained above reﬂects a scenario where the whole length of the
weld (one bead) is heated at ones, this is not in accordance with the welding pro-
cedure where one bead is gradually placed and the temperature ﬁeld of the weld
affects the distribution. In Figure 4.3 the welding process is illustrated with the
temperature change and corresponding longitudinal residual stress for a butt-weld
plate. The principle described with the three bar arrangement is now applied to the
moving weld, where situation A is the initial state with no residual stresses from the
welding. Situation B shows compression near the heated area of the weld, corre-
sponding to sate (b) in Figure 4.2. There are no compressive stresses at the center of
the weld, where the temperature is inﬁnite, as the material here will be in a liquid
state. In situation C and D the weld material has started to cool down and the ma-
terial regains it stiffness and thereby its ability to resist deformations caused by the
temperature change. This leads to the tension state described in (c) [35].
FIGURE 4.3: Development of longitudinal residual stresses with re-
spect to the temperature during the welding process [1].
==PAGE:32==
18
Chapter 4. Residual stresses
In Figure 4.3 only the longitudinal stress is shown, this is not without aim. The
previously stated effects of welding will of course induce stresses in all three dimen-
sions - longitudinal, transverse and through-thickness. The longitudinal residual
stresses are caused by contractions in the longitudinal direction as the HAZ cools
down. The magnitude of these stresses are expected to be in the range of the yield
limit. This is not the case for the other two directions. The transverse residual stress
is a result of the contractions in the transverse direction as the HAZ cools down, but
also a indirect result of contractions in the longitudinal direction. Further more, the
variation of the cooling process through out the height of the cross section is a factor
to consider. The residual stresses in the three directions are presented in Figure 4.4
and show that the longitudinal direction has the most substantial stresses.
FIGURE 4.4: Transverse, through-thickness and longitudinal residual
stress [35].
As previously stated, for the residual stress to remain after the removal of the
temperature change, the material has to undego plastic deformations. In Figure 4.5
the plastic-elastic thermal stress ﬁeld during welding is shown. Around the heat
source there are little stresses due to the drop of the yield limit at elevated temper-
atures. The dashed line indicates the maximum temperature of the weld, where the
local temperatures increases in front of the line and decreases behind. Increasing
temperatures means that the material expands and surrounding areas are "pushed",
yielding negative strain and compression. When compressed beyond the yield limit,
plastic deformation arise, resulting in the plastic compression zone ahead of the
dashed line. The opposite can be seen in the plastic tension zone, where the contrac-
tions during cooling creates positive strain and tension. At the edge of the plastic
tension zone in Figure 4.5 a cyclic presentation of the stress-strain relationship is pre-
sented. First, the material reaches compressive yield limit, then it unloads elastically
into the tensile stress. Thirdly, the tensile yield limit is reached and lastly, it unloads
elastically the other way [35].
FIGURE 4.5: The plastic-elastic thermal stress ﬁeld of a moving heat
source .
==PAGE:33==
4.3. Factors inﬂuencing the residual stresses
19
4.3
Factors inﬂuencing the residual stresses
4.3.1
Material properties
The characteristics values of a material inﬂuences the behaviour and thereby also
the creation of residual stresses. According to Radaj [35] the following parameters
are the most inﬂuential:
• The melting temperature, Tm. Using a material with higher melting temper-
ature means that more heat must be applied to the piece and thereby induc-
ing more residual stresses and deformations. With this in mind, a material
like aluminium (Tm = 600◦C) would be better suited to weld, than titanium
(Tm = 1800◦C)
• Thermal diffusivity, a. This is a product of the thermal conductivity divided
by the density and speciﬁc heat capacity, giving a measure of the equilibrating
rate of the material. In the sense of minimizing residual stresses, low values of
thermal diffusivity is to be aimed for.
• Thermal expansion, α. As previously stated, residual stresses from welding
develops when the material is restrained from deforming. In consequence,
less expansion yields less deformations, resulting in less residual stresses. Ti-
tanium has relatively low thermal expansion, and would therefore be more
appropriate than aluminium.
• Elastic modulus, E. The measure of the materials ability to resist elastic de-
formations. Accordingly, low elastic modulus yield large deformations as it
can not resist to deform, but also less residual stresses develop as it is not re-
strained by its own stiffness. Steel has a relatively high elastic modulus and
thereby creates less deformations, but more residual stresses.
• Yield limit, fy. For the residual stresses to remain after thermal effect is re-
moved, the material has to reach the yield limit during the heating. Elevated
yield limits generates higher residual stresses, both in the regards of peak val-
ues and average values. The deformation energy that is saved in the weld is
then increased compared to a material with lower yield limit. As a result, the
structure becomes a more brittle one. Conversely, the deformation are reduced
by the smaller and less extensive plastic strains.
All, but the ﬁrst items are temperature dependent and will be further discussed
in chapter 7.3 and 8.3. But a general tendency is that mechanical properties (α, E
and fy ) drops as the temperature increases.
4.3.2
Temperature ﬁeld
The temperature ﬁeld of a weld depends on several parameters, two of most signif-
icant ones being welding speed and heat input. If welding with higher speed and
heat input the welding pool will resemble an ellipse rather than a circle. In Figure 4.6
a large temperature gradient over barely 4 cm is found, this is for a welding speed
of 2.4 mm/s and a heat input of 3200 W. This level of heat input causes volumetric
expansion, but also plastic deformations.
==PAGE:34==
20
Chapter 4. Residual stresses
FIGURE 4.6: Left: Temperature gradient. Right: Temperature ﬁeld
[14].
4.3.3
Cooling rate
The micro-structure and hardness of the HAZ highly depend upon the cooling rate
according to Masubuchi [22].
Cooling is a consequence of the heat being dispersed from the welding pool to
the surrounding areas. The cooling rate are affected by several parameters, such as;
plate thickness , preheating temperature, heat input, type of electrode coating, weld
length and joint geometry.
4.3.4
Weld length and width
To determine the effects of the length of the weld, experiments was conducted by De-
Garmo, Meriam and Jonassen i 1946 [12]. A series of plates were welded with only
one changing parameter, the length of the weld. Their results are shown in Figure
4.7. From their experiment, one can clearly see that there are little longitudinal resid-
ual stresses for short welds, and as the weld lengthens the residual stresses increases
and stagnates after a while. The experiment revealed that for an unrestrained 25 mm
thick carbon steel plate that was butt-welded with SAW in two passes the stagnation
point is near 257 mm. For the longitudinal direction the residual stresses are bound
to be zero at the weld ends and great tensile stresses are found in the center of the
weld. The experiment focused on the maximum tensile stresses for the determina-
tion of this length.
In the transverse direction the residual stresses were found to be in compression
at the weld ends and tensile in the center of the weld. The maximum value of both
the compression zone and the tension zone was not notably effected by the length of
the weld in the current experiment.
The width of the specimen has a negligible impact on the residual stresses as
long as its more than several times the width of the HAZ.
4.4
Deformations
Previously in this chapter, stress have been thoroughly reviewed. A contrast to this
may be the deformations, being that stresses develop when restrained from defor-
mation.
In Figure 4.8 some typical deformations modes related to the welding process is
presented. There is transverse shrinkage (a), which will depend on the transverse
==PAGE:35==
4.4. Deformations
21
FIGURE 4.7: Effect of length on residual stresses [12]
constraints, namely the stiffness of the material and transverse length from weld to
edge. In (b) the longitudinal shrinkage is shown, also here the end constraints and
stiffness of material becomes of importance. The angular distortion is presented in
(c). To minimize this distortion, especially in multi-pass welds, one can use alternat-
ing welding rather than welding one side at a time. In addition, the angle of the base
material can be a factor for reducing angular distortion. Reduced angle often results
in reduced distortion. This type of deformations becomes greater with increasing
plate thickness and if restrained at edges it could lead to bending distortion. Lastly,
the rotative distortion is displayed. Opposite to the angular distortion, the likeness
of this type of deformation increases with reduced plate thickness.
FIGURE 4.8: Typical welding deformations [46]
Obviously, if exclusively considering the deformations one would want them
eliminated. However, if this is at the cost of increased residual stresses and other
debilitating effects, one would need to reconsider.

==PAGE:37==
23
Chapter 5
Contour Method
To determine the residual stresses from welding one has to conduct some experimen-
tal work. There are several widely excepted measuring methods for investigating
residual stresses. One often distinguishes between destructive and non-destructive
measuring methods, where the contour method belongs to the former as the main
principle of the method lies in the relaxation of the material upon cutting. With
this method one obtains measurements of the residual stresses throughout the cross
section of the specimen, mapping the full contour of the piece. Hence, the name,
contour method.
In the following chapter a general introduction to the method is presented before
the a more in depth examination of the theory behind the key elements of the method
is reviewed.
The following chapter is based on the works of M. B. Prime and the Los Alamos
National Laboratory regarding the contour method as a practical residual stress
measurement method.
5.1
Main concept
5.1.1
Introduction
The contour method is a fairly new method (2000-2001) to map residual stresses
through relaxation measurements. The method consists of three phases. First, the
material is cut into two pieces using a wire electrical discharge machine (EDM).
Then, the contour of the cut-surface is measured and ﬁnally the contour is modelled
in a ﬁnite element program.
FIGURE 5.1: Measurement penetration vs Spatial resolution for sev-
eral measuring methods [38]
==PAGE:38==
24
Chapter 5. Contour Method
The contour method is suitable for a wide rage of materials. Nevertheless, it is
essential that the material i electrically conductive, more about this in chapter 5.2.
The geometry of the specimen can also vary, but if the specimen has a cross section
smaller than a 5 mm by 5 mm, the precision requirement increase signiﬁcantly. The
contour method is a full-ﬁeld measurement, meaning that the full height, depth and
thickness is measured. In ﬁgure 5.1 it can be seen that the contour method is suitable
for thickness from a few millimetre up to a meter.
The method is a variation of the Bueckner’s superposition principle.
5.1.2
Measurement principle
Theoretical implementation
In the ﬁrst picture (A) in Figure 5.2 the original stresses are shown on a quarter of
a specimen. Next, the specimen is cut in two and the stress in x-direction (σx) is
relaxed close to the "cut" surface. The surface itself is fully relaxed in x-direction, but
only partially relaxed in z-direction. In the third picture the deformation from the
relaxations are forced back ﬂat, so that σx on the surface is the same as the original
σx from picture A.
FIGURE 5.2:
Super-positioning principle applied for the contour
method [38].
==PAGE:39==
5.1. Main concept
25
From the ﬁgure above one can intuitively see that by superimposing the stress
state presented in B with the forced stress state presented in C, the result will be the
original stress state (A). This can also be shown mathematically, as in Eq. 5.1 where
σ presents the entire stress tensor and the superscript is the steps presented in Figure
5.2.
σA(x, y, z) = σB(x, y, z) + σC(x, y, z)
(5.1)
At the fully relaxed surface in B, σx, τxy and τxz are all zero, this means that the
original distribution of those stresses can be uniquely determined at x=0 as shown
in A. To make the theory complete one would need in-plane measurements of dis-
placements from the cut surface.
Practical implementation
The previously explained principle of the super-positioning is implemented together
with a few assumptions to experimentally determine the residual stresses normal to
the plane of the cut. The measurements of the contour only gives information about
the displacements normal to the plane of the cut. This is the only information pro-
vided by the experiment and therefore restricts the analytical approach. By elasti-
cally forcing the expanded surface back to its original state in the x-direction only, all
the transverse displacements are left undisturbed. As the transverse displacements
are left out, the shear stresses will automatically impose the free surface conditions
where they are equal to zero for both planes (τxy and τxz).
When cutting the specimen some shear stresses are released on the plane of the
cut. The surface tractions are presented as components in x- and y-direction in Fig-
ure 5.3 and further derived in Eq. 5.2.
Tx = −σxnx
Ty = −τxynx
(5.2)
Where nx is the surface normal vector. In Figure 5.3 it is seen that the tractions
from releasing the normal stress are symmetrical about the cut plane, while the trac-
tions from releasing the shear stress are anti-symmetrical about the cut plane. Thus,
when the two contours from a cut are averaged, the shear stress and transverse
displacement will cancel out, leaving only the effects from release of the residual
stresses normal to the surface.
FIGURE 5.3: Surface tractions after cut. The normal tractions Tx is
seen to be symmetric about the cut plane and the transverse tractions
Ty are anti-symmetric [30].
==PAGE:40==
26
Chapter 5. Contour Method
As both the normal and shear stresses in B are equal to zero the following can be
found from the condition in Eq. 5.1:
σA
x = σC
x
τ A
xy = τ C
xy
τ A
xz = τ C
xz
(5.3)
Using this together with the boundary conditions (global equilibrium) of forces
in x-direction and moment about the y- and z-axis, the original stresses in A can be
uniquely determined [32].
Assumptions and Approximations
• Elastic behavior of the material. When the stresses are released upon cutting,
one has assumed that the material behaves elastically. To this end the super-
position principle can be implemented.
• No stress is induced in the specimen by cutting. To introduce as little distur-
bance as possible when cutting, a no contact method is used (wire EDM). It is
assumed that the material removal by this cut does not effect the displacements
of the contour at any degree of importance.
• A ﬂat surface as base. This assumption is plainly there to ease the modelling
process. The surface of the cut is not modelled before step C in Figure 5.2, it is
assumed to be a ﬂat surface as in step A.
• The specimen is symmetric about the cut plane. As explained earlier, the
two contours from each part after cutting is averaged to remove shear stresses.
This introduce another assumption; the stiffness is the same on both sides of
the cut. This can be achieved by using a homogeneous material and making
sure that the specimen is cut precisely in half. In practice, the stiffness only
needs to be equal for the part that signiﬁcantly effects the deformations of the
cut surface. The signiﬁcant part can usually be estimated to be 1.5 times the
St. Venant’s characteristic distance measured from the cut surface. As a con-
servative estimate of the characteristic distance the maximum cross-sectional
dimension can be used.
• Errors from anti-symmetric cutting will be averaged away. If the specimen
were to move during the cut or the cutting itself were to wander, this would
lead to a anti-symmetric cut. This is usually not a cause of error as the two con-
tours are averaged as seen in Figure 5.4. The contour marked red in Figure 5.4
is mirrored so that the points opposite of each other after the cut are compared.
FIGURE 5.4: Principle of averaging an anti-symmetrical cut [30].
==PAGE:41==
5.1. Main concept
27
5.1.3
Practical Measurement Procedures
Cutting procedure
Some of the key features of the cutting procedure is presented in below, and will
further discussed in Section 5.2.
• Gives a straight cut
• Leaves a smooth surface
• Has minimal cut width (kerf)
• Removes as little material as possible from the allredy cut surface
• Does not leave any plastic deformations
• Does not induce any residual stress
The wire electrical discharge machine (EDM) is the currently best choice to fulﬁl
the above listed features. One of the main reasons for this is the fact that the cut-
ting is of the sort non-contact (between the wire and specimen), yielding no plastic
deformations on the surface of the cut, as oppose to conventional cutting machines.
The cutting error, bulge error (explained in Section 5.2.4) increase with the diam-
eter of the wire and it is therefore desirable to chose a wire diameter as small as
possible, to the extent that the wire does not break during cutting or use unreason-
able cutting time. The minimal robust wire is chosen based on the material being
cut and the EDM used. A brass wire is expected to give the best result, as oppose to
coated wires as seen in Fig 13 in the paper by M.B. Prime from 2001 [30].
The contour method relies heavily on a precision cut, and the reliability of the
method is directly linked to the quality of the cut. The recommended settings are
low power and high injection, which yields the best surface ﬁnish. These settings
are often referred to as "skim" or "ﬁnish" cut settings. Machines vary in options
for setting, but often there is a rough cut choice and three-four options for skim
cuts. Even though one desires the best cut, one should take into consideration the
possibility of wire breakage or extremely slow cutting if choosing the option of the
ﬁnest of the skim cuts.
Several papers suggest that the specimen should be submerged in the temperature-
controlled water before clamping it, in order to ensure that the clamping is sufﬁcient
after any dimensional changes in the specimen due to thermal effects. The machine
should be programmed to carry out the cut in one pass. Thereafter, the specimen
should be removed from the machine and the contour rinsed. Here it is imperative
that the integrity of the contour is conserved [33].
Measurement of the contour
The typical values for the peak-to-valley measurement is in the order of 10 µm to
250 µm. To visualize, this is in the same range as the diameter of a thin human
hair. For measurement to this precision, a coordinate measuring machine (CMM) is
commonly used. However, there are papers where laser scanning has been adopted
with great success [33].
After the specimen is cut, there are two parts each with a "relaxed" surface (trans-
verse plane at cut) that are to be measured. Prime and Dewald suggests that the two
==PAGE:42==
28
Chapter 5. Contour Method
parts should be placed in front of the CMM which is programed to obtain measure-
ments form the entire surface with spacing adequate to correctly determine the dis-
placement ﬁeld. If starting with no prior knowledge about the necessary measure-
ment density, one could start with a coarse grid to get a improved understanding of
the displacement ﬁeld, and then adjust to a ﬁner grid that will capture the necessary
detail level of the ﬁeld. The coarse grid will give a indication of the tendency of the
ﬁeld, and smooth out the potential roughness of the cut surface.
To clarify, a sketch showing the a speciemen with its respective planes of direc-
tions is shown in Figure 5.5.
FIGURE 5.5: Illustrative representation of the reference planes relative
to the weld marked as a thick gray line [10].
5.2
Wire EDM
5.2.1
Introduction
When utilizing the contour method, the approach used to cut the test specimen is
crucial to assure correct deformation measurements. The contour method requires
a cutting process which possesses a variety of characteristics; cut-plane must be
straight and smooth, minimal cut width(kerf), removal of material at already cut
surface must be kept to a minimal and induction of new residual stresses must not
arise, nor must plastic deformations occur. At this point of time, the only cutting
approach capable of best fulﬁlling this characteristics, is the wire electrical discharge
machining(WEDM) technique. WEDM, is a cutting process where a wire is electri-
cally charged with respect to the work-piece. Sparks arise from the electrical dis-
charge and precisely erodes material locally from the work-piece. This method is
a non-contact cutting process, as the wire and the work-piece should at no point
come in contact. Hence, no tool force and plastic deformation can occur due to the
cutting[38, p.115-116].
5.2.2
Discharge Mechanism
Set-up
The main set-up of WEDM consists of a wire, a workpiece, supply/take-in spool,
two wire guides and a work tank containing dielectric ﬂuid. This is illustrated in
Figure 5.6. As WEDM is a thermal process induced by an electrical mechanism it
is essential that both the wire(cathode) and the workpiece(anode) possess electri-
cal conductivity properties, so that electrons can be transported from the workpiece
to the wire. The function of the supply/take-in spools, is to feed the wire past the
==PAGE:43==
5.2. Wire EDM
29
workpiece. Without this wire-feeding, a breakage of the wire would be unavoidable
due to the fact that both the wire and workpiece erode as arches form. The dielectric
ﬂuid surrounding the workpiece and circulating through the spark gap, serve a num-
ber of purposes. Firstly, as an insulator which suppress the spark to form until the
needed electrical voltage is applied to cause it to change into an electrical conductor,
thus, an electric arch can occur. Further, it work as a cooler for the eroded material
and the local area on the workpiece and the wire effected by the spark. Lastly, it
ﬂushes the eroded material chips away from the spark gap [11, p.1-3]. In the next
two sections, details regarding the spark mechanism and the dielectric ﬂuid will be
elaborated.
FIGURE 5.6: Illustration of main set-up of WEDM [13, p.44].
Spark Mechanism
As mentioned in the prior section, the distant between wire and workpiece is of
great importance. This distances or gap is addressed as the spark gap and must be
obtained at all times during the WEDM to ensure that no contact between the wire
and workpiece occur. Additionally, the sparking gap need to be small enough that
when the desired voltage is reached a spark will occur. Therefore, the magnitude of
the spark gap is obtained by a balance between the two aspects just mentioned, and
is largely governed by the desired voltage input and isolation factor of the dielectric
ﬂuid. In the case of contact, the wire will break off due to fuse between the wire and
workpiece, which will lead to stopping the machining process and rethreading of the
wire before the WEDM can be resumed. At the point of breakage, the fusion of the
wire and workpiece will result in a irregular surface, which is highly undesirable[11,
p.1]. Figure 5.7, illustrates a plane view of where the spark gap is situated relative to
the wire and workpiece.
An important aspect of the sparking mechanism is that at any given point in
time, during WEDM, only one spark is taking place. The frequency range, 2,000-
500,000 sparks pre. second, that is used in WEDM, contributes to the illusion of
multiple sparks occurring simultaneously. Sparks arise between the electrodes and
moves from one place to another on the electrodes. The location where the spark
occur is always located at the point where the distance between the wire and work-
piece is smallest. As a spark forms, the material from both the wire and workpiece
will be vaporized. Therefore, making, sequentially, the second smallest distance the
smallest distance and also the location for the next spark to form. Below in Figure
5.8 illustrations of this sequence of spark location is shown.
==PAGE:44==
30
Chapter 5. Contour Method
FIGURE 5.7: Illustration of spark mechanism in WEDM [11, p.19].
FIGURE 5.8: Left: Spark that arise at the narrowest point between
wire and workpiece. Right: Subsequent spark that arise at the new
narrowest point between wire and workpiece [11, p.3-4].
As mentioned previously, intense heat induced by the spark is the main element
which erode material from the workpiece. However, one of the advantageous fea-
tures that is associated with EDM, is that the workpiece is not warmer than to the
touch during machining. Although the area where sparking affect the material is
of high temperature, this is of no concern due to the smallness of the area affected.
The dielectric ﬂuid have no difﬁculties with rapidly cooling down the area. Thus,
minimising the heat affected area. The heat generated locally due to the spark is of
such intensely that it vaporizes the material where the wire and workpiece is clos-
est. This vaporized material from both the wire and workpiece, which is described
as a cloud, is located in the sparking gap. When the spark ceases, the cloud solidiﬁes
into an EDM chip or a tiny hollow sphere due to the cooling effect from the dielectric
ﬂuid. As the EDM chip or tiny sphere is located in the spark gap, this particles must
be removed to insure efﬁcient machining. This is accomplished by ﬂushing dielec-
tric ﬂuid true the spark gap [11, p.2-5]. In Figure 5.9a, 5.9b and 5.9c, the process of
material removal is illustrated.
==PAGE:45==
5.2. Wire EDM
31
(A) Generation of vapor cloude from wire
and workpiece during sparking[11, p.6].
(B) Suspension of the vapor cloud in
spark gap before solidiﬁcation, after
the sparkt hav ceased[11, p.6].
(C) Solidiﬁed material from wire and
workpieces ready to be ﬂushed out
of the spark gap[11, p.7].
FIGURE 5.9
Dielectric Fluid
Dielectric ﬂuid is necessary to obtain the spark gap between the anode and the cath-
ode. In the WEDM process, this ﬂuid is normally deionized water. The main feature
of the dielectric ﬂuid are the electric insulation properties, and to maintaining these
properties until the desired voltage is reached between the anode and cathode. At
this point the dielectric ﬂuid should convert locally, at the narrowest point between
the anode and cathode, to an electrical conductor and enable sparks to arise. At the
point where the dielectric ﬂuid reach conversion is known as the ionization point.
As the spark ceases the dielectric ﬂuid deionizes and returns to be an electrical in-
sulator. This sequence, of changing from an insulator to an electrical conductor, and
==PAGE:46==
32
Chapter 5. Contour Method
back to an insulator, happens for each spark that is generated between the anode
and cathode [11, p.3-4].
As electricity ﬂow from the anode to the cathode, in the form of sparks, heat is
generated. This heat is dissipated trough two mediums; the workpiece/wire and the
dielectric ﬂuid. The intense and local heat generated by the sparks, is able to vapor-
ize a small piece of both the wire and workpiece. However, since the area effected is
of such a small magnitude, the heat is easily distributed through the dielectric ﬂuid
and the bulk material in the workpiece and wire. In the cases where high amperes
are utilized, cooled dielectric ﬂuid is needed to prevent the heat of the workpiece to
elevate higher than the warmth of touch [11, p.155].
As mentioned earlier, the spark generates a vapour cloud consisting of material
from both the wire and workpiece. In the solidiﬁcation process, the outermost par-
ticles of the cloud solidify ﬁrst and move inwards toward the centre of the cloud,
resulting in the making of a hollow sphere, addressed as an EDM-chip [11, p.155].
The rate of which the EDM-chips is removed from the spark gap must be higher
or of an equal magnitude of the generation. This will then ensure that there is a
consent number of EDM-chips in the spark gap and will contribute to a stable op-
eration of the servo system. The main way to remove the EDM-chipes is to ﬂush
them out whit a jet of dielectric ﬂuid, which is passed trough the spark gap. After
which the EDM chips have cleared from the spark gap, they are suspended in the
ﬂuid and transported to a collection and ﬁltration system. Here, the system ﬁlters
out the EDM-chips and other debris. This system also reprocesses the ﬂuid such that
is possess the acceptable dielectric qualities prior to reuse[11, p.155-156].
5.2.3
Parameters Effecting the Mechanism
In WEDM, there is several parameters which can be changed to alter the end result of
the cut. Main machining performances of the cut that is controlled by the parameters
is; Surface roughness, kerf width and Metal Removal Rate. Below are there two
bullet point lists containing the machining performances and parameters that is used
to obtain the desired cut, respectively. The lists is sourced from [16].
Machining Performances
• Surface Roughness, Ra [µm]
• Kerf width, kerf [µm]
• Metal Removal Rate, MMR [mm3/min]
Parameters
• Table Feed [mm/min]: Speed of work pieces relative to the wire(horizontal).
• Puls-on Time, Ton [µs]:Time electricity is applied to the work piece.
• Puls-off Time, Toff [µs]: Time no electricity is applied to the work piece.
• Wire Speed, Vw [m/min]: Speed of wire feeding past the work piece.
• Wire Tension, FW [gf]: Tension applied to the wire in gram-force.
• Flushing, P [kg/cm2]: Pressure applied from ﬂushing.
==PAGE:47==
5.2. Wire EDM
33
• EDM Wire, Dw [µm]: Diameter of wire.
The quality of the cut is essential to obtain good measurements for the Contour
method. The diameter and material of the wire has been proven to have a substantial
effect. According to [38, p.116], Brass is the preferred material, and have shown
better cutting performance then materials such as Tungstein and Zinc-coated brass.
The wire diameter is usually governed by the work-piece thickness, see table below.
TABLE 5.1: Table for determining wire diameter [38, p.116].
Specimen thickness
EDM wire diameter
< 15 mm
100 µm
10 mm-100 mm
150-200 µm
> 50 mm
250 µm
Most EDM machines have pre-deﬁned setting for cutting. These settings are
usually categorised as skim/ﬁnish cuts or rough cuts. The rough cut settings are op-
timized for MRR and as suggested by the name, result in a rough surface. In the
contour method, it is important to remove as little material as possible and strive
towards a low roughness. The skim/ﬁnish cut settings is therefore recommended.
The skim/ﬁnish setting is of a low power type, where MRR is not a important factor.
Thus, making the process of skim/ﬁnish cutting substantially more time consuming.
Conventional skim/ﬁnish cutting is used on a precut surface, where rough cut settings
have been used. Whereas in WEDM for the contour method, the ﬁrst and only cut
is preformed using skim/ﬁnish setting. Thus, add even more to the execution time of
the cut [38, p.116].
5.2.4
Limitations and Erroes
• Cutting irregularities (symmetric errors). These type of errors does not aver-
age away, and must be considered in the handling of the data. They are usually
caused by poor execution of the experiment and can therefor to a large extent
be avoided. Some of the most common symmetric errors from the cutting pro-
cesses are listed below:
– Local cutting irregularities. Examples of this might be wire breakage or
overburning at some foreign particle. These errors are in the magnitude
of the wire diameter and are corrected by the data smoothing process.
– Change in width of cut. If the material is heterogeneous in some areas
the ECM cut width can vary as the width is dependent on the material.
Changes in the thickness of the specimen can also lead to change in width
of cut.
– "Bowed" cuts and "ﬂared edges" are errors that stem from the chosen
setup. The "bowed" cut arise when vibrations are present in the wire. This
effect can be diminished by using low power and high injection settings.
The "ﬂared edge" on the other hand this can be controlled by adding a
material to the wire exit surface of the specimen. This errors are explained
in a grater depth in [10].
• Bulge error. This is a symmetric error that can cause signiﬁcant bias in the
contour method results if not dealt with [32]. The principle is illustrated and
explained in Figure 5.10. When the wire ECM cuts the specimen it is with a
==PAGE:48==
34
Chapter 5. Contour Method
constant width w in the laboratory reference frame. However, as the machine
moved forward the stresses are relaxed and the material at the very end of the
cut is deformed; At the time, the physical cut will still have the width w. In
summary, the width of the material that has been removed is less relative to
the original state of the body.
FIGURE 5.10: A typical round-bottomed EDM slot is illustrated to
show the principles of the bulge error. is used, but a ﬂat bottom is
illustrated for clarity [38]
.
• No assumptions of Isotropic or Homogeneous Elasticity Required There is
not required to assume that the material is elastically isotropic or homoge-
neous for this method. Nevertheless, the FM model used to calculate the stress
state in the body, must reﬂect the linear elastic behaviour accurately. For many
cases the material used in contour method, is assumed to be isotropic and ho-
mogeneous, which is for many materials a good assumption. However, it is
possible to introduce anisotropic elastic constants in the material deﬁnition,
where also, these constants can differ spatially throughout the body.
5.3
Coordinate Measuring Machine
5.3.1
Introduction
Coordinate Measuring Machines (CMMs) are well adapted and accepted in various
industries due to their ﬂexible measuring application to numerous shapes and their
ability to measurer spacial points at a very high accuracy. CMMs can be divided
into two main categorize; contact and non-contact. In both categories, it is easy to
apply automatic measuring for thousands of points, even on complex surfaces. The
conventional usage of CMM in industries are multi-folded, a bullet point list below
shows some of the roles CMM serves in companies [6, p.95] 1.
• Inspection of work-pieces (post- and in-process).
• Process-capability studies.
• Control of tools.
• Measurements during product development.
1This list is directly taken from [6, p.95]
==PAGE:49==
5.3. Coordinate Measuring Machine
35
• Measurement of models and prototypes.
• Calibration of gauges and master work-pieces.
In the forthcoming section, the tactile CMM with a moving bridge conﬁguration will
be focused on. This conﬁguration reﬂects the mechanism of the machine used in the
present work.
5.3.2
Tactile CMM
As the name tactile suggests, the CMM method depends on physical contact be-
tween the measuring probe and the work-piece to perform a measurement. There
is several conﬁguration used for the tactile CMM method. Some of the most com-
mon types is moving bridge, ﬁxed bridge, cantilever, horizontal arm and gantry.
The conﬁguration used in the present work is of the type moving bridge. This is
the best conﬁguration available at DTU campus and was selected based on recom-
mendations from technicians working in the ﬁeld of metrology. In Figure [6], an
illustration of the moving bridge conﬁguration is shown.
FIGURE 5.11:
A illustration of the conﬁguration of the moving
bridge[38].

below. The list must be seen in conjunction with Figure 5.112
1. Air bearing on granite guideway.
2. Grating scale for Y axis (measuring scale)
3. Probe conﬁguration.

==PAGE:50==

The moving bridge conﬁguration is the most widely used type in tactile CMM.
As seen in Figure 5.11, the granite plane or table top, where the work piece rests, is
stationary. The bridge move parallel to two of the sides across the table top and the
measuring head travels along the bridge in a perpendicular direction, relative to the
bridges travelling direction. Thus, is a Cartesian coordinate system generated and
is the most common coordinate system used in tactile CMM. The heavy and thick
granite plate used as the table top is very stable, this ensure a high load capacity and
generates low bending effect in the horizontal plane. As there is to columns moving
at the same time, the pace of each of them will never be perfectly the same. This
difference in pace will generate twisting motion in the bridge, which can result in
a small error. Lastly, accuracy of the measurements depend on the location of the
work piece on the granite plate[6, p.95].

The mechanical probing system in twofold; touch-trigger systems (dynamic probing
system) and three-dimensional measuring probe system (static probing system). In

5.4
Smoothing the Data- Spline
5.4.1
Introduction
Previous studies[33] have shown that to be able to utilize the resolution of the data
obtain trough CMM, a new improved method must be used to smooth out the data.
Earlier, a Fourier series was used, this method was not adequate to capture the fea-
tures of the cut-surface. The new method, least-squares approximation using bivari-
ate Basic splines (B-form splines), have two main qualities which the old method
do not possess; an objective way to select the degree of smoothing and estimate the
uncertainty of the stress map [33, p.178].
In the upcoming sections, the construction and deﬁnitions of B-form splines and
Last-squares approximation will be shortly elaborated. Furthermore will the com-
mands used in Matlab be brief described.
5.4.2
Spline B-form
The main reason for smoothing or ﬁtting the data to a surface is to discard noise
in the raw data. If there where to be used noisy data in the stress calculations, the
noise-error would augment and difﬁculties would arise when analysing the stress
results. As mentioned in earlier studies, where the contour method was utilized, a
high-order bivariate Fourier series was taken advantage of to smooth out the data.
Nevertheless, it is been reported difﬁculties of ﬁtting the data adequately to a single
Fourier expression [33, p.179]. Therefore it has been concluded that utilizing the B-
form splines, or also known as smoothly joined polynomials, is a better tool for the
present task.
B-form of Tensor Product Splines
The tensor product concept is deﬁned according to[26, p.396] as followed; "If f is a
function of x, and g is a function of y, then their tensor-product p(x, y) = f(x)g(y) is a
function of x and y, e.i., a bivariate function. More generally, with s = (s1, ...sm+n) and
t = (t1, ..., tn+k) knot sequences and aji : i = (1, ..., m) : j = (1, ..., n)] a corresponding
coefﬁcient array , you obtain a bivariate spline as"
f(x, y) =
m
X
i=1
n
X
j=1
B(x|si, ...si+h)B(y|tj, ...tj+k)aij
(5.4)
"The B-form of this spline comprises the cell arrey {s,t} of its knot sequences, coefﬁcient
array a, the numbers vector [m,n], and the orders vector [h,k]".
B-form and B-Splines
When looking at spline in B-form, it is build up from several B-splines. A simple
description of this is that the B-splines are the building blocks used to construct a
B-form spline. The B-spline is a piece of a polynomial which ﬁt together with a
==PAGE:52==

Chapter 5. Contour Method
predetermined knot sequence. In Figure 5.14, this is illustrated. In this illustration
the spline order is four, therefore is the polynomials used in the third power.

5.4.3
Matlab
When utilizing splines, the matrix software MATLAB is used for the calculations. In
this software a tool called Curve Fitting ToolboxTM have shown to be very helpful.
This toolbox can be used through a visual window or by availing the programmed
commands. The latter is applied, as this gave a larger control over the process. In the
previous studies [33], the least-squares approximation using bivariate Basic splines
(B-form splines) is used and it is intended that same method is to be applied in the
present work. The command which governs this method is shown below.
sp = spap2({knotsy, knotsx}, [ky
kx], {Y, X}, Z);
Here sp and ap2 indicates that the command construct splines in B-form and a least-
squares approximation, respectively [26, p.351]. knotsy and knotsx represent the
knot sequences, ky and kx are the chosen degree in the respective directions. Lastly,
the Y ,X and Z is the data which is to be approximated. When generating the knot
sequences the commands below is used.
knotsx = augknt(knots, kx)andknotsy = augknt(knots, ky);
As seen above this command is governed by the arrays describing the knot place-
ment, knots, and the chosen degree in the respective directions, ky and kx. This
command generate knot sequences in the order which is needed in the spap2 com-
mand. As the spline is now generated, the remaining operation is to extract the new
data from a desired grid in the x an y direction. Below the comand used to extract
the new "z" values is shown.
vals = fnval(sp, {Y Y, XX});
The command fnval uses the spline generated form spap2 and the new uniform
grid which is named XX and Y Y to extract the approximated "ZZ", which here is
named vals. A simple way to visualize this is to use the command below.
figure(1)
mesh(XX, Y Y, vals)
The whole script use can be viewed in Appendix N.
==PAGE:53==
