Chapter 6
Numerical modeling
One of the main objectives in this thesis is to develop a FE-model which can rep-
resented the physical phenomena that takes place during and post welding. To be
more speciﬁc, the desired outcome is to have a FE-model which have the capability
of performing calculations which result in an accurate approximation of the residual
stresses in the welded piece. In this chapter, the concept of numerical modelling is
elaborated. Here the overall mindset of numerical modelling is addresses, together
with the decoupling of the complex problem and accuracy of the resulting residen-
tial stresses.
6.1
Numerical Modelling Concept
This section contains an overview of what numerical modelling entails with respect
to computational welding mechanics(CWM).
Numerical models are a widely spread concept which is used in numerous dis-
ciplines, e.g. chemistry, climatology, astrophysics, biology and engineering, etc. In
the context of CWM, a model is deﬁned as a ﬁnite element model(FEM). This FEM
consist of equation and algorithms which are suited to describe complex problems,
which do not have an simple analytical solution. The problem is represented using
discretization, where continuous functions and equation are transferred into dis-
crete counterparts in time and space. It is important to mention that, when using the
above mentioned method, there will always arise discretization errors.
In the process of constructing a numerical model there are some essential proce-
dure steps which must be carried out to ensure a valid or representative model. A
schematic illustration of the process is shown in ﬁgure 6.1.
Some of the various terms in ﬁgure 6.1, do not express an evident meaning at
ﬁrst glance, therefore, a brief characterization of the terms are presented below.
• Qualiﬁcation is the process where the conceptual model is assured to be correct
with respect to reality.
• Veriﬁcation is the process where the FEM is conﬁrmed to be true with respect
to the conceptual model.
• Validation is the process where the certainty of the model is evaluated by com-
paring with experimental results.
When the model have successfully gone trough the process of Qualiﬁcation, Ver-
iﬁcation and Validation, the model can be categorized as valid for use as a tool to
predict similar cases, but not identical, to the validated case. Most often, the model
==PAGE:54==
40
Chapter 6. Numerical modeling
FIGURE 6.1: Schematic presentation of the process needed to gener-
ating a representative numerical model/simulation. The illustration
is based on ﬁgured 1. in [17].
is in need of calibration, in terms of the parameters used, to match some measure-
ments. The measurements used in the veriﬁcation can not be used in the calibration,
this is a requirement enforced so that dependency is preserved. The process just de-
scribed, are in most cases an extensive procedure, due to the fact that the material
model and the heat input must both go through calibration and veriﬁcation, [17]
[39].
6.2
The Coupled Problem
In the opening of this section a brief description of what a coupling problem entails is
presented. Further on, is the scope of the coupling problem in relation to the present
work addressed. This section is based on literature from Markert [21] and Radaj [35].
The essence of engineering and science is based upon using and studying certain
systems (chemical, biological,mechanical, etc,) and subjecting excitation or changes
to the external conditions to seek a response trough the system. This in it self, for-
mulates the problems which is of interest in this project. In such systems, there
can be many different physical phenomenons that are associated with numerous
components, which fundamentally governs the system response. This physical phe-
nomenons can possibly have mutual interaction, behave non-linear and develop on
different time scales and spatial domains. When systems reaches complexity of this
degree, the components of the system must be described by applicable theoretical
models, which capture the deep-seated coupling mechanisms between the depen-
dent ﬁelds[21]. The following deﬁnitions of different types of coupling problems are
proposed in the referenced literature1.
• Coupled problem: A coupled problem is one in which physically or computa-
tionally heterogeneous components interact dynamically. The interaction is
1The deﬁnitions presented in the bullet point list is directly taken from [21, p.4].
==PAGE:55==
6.2. The Coupled Problem
41
multi-way in the sense that the solution has to be obtained by a simultaneous
analysis of the coupled equations which model the problem.
• Coupled multi-ﬁeld problem: A coupled multi-ﬁeld problem is one in which the
individual ﬁeld equations are functions also of the other ﬁeld variables govern-
ing the problem which requires the concurrent treatment of multiple depen-
dent ﬁelds in a model. Every coupled problem is per se a coupled multi-ﬁeld
problem.
• Coupled multi-physics problem: A coupled multi-physics problem is a coupled
problem in which multiple physical models or phenomena are handled simul-
taneously. That is, if (1) different discretization techniques are used for indi-
vidual subproblems on different (possibly coinciding) spatial domains or (2)
individual ﬁeld variables represent distinctly different but mutually interact-
ing physical phenomena. A coupled multi-ﬁeld problem is not per se a cou-
pled multi-physics problem.
When designing and manufacturing structures which includes welding, it is im-
portant to acquire control over the residual stresses and welding distortions which
arise in the work piece. These aspects, can to a large extent, impair the material
strength and complicate the manufacturing process. Therefore, it is important to
take measures for reducing the residual stresses and distortions or to relax the ma-
terial after welding. Some consequences that can emerge when these issues are not
addressed can include; manufacturing tolerance requirements for the shape and di-
mensions may not be met and the increase in grove gap and misalignment of the
joint can cause welding complications. Residual stresses, when released upon ma-
chining, will result in unknown distortion of the material, which in most cases is
unacceptable. This types of stresses is often a factor in the arise of brittle fractures in
the ﬁnished structure. When looking at fatigue strength and corrosion resistance, it
is known that tensile residual stresses are a main reason in the reduction of these as-
pects. Although, tensile residual stresses are unfavourable for fatigue strength and
corrosion resistance, it is favourable in respect to stability. Whereas, compressive
residual stresses is inversely favourable for fatigue strength and corrosion resistance
and unfavourable in respect to stability. Even though some positive outcomes can
come form residual stresses, as mentioned above, these outcomes are of secondary
practical signiﬁcances.
The above mentioned events result in the structuring of the material as following
listed principal sections.
• Welding temperature ﬁeld analysis.
• Welding residual stress and distortion analysis.
• Reduction of welding residual stress and distortion,
• Effects of welding on strength
Through the history of analyses of welding residual stresses and welding distor-
tion, each of the areas have, to a large extent, developed separately. Even though, the
two are closely linked in the physical aspect. This may stem from the fact that resid-
ual stresses are essentially only used for a basis in the material strength analyses and
welding distortion are predominantly a phenomena which impair the manufacturer.
In addition to these branch differentness there is a signiﬁcant differences in the me-
thodical approach for these analyses. Generally, it is necessary to establish a ﬁnite
==PAGE:56==
42
Chapter 6. Numerical modeling
element model for the structure to obtain the welding residual stresses, in contrast
to the assessment of welding distortion where most often it is adequate to utilize
common engineering theories to establish the desired result.
When looking at the multitude of inﬂuencing parameters and their non-linear,
transient and temperature-dependent effect, it is apparent that this blocks the chance
for valid statements on welding deformations and welding residual stresses for in-
dividual cases to be made, and render generalizations controversial. A person with
a practical approach, use a standardized deﬁnition of terms relating to weldabil-
ity as a classiﬁcation system, which, to an extent have some value when examin-
ing the effects of welding residual stresses and welding deformations. Even if this
is a more linguistic than scientiﬁc approach. Nevertheless, the scientiﬁc analysis
have rendered an approach which reduces the complexity of the situation by using
decoupling of the thermodynamic, mechanical and microstructural processes. The
schematics of the decoupling can be viewed in Figure 6.2.
FIGURE 6.2: Schematic presentation of decoupling approach and mu-
tual inﬂuencing of temperature ﬁeld, stress and deformation ﬁeld and
microstructural state ﬁeld in numerical modelling [35, p.5].
The arrows represent the mutual inﬂuences present; the direction of the arrow
dictates the sequential inﬂuence between the ﬁelds. The visual state of the arrows
dictates the degree of the inﬂuence. A solid arrow express a strong inﬂuence and a
dotted arrow inversely indicate a weak inﬂuence. These weak inﬂuences are often
negligible, in respect to an engineering perspective.
6.3
Accuracy classiﬁcation
Establishing an accuracy level needed for a numerical model, gives the opportunity
of modelling as simple as possible while upholding the credibility of the model.
==PAGE:57==
6.3. Accuracy classiﬁcation
43
The accuracy of a numerical model can be divided into classes describing the
complexity of the different aspects of the model. In the following, the heat input
model, the material model, the geometry and the discretization will be discussed in
therms of accuracy.
The classes presented by Lindgren [18] are presented in Table 6.1, where the ﬂex-
ibility of the model describes the level of inﬂuence each bead has on the deforma-
tions. This way a single bead on a thin plate will be quite ﬂexible, while several
beads on a thick plate will be rather rigid.

6.3.1
Heat input model
There are several ways of modelling the heat input in a numerical model. Jessen, did
a review of different models in 2016, where he tested Ellipsoid heat source, Goladak
double ellipsoid heat source, laser heat source and Prescribed temperature. The ﬁrst
three was modelled with subroutines in Abaqus as a moving heat source and re-
quire complex elements. The last approach, Prescribed temperature is the build-in
feature in Abaqus Welding Interface (AWI) and models the heat as discrete chunks
laid down with a prescribed temperature. The latter is a far more simple modelling
option, and is recommended for the lowest accuracy categories, as can be seen in
Table 6.2 below.
TABLE 6.2: Accuracy class with corresponding heat input model
adopted from [18].
Basic and standard simulation
Constant ﬂux in weld pool region
Accurate and very accurate simulation
Double ellipsoid heat source model
From the results found by Jessen [39, p. 142], one can also see that there is small
differences between the stress distributions for the 3D models with the various heat
input approaches.
From this it can be seen that a prescribed temperature is supported by the accu-
racy level of basic/standard.
==PAGE:58==
44
Chapter 6. Numerical modeling
6.3.2
Material model
Later on in Section 7.3, the thermal and mechanical properties of steel are reviewed.
Thermal parameters together with the Young’s modulus, thermal expansion and
yield limit being the most important ones in relation to the accuracy level. In addi-
tion, there are three aspects that also need to be considered according to Lindgren
[18]:
• Cut off temperature, Tcut.
• Effects of phase change/Change in micro structure of material.
• Rate-dependent plastic behaviour at elevated temperatures.
The cut-off temperature indicates at which point the changes in the material
properties are no longer included in the mechanical analysis. Up to this point, the
thermoelastoplastic properties need to be well documented for all accuracy classes.
This means that one can assume all properties to be constant above the cut-off tem-
perature, which can be convenient as these are challenging to experimentally obtain.
The phase change effects are here related to the transformation in the microstruc-
ture of the material that arise during heating and cooling.
The rate-independent plasticity is the permanent strain that manifest once a ma-
terial is stressed beyond a the yield stress. The stains are assumed to form instan-
taneously, thereby being independent of time. While the dependent includes the
effects of time, creep. This is further discussed in the plasticity section in Chapter 7.
In the Table 6.3, the requirements for each accuracy class is presented.
TABLE 6.3: Summary of accuracy classes depending on the thee bul-
letpoints above, adpoted from [1] which is based on the works of

In the table one can see that class 1 only requires the latent heats to be included
and the cut-off temperature can be as low as half of the melting temperature. When
reviewing residual stress one can see from the temperature dependent elastic mod-
ulus plotted in Section 7.3, that the steel mainly gains stiffness when T < 0.5Tmelt.
With stiffness being a key parameter for the growth of residual stresses, one can see
that Class 1 can be sufﬁcient when residual stresses are in focus. There have also
been studies where the effects of Tcut has been investigated. It was found that Tcut =
Tmelt gave an overestimation of 2% of the residual stresses and Tcut = 0.42Tmelt re-
sulted in an overestimation of 15% [45].
Class 2-3 requires that all three phase change effects are included in the model,
in addition to increasing the cut-off temperature to 0.7 Tmelt. In class 4 the creep is
also considered, in addition to the cut-off temperature being equal to the melting
temperature.
==PAGE:59==
6.3. Accuracy classiﬁcation
45
In a welding simulation, the time associated with T > 0.5 Tmelt, is quite little and
the rate-dependent effects can therefore be excluded. For steel the melting temper-
ature is about 1500◦C, yielding a cut-off temperature of about 750◦C for accuracy
category 1. This means that material properties up to this point must be modelled.
6.3.3
Geometry and discretization
Lindgren [18] states that there is no simple relation between accuracy classiﬁcation
and ﬁnite element formulation, but a clear distinction can be made between 2D mod-
els with plain stain and 3D models. In welding, the heat conduction is of great im-
portance, and it should be included with a through thickness propagation in a 3D
model. 2D models require far less computational power, and can give sufﬁcient re-
sults for out of plane deformations.
In the present work, both out-of-plane and in-plane deformations are of interest,
therefore a 3D model will be used.

Chapter 7
Thermo-Mechanical Analysis
The aspects of the thermo-mechanical analysis is in this chapter addressed, together
with the theoretical models which is used in Abaqus. All the thermal phenomena
which occur is identify and the ones which is of importance for the analyse is elab-
orated. Further, the thermal and mechanical parameters used in the analysis i elu-
cidated and the manner which this parameters is implemented in the FM-model is
shown.
The numerous thermal properties which the material possess is discussed, to-
gether whit how the
7.1
Thermal Analysis
The fundamental principle of thermal analysis concerns conservation of heat [8]. A
wider interpretation of the heat transfer analysis is presented in Figure 7.1. However,
as explained in the previous chapter, a sequential thermo-mechanical approach is
chosen an thereby several of the phenomena in Figure 7.1 are neglected.
FIGURE 7.1: Aspects affecting the modeling of heat transfer [8]
Fluid mechanics, electromagnetism and micro-structure formations are not a part
of the chosen approach and are neglected. The latent heat concerns the phase of the
material and does not affect the temperature of the body. In that sense, the latent
heat is not of interest for the chosen approach. This leaves the speciﬁc heat capacity,
conductivity, convection and radiation. The principles of each are described in the
following section with their governing equations.
==PAGE:62==
48
Chapter 7. Thermo-Mechanical Analysis
7.1.1
Heat Propagation
This section and all its subsections are based on the works of D. Radaj.
Output of welding heat source
The main parameter of the welding heat source regarding the temperature ﬁeld is the
heat input. For a stationary heat source applied for a short period of time the input
is deﬁned as the heat energy, Q [J] and a continuously acting source it is deﬁned as
the heat ﬂow, q [J/s].
The total amount of heat ﬂow from an arc is calculated as the amperage, I times
the voltage, U when direct current is applied. The heat output can thereby be de-
scribed by Equation (7.1):
q = ηhUI = ηhRI2
eff
(7.1)
Where, ηn represents the heat losses from welding, including the heat dissipation
to the surrounding areas (from convection and radiation), spray losses and losses re-
lated to heating of the electrode for non-melting electrodes. This way η is a measure
of the arc efﬁciency, and for submerged arc welding its value is recommended to be in
the range 0.91 −0.99 [15]. In the present work, an arc efﬁciency of η = 1 is assumed,
due to simplicity and lack of information about the welding.
When alternating current is applied, the formula includes the resistance, R =
U/I, and the effective ampere,Ieff. This is shown in the last term in Equation (7.1).
Another, and occasionally more advantageous way of presenting the heat ﬂow
is by transforming it to a measure per unit length of weld, qw [J/mm] rather than
by per unit of time, q [J/s]. The heat output q is easily converted by diving it by the
welding speed, v.
qw = q
v
(7.2)
A high degree of uncertainty regarding the welding temperature ﬁeld give rise
to uncertainties with respect to the above discussed values Q and q. This means that
individual cases needs both experimental work and theoretical analysis to determine
these values with higher certainty.
Heat conduction
To describe the relation between momentary local heat ﬂow and momentary tem-
perature ﬁeld Fourier’s law (Equation 7.3 ) of heat conduction applied.
q∗= −λ∂T
∂n
(7.3)
In the equation above, the heat output is denoted by ∗, referring to the area spe-
ciﬁc heat output density [J/mm2 s], also called the heat ﬂux density. The equations
shows that the heat ﬂux density is the product of the coefﬁcient of thermal conduc-
tivity, −λ [J/s · mm · K], and the negative temperature gradient, ∂T
∂n , perpendicular
to the area. The thermal conductivity depends on several material parameters, but
mostly chemical composition, micro-structural state and temperature. [35].
Now, including the local temperature change on the temperature ﬁeld, the ﬁeld
equation of heat conduction is found,equation (7.4).
==PAGE:63==
7.1. Thermal Analysis
49
∂T

(7.4)
Where, Qv [J/mm3], is the heat energy released or used per unit of volume and
the fraction right before the parentheses,
λ
cpρ is the thermal diffusivity, denoted by a.
[35]
To solve equation 7.4 one needs to establish the initial boundary conditions,
which can be interpreted as the temperatures at t = 0. One also need to set the
boundary conditions for t > 0, which means the heat dissipation from the boundary
surfaces. The dissipation can be described by two phenomenons, convection and
radiation.
Convection
Convection is related to the movement of particles mainly in ﬂuids or gases, and
one distinguishes between natural and forced convection of heat transfer. The for-
mer occurs when movement is solely caused by differences in density as a result
of temperature differences. The latter is a result of external forces maintaining the
movement. In SAW the forced convection would be a result of the blow effect of the
arc.
The mathematical relationship between the heat ﬂow and heat transfer by con-
vection is expressed in Eq. 7.5
q∗
c = αc(T −T0)
(7.5)
Where, q∗
c, denotes the area speciﬁc heat output [J/mm2s] and the coefﬁcient
of convection heat transfer is, αc [J/mm2s◦C]. This coefﬁcient depends on the ﬂow
conditions on the surface of the body, its surface properties and its properties of the
ﬂowing medium. Using Newton’s law the ﬂow output is to be proportional to the
temperature difference between the surface temperature, T, and the gas or liquid
temperature, T0, with the dependence of the coefﬁcient of convection heat transfer.
Radiation
Heat radiates as electromagnetic waves in space. The emissivity, ε, is a measure of
the surface efﬁciency of emitting energy as thermal radiation. The emissivity can
range from 0 to 1, where 0 would represent a surface material that is the perfect
reﬂector, and 1 an absolute black body.
According to Stefan-Boltzman’s law the heat output density related to radiation,
q∗
r [J/mm2 s], is proportional to the surface temperature to the power of four, and
its magnitude govern by the Stefan-Boltzman coefﬁcient, σ, and the emissivity, ε, as
shown in Eq. 7.6.
q∗
r = εσ(T 4 −T 4
0 )
(7.6)
Where, T, represents the body temperature relative to absolute zero of the tem-
perature scale and the temperature, T0, of the surroundings relative to absolute zero.
A perfect black body has a Stefan-Boltzman coefﬁcient equal to 5.67·10−8W/m2/K4
and the emissivity can thereby be interpreted as the degree of blackness of a surface.
==PAGE:64==
50
Chapter 7. Thermo-Mechanical Analysis
7.1.2
Thermal Boundary Conditions
The initial boundary conditions is in this case is the initial temperature of the body
before welding. This can either the room temperature, constant over the whole body,
or a elevated temperature caused by preheating of the body. If one expects high tem-
peratures to be applied, preheating is beneﬁcial to decrease the temperature gradi-
ent.
There are three types of boundary conditions that need to be considered:
• Prescribed temperature
• Prescribed heat ﬂow density
• Heat transfer to the surroundings
The prescribed temperature can be isothermal, meaning a constant temperature
across the entire boundary surface or have a more complex distribution. The pre-
scribed heat ﬂow density may, in its simplest case be adiabatic, meaning a loss of
heat ﬂow. Finally, there is the heat transfer to the surroundings, which is described
by the convection and radiation (see Equation 7.5 and 7.6).
7.1.3
Finite Element Formulation
The equations presented previously in this chapter can only be implemented ana-
lytically for simple geometries, with correspondingly simple boundary conditions.
Another restriction is that the material properties must be temperature independent.
Neither is the case when working with welding of steel and therefor a numerical ap-
proach using the ﬁnite element method (FEM) is introduced. In this thesis, Abaqus
is the chosen FEM program, and thereby the ﬁnite element formulations stem from
their choice of approach presented in The Abaqus Theory Guide [41].
The governing equation for the temperatures is the one describing the conduc-
tivity, presented in Equation 7.4. This equation, can also be described by means of
tensor notation
ρcp ˙T = (λT,j),j + Qv
(7.7)
where T is the temperature, ρ, is the density, cp, is the speciﬁc heat, λ, is the heat
conductivity and, Qv, is the heat supplied per unit volume.
When formulating a ﬁnite element equation the tensor representation gives a
cleaner formulation and is therefore used in th following. By multiplying the basic
energy balance with a virtual temperature weighting function, ¯T, and then integrate
over the entire volume, the general ﬁnite element formulation of heat transfer is
found:

One can separate the time and space variable so that the individual temperatures
of the elements can be interpolated. Generating a vector that stores the temperature
development at each node, T, and a matrix that holds the interpolation functions
(also called shape functions), N.

It should be noted that Abaqus treats the analysis as uncoupled in the sense that
the rate of internal energy, U, only depend on the temperature and, q, and, r, does
not depend on strains or displacement of the body [41].
7.2
Mechanical Analysis
In Section 6.2, the concept of the sequentially coupled thermo-mechanical analysis
is discussed. In this chapter the concept is applied. The nodal temperature history
found from the thermal analysis is applied as a thermal load in the mechanical anal-
ysis. In the following the governing equations and ﬁnite element formulations are
presented, as the means for calculating the stress and strain.
Newtons second law presents the basis for the mechanical analysis with the par-
tial differential equations for force equilibrium. In Equation (7.10) the expressions
for the static case is presented for a rectangular Cartesian coordinate system.

can be expressed in the weak form [41]:

For solving the force equilibrium equation (7.12) an assumption of small dis-

transformation plasticity, tp, viscoplasticity, vp, creep c or temperature ,th. There are
more sources of stain, but not all need be included in the calculations, depending on
the purpose of the work. In this case, the end result is to ﬁnd residual stresses from

welding and on that account it will sufﬁce to include the elastic, plastic and thermal

ij
(7.13)
During the welding, the plastic deformations caused by the thermal cycles cause
the growth of residual stresses. However, when using Hooke’s law to relate the
stresses to the strains one assumes that its the elastic part of the strains that cause
stresses. The same assumption is applied in experimental works as neutron diffrac-
tion, hole drilling and contour method, where one measure the elastic strain and
convert them to stresses [15].
7.2.1
Mechanical Boundary Conditions
The mechanical boundary conditions involves the degree of constraint that is sus-
tained during the loading period. In the case of welding, the load is the heat sup-
plied from the arc. If an object is subjected to a uniform temperature increase and
decrease, while being totally unrestrained from movement, no residual stresses will
develop in the object. This is of course not the case with welding, as the heat is
locally applied. The amount of stresses that built up due to the temperature gradi-
ent, heavily rely on the amount of physical restrains that are applied to the welding
object.
When cooling, the material will want to contract. With no constraints, the mate-
rial will succumb and deform. If the object is constrained against deformation the
tension from the contraction will rise substantially and at a point start cracking if the
residual stresses succeeds the yield stress.
For this reason, the mechanical boundary conditions are extremely important
when investigating residual stresses.
7.2.2
Finite Element Formulation
To formulate a discrete representation of the equilibrium equation the principles of
virtual work is again applied. By applying point-wise interpolation of the displace-
ments and using the shape function of an element, one can determine the nodal
displacements. The connection between strain and displacement is given in matrix
form in Equation (7.14), where, B, represents the fundamental strain-displacement
matrix and, u, the displacements.
εtotal = B · u
(7.14)
The strain-displacement matrix can be interpreted in the same way as the temperature-
gradient interpolation matrix (N) in the thermal analysis. The stresses relate to the
strains in terms of the material behavior of the body. In matrix format, this can be
presented as:
σ = C · εtotal
(7.15)
Where, C, is the constitutive matrix, e.g described by Hooke’s law for thermo-
elastic material [15]. This matrix is general, not restricted to follow Hooke’s law, it
can also be used for anisotropic materials.
The two equations described above is used to form the equation of virtual work.
The general ﬁnite element expression for the system is:
==PAGE:67==
7.3. Material Modelling
53
K · u = Rb + Rs + Rc
(7.16)
where, K, represent the stiffness of the system, Rb, the body force over the vol-
umes, Rs, the element surface force and, Rc, the concentrated nodal force.
7.3
Material Modelling
In the following chapter the material properties needed for welding simulations are
discussed.
A study conducted by Zhu and Chao [49] shows that there are great variation in
the importance of temperature dependent properties for welding simulations.
Regarding the thermal properties, they conclude that using room temperature
values for all the thermal properties can be sufﬁcient for a welding simulation, with a
larger uncertainty for the thermal conductivity and negligible effects of temperature
dependent material density and speciﬁc heat.
Regarding the mechanical properties they have different opinion. As the yield
stress is the main parameter in welding calculation, its value can have great affect
on the ﬁnal stress distribution and distortion. Also, they point out that using room
temperature values for the yield stress, means that there will not be any residual
stresses as only the elastic strain would manifest under these conditions. Meaning
that the yield stress must be modelled as a temperature dependent value. However,
they conclude that from an engineering perspective a "piece-wise linear function"
is sufﬁcient where material properties for elevated temperatures are not available.
The other mechanical properties, such as Young’s modulus and thermal expansion,
are found to have little effect on neither residual stresses or distortion, using the
value corresponding to room temperature is advised. Nevertheless, previous work,
[39] and [1], Sommerlund and Ammitzbøl) have included temperature dependent
values for all parameter, and this will be adopted to the present work.
All the graphs that are plotted in this section can also be seen in the input ﬁles
used for Abaqus in Appendix R.
7.3.1
Thermal properties
In this section the thermal properties that are to be used in the thermal analysis is
discussed. There are three parameters that will be considered:

Speciﬁc heat capacity
If studying the units, one can see that speciﬁc heat capacity is the energy needed
to change a unit mass by one degree. The graph presented below is applicable for
mild- and low-alloy steels.
FIGURE 7.2: Speciﬁc heat capacity as a function of temperature.
The peak that is seen just before 800 ◦C is caused by the latent heat, where more
energy is needed to change the temperature due to the phase change from solid to
liquid associated with the melting of the steel.
Density
The role of density in a thermal analysis is connected to specifying the heat storage
of the material.
A common assumption regarding the temperature dependency of the density is
that it linearly declines until the melting point of the steel [15]. This is applied in the
present case and can be seen in Figure 7.3.
FIGURE 7.3: Density as a function of temperature.
One can think that density is more of a mechanical measure, however, it is rarely
applied in the mechanical analysis, unless gravity is a governing factor of the calcu-
lations.
Thermal conductivity
The thermal conductivity is discussed earlier in the chapter, in short it is about the
materials ability to transfer heat by molecular collisions. Figure 7.4 illustrates the
values used in the present work for temperature dependent thermal conductivity.
When the steel reaches the liquid state4, the conductivity changes drastically. As
mentioned the conductivity depends on the molecular collisions, in a liquid stat this
happens more rapidly than in a solid.

7.3.2
Mechanical Properties
The residual stresses from welding mainly depend on the thermal history of the
mechanical properties and the thermal history of the specimen. As such, the tem-
perature dependency of the listed properties are considered, but also the annealing
temperature and cyclic loading should be considered.
In the present work, the steel s355ML is used in the experiments and is therefore
the only steel type considered here.
• Young’s modulus, E [MPa]
• Yield stress, σy [MPa]
• Thermal expansion, α [1/◦C]
• Poisson’s ratio ν
• Plasticity
Young’s Modulus
The values obtained for the Young’s modulus is based on the work by Jessen [39,
p. 202], where he conducted a series of tests on heated material. The experimental
values where compared to recommendations by the Eurocode DN-EN 1993-1-2, and
showed good correlation.
FIGURE 7.5: Young’s modulus as a function of temperature for steel
S355ML as found by Jessen [39].
Yield Strength
The yield strength represents the stress level at which the material goes from be-
having elastic to plastic. Jessen’s experimental work, Figure 7.6, found the values to
==PAGE:70==
56
Chapter 7. Thermo-Mechanical Analysis
be 408 MPa at 20◦C and almost linearly decreasing towards zero, approaching the
liquid state at about 1500 ◦C.
FIGURE 7.6:
Yield stress as a function of temperature for steel
s355ML, as found by Jessen [39].
Thermal expansion
Here the expansion related to the thermal inﬂuence is presented. The change seen
in Figure 7.7 is connected to the phase transformation. From the solid state at 20◦C
the material expands as its heated, and at about 750◦C the material starts contract-
ing. At this point the atom structure of the material changes and causes a change in
response. The second shift, where the material starts expanding again, is due to the
phase transformation from solid to liquid.
FIGURE 7.7: Thermal expansion coefﬁcient as a function of tempera-
ture, adapted from the works of Langekjær [15].
Poisson’s Ratio
The Poisson’s ratio is the relationship between the axial and the transverse strain.
Its found by dividing the transverse expansion by the amount of axial compression.
Common values for steel are in the intervall 0.28-0.33 at room temperature. In Figure
7.8 one can see that the ratio stagnates at just below 0.5, as this represents the state
of an incompressible ﬂuid.
7.3.3
Modelling plasticity
When modelling a welding process it is imperative to consider the correct material
characteristics. According to Sluzalec [42] there are three main aspects of plasticity
that need to be accounted for; Plasticity criterion, hardening effects and plastic ﬂow
rule.
==PAGE:71==
7.3. Material Modelling
57
FIGURE 7.8: Poison’s ratio as function of temperature, adopted from
the works of Jessen [39].
Plasticity criterion
The stress level that is needed to induce permanent deformations is called the yield
stress (fy). The yield stress can be found by the use of Von Mises yield criteria, and
can be presented as a surface when considering three directions as shown in Figure
7.9.
FIGURE 7.9: A 3D presentation of the Von Mises yield surface [36].
Hardening
Strain hardening is the concept of material gaining resistance against deformation
when it is loaded beyond the yield limit and then unloaded again. There are sev-
eral methods of modeling this increase, two of them being isotropic and kinematic
hardening.
Isotropic hardening means that the initial yield curve (seen in 3D in Figure 7.9
and 2D to the left in Figure 7.10) expands, however without changing neither centre,
shape or orientation. When modelling isotropic, one neglects most effects from cyclic
loading. Due to the ease of implementing this modelling approach compared to
other, it is widely used in (CWM).
==PAGE:72==
58
Chapter 7. Thermo-Mechanical Analysis
FIGURE 7.10: Yield surface for isotropic hardening (left) and kine-
matic hardening (right) [15].
Another way of modelling the strain hardening is the kinematic approach. With
kinematic hardening one assumes that the yield curve sustains its size, shape and ori-
entation, but changes centre point, as seen to the right in Figure 7.10. This approach
takes into account the effects of cyclic loading, which the isotropic model dose not.
Plastic ﬂow rule
The plasticity criterion described when the material would yield, whereas, the plas-
tic ﬂow rule describes the magnitude of the deformation. To determine the plastic
strain one applies small deformation theory as explained in Section 7.2.
Plasticity for s355ML
When modelling plasticity there are several aspects that need to be considered in
addition to the hardening. First of all, one distinguishes between monotonic and
cyclic plasticity. During the welding of a multi-pass weld the material will experi-
ence heating and subsequently cooling several times. Meaning that cyclic plasticity
is the most correct model in the present work.
Secondly, one often distinguishes between rate-dependent and rate-independent
material behaviour. Goldak and Akhlaghi[8] suggests that an approach where one
applies rate-independent behaviour for T < 0.5Tm, rate-dependent for 0.5Tm < T <
0.8Tm and linear viscosity for T > 0.8Tm. However, in the current work it is residual
stresses that are of concern and these stresses develop during the cooling. It can
be seen in Figure 7.5 that the steel looses its stiffness for temperatures over 550◦C.
Stiffness being imperative to the development of stresses, it is chosen to model rate-
independent for all temperatures.
Regarding the hardening, extensive research of modelling approaches have been
carried out in relation to residual stresses. Jessen [39, p. 61] found that there was
an agreement in the literature regarding the use of models. A mixed model (be-
tween isotropic and kinematic) is the most accurate model, the isotropic model tends
to overestimate the residual stresses and the kinematic underestimate the residual
stresses.
Jessen [39] also fund that O. Muransky et Al [27] recommended to use a isotropic
model, with material data derived from monotonic test with a single annealing point
as an approximation. This approach is assumed to give moderately conservative
predictions. In addition, Jessen [39] expresses some concerns about his results from
the cyclic tests. With this is mind, the recommendations by O. Muransky et al are
applied in the present work, with the monotonic material data derived by Jessen
[39].
==PAGE:73==
7.3. Material Modelling
59

Chapter 8
Numerical approach
In the process of creating FM-model, it is essential to have a overview of the possi-
bilities and choices that must be taken in respect to the model. The general Abaqus
modelling choices in relation to element selection and mesh density are ﬁrst dis-
cussed. Next, the heat modelling approach built into Abaqus Welding Interface
(AWI) is reviewed step by step. Finally, the mechanical modelling is considered,
which includes the accuracy level needed for the present case and the modelling of
the welding bead.
8.1
Model Choices
8.1.1
Element Selection
In the present work, only 3D elements are considered as only 3-dimensional mod-
els are used for the numerical modelling. In Figure 8.1, different 3D elements are
shown, with the distinction between linear and quadric elements, where the upper
row represents the linear elements and the bottom row the quadric elements. In
the Abaqus Analysis User’s Guide the distinction is clariﬁed [40]. As illustrated,
the linear elements have a node in each corner and use linear interpolation between
the nodes. These type of elements are also referred to as ﬁrst-order elements. The
quadric elements have a mid-node between the corner nodes and use quadric in-
terpolation. These are also termed second-order elements. The triangular elements,
tetrahedrons, use a modiﬁed second-order interpolation method, and is also called
modiﬁed second-order elements.
FIGURE 8.1: Different ﬁnite element modeling elements in 3D [44]
There are several considerations to take into account when choosing element
type, some are listed here:
• Shell or 3D elements. In a welding simulation, the heat ﬂow is essential. In a
shell element, the heat ﬂow normal to a surface is excluded. Thereby the stress
==PAGE:76==
62
Chapter 8. Numerical approach
distribution is assumed to be linear through the thickness. Shell elements can
be beneﬁcial for thin walled structures, but is inadequate where the HAZ is of
importance.
• Linear or quadric elements. Det Norske Veritas (DNV) [28] suggests that lin-
ear elements give a more robust numerical model as the element formulation is
simpler. This especially applies for analyses involving large deformations and
rotations. Quadric elements are beneﬁcial where large quantum of linear ele-
ments are needed to achieve the same accuracy as found by quadric elements.
• Full or reduced integration. A full integration uses all the integration points
to form the element stiffness, while reduced integration uses a reduced num-
ber of these integration points for the same process. The drawback of using
the reduced approach, is the possible loss of accuracy. However, the compu-
tation time can be greatly reduced. When choosing the reduced integration
approach, one has to be aware of the zero energy modes, "hourglassing". DNV
recommend that hourglass control is used when choosing the reduced inte-
gration approach and that the hourglass energy is less than 5% of the internal
energy [28].
• Sequentially coupled thermal-stress analysis. Abaqus User’s guide [40] dic-
tates that any of the heat transfer elements can be used in thermal analysis.
However, it should be noted that the corresponding continuum or structural
element must be chosen.
8.1.2
Mesh Density and Mesh Technique
As mentioned in the previous section, the needed number of elements i.e. mesh
density, heavily relies on the choice of elements and the accuracy needed for the cal-
culations. For a welding simulation, the heat gradient and thereby also the stress,
greatly varies through the model part. Knowing that the temperature gradient is
large at the weld and near the HAZ, one can customize the mesh density to have
smaller elements in this area and a more coarse mesh where the temperature gradi-
ent is expected to be of minor importance.
There are also several types of meshing techniques, depending on the needs. In
the following, some of the techniques are presented according to Abaqus deﬁnition
[40].
• Free meshing. This is the easiest way of meshing. It does not require any
predeﬁned paths or partitions.
• Swept mesh. This technique is used for complex solid and surface regions. It
involves two steps, ﬁrst a mesh is applied to the source side, then that mesh is
copied to all the element layer, until it reaches the target side. This technique i
chosen when using AWI to simulate a weld sequence.
• Structured and mapped meshing. Here a predeﬁned mesh topology is used
to create the structural mesh. Abaqus uses the mesh of a regular region and
transforms it to ﬁt an irregular region. The mapped mesh is a sub-category of
the structural mesh. "Mapped meshing only refers to the structural mesh of
four-sided, two-dimensional regions" [40].
• Bottom-up meshing.
All the other techniques uses a top-down approach,
however, bottom-up meshing gives the opportunity to mesh the other way
==PAGE:77==
8.2. Thermal Modelling
63
around. It is a manual process and is not recommended unless the top-down
techniques are insufﬁcient.
8.2
Thermal Modelling
When modelling a welding situation, the study of heat and heat propagation is es-
sential. In previous chapters, the theoretical part of this was been discussed, and
now the modelling approach in Abaqus will be reviewed.
In Chapter 6, the accuracy classiﬁcations are discussed and found that the pre-
scribed temperature approach yields a basic/standard accuracy. This also corre-
sponds well with the scope of this thesis, in terms of a multi-pass weld and the main
objective being residual stresses.
The built in tool in Abaqus applies the prescribed temperature approach. How-
ever, Jessen [39] developed a subroutine for Abaqus in 2016, which utilizes the al-
ready existing subroutine, Dﬂux, to model the heat input as a double ellipsoid heat
source model. Jessen [39] found that there was little difference in the two modelling
approaches in regards of residual stresses from a multi-pass weld.
Goldak and Akhlaghi [8] suggests that a weak point in applying the prescribed
temperature approach lies in the starting of the weld. At the start of a weld, when
the heat is applied to the material, a molten layer starts to form, and this layer will
grow with the time of the applied heat. With the prescribed temperature approach
one gets a sharp transition from the cold material to the molten weld pool. However,
they have also concluded that this start transition has little effect on the temperature
ﬁeld later on. Meaning, that if the starting point of the weld is not of special interest,
it can be ignored.
As a conclusion, taking into consideration both the accuracy and ease of mod-
elling, the prescribed temperature approach is chosen.
8.2.1
Heat Modelling - Prescribed Temperature
AWI uses the prescribed heat source modelling for describing the temperature distri-
bution. With this approach one assumes that the beads are laid down in discrete
chunks. Each chunk is deposited into the model and then activated by a "model
change" in Abaqus.
A step-wise introduction to the prescribed temperature approach:
• The beads are placed in discrete chunks along the weld path.
• The holding time for each chunk is calculated by ttorch = Lchunk
v
. Where Lchunk
is the length of the chunk and, v is the welding speed.
• The target torch temperature, normally higher than the melting temperature,
is applied at the surface between the chunk and base material1.
• The target torch temperature is ramped up over the holding time, starting at
the initial temperature of the material. This ramping period is usually small
compared to the holding time.
• The initial temperature of the weld material is applied to the bulk mass of the
chunk.
==PAGE:78==
64
Chapter 8. Numerical approach
FIGURE 8.2: Illustration of area where the torch heat up temperature
is applied [1] simpliﬁed shown for a one bead weld.
This means that the heat input depends on the volume of the chunk, the surface
area between the chunk, the base material and the holding time for each chunk. This
way the surface represents the target temperature of the arc, and the bulk mass of the
chunk, the weld pool. The initial temperature of the weld material 2 should be the
melting temperature of the material or higher. The procedure is presented step-wise
in Figure 8.3.
1
4
3
2
FIGURE 8.3: Sequence of placing the chunks. 1. A cold material 2. The
target torch heat up temperature is applied to the interface surface 3.
The chuck is laid down with the weld material’s initial temperature
4. The procedure continues for the next chunk in the bead.
1The cross hatched area in Figure 8.2.
2Temperature of chunk bulk mass.
==PAGE:79==
8.3. Mechanical Modelling
65
This type of approach aims to model the amount of heat that is applied to the
heat affected zone (HAZ), rather than trying to replicate the actual temperature dis-
tribution.
8.2.2
Bead Removal Approach
Pass-By-Pass bead removal means that all the passes are active in the model through-
out the simulation, except for a model change step where the pass is removed and
reinstated within a very short period (10−7s). This ensures that the passes are strain
free until they are deposited in the model. This is more accurate as the weld material
that is yet to be laid down is not affected by the strain from the previous passes.
The reason for having all passes active through the simulation is the nodal dis-
placement that occurs in the welding process. In the present work there is a compre-
hensive model where large displacements are expected in the HAZ. With these large
displacements, there would be a difference between the nodal coordinates stored at
the beginning of the the analysis and the displaced nodal coordinates during the
analysis. This can cause difﬁculties with reaching convergence during the analysis.
Model-Level bead removal means that all the beads are removed at the begin-
ning of the analysis and deposited into the model one by one. This approach is very
intuitive, as the simulation will show a process very much alike the a real life weld-
ing process. However, it might be difﬁcult to reach convergence due to the nodal
displacements mentioned above. Abaqus recommends that for stress analysis this
method is only applied for models with ﬁrst order elements.
Now, in the heat analysis there are no displacements and the model-level bead
removal is applied. AWI has preselected this for the heat analysis and only lets the
user chose a approach for the stress analysis.
8.2.3
Thermal Properties and Boundary Conditions
In Section 7.3.1 the thermal properties: speciﬁc heat, density and thermal conductiv-
ity was discussed. When modelling these properties, their temperature dependency
are included as material behaviours in Abaqus.
The thermal boundary conditions in terms of Abaqus deﬁnition is the target heat-
up temperature applied to the interface surface between the base material and the
chunk.
8.3
Mechanical Modelling
The mechanical properties are reviewed in Section 7.3, where its evident that the
temperature plays an important part in modelling the mechanical properties of the
material. In the following the parameters needed in the modelling phase are dis-
cussed.
8.3.1
Bead Removal Approach
As described in the previous section, there is expected large strains in the welding
region and to avoid complications with convergence, the Pass-By-Pass approach is
used in the present work for the stress analysis.
==PAGE:80==
66
Chapter 8. Numerical approach
The element selection discussed in Section 8.1, also shows that ﬁrst-order ele-
ments are used in the model, so by Abaqus recommendations the Model-Level bead
removal could be used. However, due to the large strains that were expected, the
Pass-By-Pass bead removal was the superior choice.
8.3.2
Material Model
In Section 6.2, the accuracy classes were discussed, and it was concluded that a ba-
sic/standard approach is sufﬁcient in a multi-weld simulation with residual stresses
as the main interest. This means that a cut-off temperature of at least 0.5Tmelt is
required.
Hansen [15] suggests a melting temperature of 1500◦C as the temperature of the
solid material is estimated to 1495◦C and the liquidus temperature 1540◦C. These
values are adopted to the present work.
When, Tmelt = 1500◦C, the cut-off temperature needs to be at a minimum of
750◦C. In Chapter 7, the temperature dependence of all the mechanical parameters
are presented. There it can be seen that all parameters are modelled up to a mini-
mum of a 1000◦C, satisfying the requirement for basic/standard accuracy.
An annealing temperature of 1500◦C is also implemented in the model, equal to
the melting temperature. For all temperatures above annealing, the strains are set to
zero.
The base material in the present work is steel S355ML, and all properties that are
relevant are presented in Section 7.3. Due to the lack of information about the ﬁller
material, it is modelled after the properties of the base material. Hence, all effects
that may rise due to differences in strength, are neglected in the present work.
==PAGE:81==
67
Chapter 9
Veriﬁcation of model
In the following chapter the basis of the numerical model is reviewed. First, all the
properties used in the models are presented and then some of the model features are
examined with the purpose of verifying, validating and optimizing the model.
9.1
Overview of Model
9.1.1
Plate Information
The plate that was modelled is seen in Figure 9.1.
The main plate was welded
with SAW in the longitudinal direction. The cross plates were welded on the main
plate to minimize the angular distortion associated with welding. How these plates
were modelled is a matter of structural boundary conditions. The end plates was
welded on the main plate and their effect on the angular distortion and residual
stress growth had to be considered.
It would have been beneﬁcial for the computational time to only model half of
the plate as it appears to be symmetrical around the welding line, see Figure 9.1.
Except for the lifting eye on the left edge, which was considered to be of much lesser
stiffness than the plate it-self and therefore not "activated", hence it was not con-
tributing to the overall stiffness. The temperature was expected to dissipate in the
same manner to both sides, which also supports the idea of only modelling half the
plate. However, the beads were not deposited symmetrically 1, making it necessary
to model the full plate.
FIGURE 9.1: The plate that was modelled, with its dimensions to-
gether with placements of the cross-plates and end-plates.
1See Figure 9.4 for sketch of bead geometry
==PAGE:82==
68
Chapter 9. Veriﬁcation of model
In Figure 9.2 the plate with its partitions in all three directions are shown. Nearly
all the partitions are included to be able to extract temperature measurements at the
desired locations relative to time. The locations here, correspond to the placement
of the thermo-pins from the experimental work. As partitions restricts the meshing,
it is advantageous to limit both the number of partitions and their extent as much as
possible. In the Figure 9.2 it is seen that the horizontal partition only extend over the
area were thermal measurement where extracted.
FIGURE 9.2: Plate modelled in Abaqus with all its partitions.
The through-thickness partition ensures that the temperatures were extracted at
the middle of the plate, y = 20mm. In the longitudinal direction, the eight partitions
closest to the weld on each side, are there to be able to extract temperatures at the
positions of the thermo-pins, which varies in both x- and z-direction. The outer par-
tition on each side is there to be able to change the mesh seeding in the longitudinal
direction. In the transverse direction, there are also eight partitions on each side of
the weld, all with the same purpose as the eight longitudinal partitions.
The partitions in the welding area in the transverse direction are there to be able
to discretely deposit the beads. In Figure 9.2 the beads are divided into 50 equally
sized chunks.
9.1.2
Summary of Modeling Approach
Coordinate System
As seen in Figure 9.2 the x-direction is transverse to the weld, y-direction represented
the thickness of the plate and z-direction is the welding direction, in the present
work determined as the longitudinal direction.
Units
Abaqus does not operate with units, it is up to the user to chose the a unit system
and interpret the results with that system in mind. In the current work, SI-units
are chosen, with the exception of temperature, which are implemented as degrees
Celsius, not Kelvin. The two scales have the same increments, and is therefore not a
issue for the calculations.
Material properties
In Section 7.3 the values for each material property included in the model is pre-
sented. The material used is steel S355ML, meaning that it is thermomechanically
==PAGE:83==
9.1. Overview of Model
69
rolled. To summarize, these are the properties that were implemented for both the
base and ﬁller material:
• Thermal properties
– Speciﬁc heat capacity, Figure 7.2
– Density, Figure 7.3
– Thermal conductivity, Figure 7.4
• Mechanical properties
– Young’s modulus, Figure 7.5
– Yield stress, Figure 7.6
– Thermal expansion, Figure 7.7
– Poisson’s ratio, Figure 7.8
– Stress-strain curve, incl. plasticity, Figure 7.12
In addition to the properties listed above, AWI requires information about the
heat dissipation from the boundaries, in the form of convection and radiation. The
following values were used:
• Absolute zero temperature: −273.15◦C
• Stefan-Boltzmann constant: 5.67 · 10−8 W/m2/◦C4
• Emissivity: 0.9
• Film coefﬁcient: 5.77 W/m2/◦C
Elements
In the model, two different elements were used. In the thermal analysis 8-node
linear heat transfer brick elements were applied, while in the mechanical analysis
8-node linear stress brick elements with reduced integration and hourglass control
were used.
Thermal : DC3D8
Mechanical : C3D8R
In Figure 9.19 the applied mesh is shown. The meshing pattern was created
utilizing the meshing technique swept mesh with varying element size; Starting with
2 mm at the weld, increasing to 20 mm at the plate ends.
Prescribed Temperature
The chosen method of implementing the heat from the arc during the welding was
the prescribed temperature approach.
• The continuous process of welding is in the numerical model interpreted as
discrete chunks being deposited at a certain rate. The chunks, consist of an
inter-facial boundary between the chunk and the base material, where the
torch temperature is applied and a solid mass representing the welding pool.
The temperature of the weld pool must minimum be equal to the melting tem-
perature of steel.
Ttorch = 2500◦C
Tmelt = 1500◦C
==PAGE:84==
70
Chapter 9. Veriﬁcation of model
• In the mechanical analysis, all the chunks are present during the analysis, ex-
cept for a short step where the chunk is removed right before the temperature
load is applied. This ensures that the chunks are strain-free before the load
is applied, while maintaining the connection to the relevant nodes. A more
in-depth elaboration about the pass-by-pass bead removal is decried in 8.2.2.
9.2
Plate and Weld Geometry
The geometry prior to welding is shown in Figure 9.3, where one can see that there
is a 30◦angle on the top 35mm of the plate, leaving 5mm of uncut edge.
FIGURE 9.3: Plate geometry prior to welding. All measurements are
in millimetres and degrees.
The dotted lines in Figure 9.3 represents the placement of the thermo-pins used
to measure the temperature during the welding. In Appendix C more information
about the placement is given.
At the time of the welding experiment, a sketch of the bead geometry was made.
This sketch is merely an indication of the placement of the beads and not an exact
geometrical representation.
The process of ﬁnding the exact geometry of the weld during the welding is
a tedious task with little to gain. However, the welded plate was cut during the
experiment and could be treated with acid to make the contour of each bead more
distinct. This was not completed in the present work, the weld geometry in Abaqus
is thereby solely based on the sketch drawn during the welding, as shown in Figure
9.4.
The volume of each bead should be approximately the same, with small varia-
tions due to the heat input.
FIGURE 9.4: Left: Weld geometry sketched during the experiment.
Right: Weld geometry sketched in Abaqus.
The welding beads were deposited in the following sequence:
==PAGE:85==
9.3. Analysis of Experimental Thermal Data
71
1. The top bead was ﬁrst welded. There are little information about this bead,
neither temperature measurements, nor exact information about the heat input
is present in the experimental report.
2. The welding area was pre-heated to about 100◦C
3. Then, bead 1-15 was welded. Here both temperature measurements and exact
information about the heat input was present.
4. Finally the bottom bead was welded. This bead had the same information as
bead 1-15.
This sequence caused some confusion, as a bottom bead would normally be
welded ﬁrst, with the task of keeping the two parts together. In addition, the bound-
ary conditions were changed between the ﬁrst 16 beads and the bottom bead.
As there is little information about the top bead, no thermal data from this bead is
compared to the simulation values. However, it was chosen to model the top bead to
include the potential stresses that might manifest from it. To shorten the numerical
simulation, it was not chosen to let the part cool down to 20◦, then pre-heat the
welding area. Instead, bead 1 was deposited 175s after the top bead, yielding the
same temperature in the welding area as if it was preheated.
Regarding the bottom bead, it was initially planned to include it in the model as
sketched in Figure 9.4. Then a model with a larger gap between the top and bottom
bead was tried. Neither models gave results, as Abaqus could not ﬁnd convergence
in the stress analysis. It was chosen to model without the bottom bead, knowing
that it is welded last and might give signiﬁcant affect to the stress values in the
surrounding areas.
9.3
Analysis of Experimental Thermal Data
The experimental data given by Andersen and Hassan [2] was analysed to verify
both the heat input and the heating/cooling time associated with each bead. In Fig-
ure 9.5 the temperature measurements are presented for the plate PL40_1_2, welded
by submerged arc welding (SAW).
FIGURE 9.5:
Temperature measurements from the experimental
works executed by Andersen and Hassan [2]
Each graph in Figure 9.5 represents the measurements from senors marked 101-
116. In Appendix C the placement and label of each senor is speciﬁed.
The ﬁrst step of analyzing the measurements was to ﬁnd the laptimes, which is the
total time associated with each bead. The laptime is thereby the sum of the welding
time of one bead and the subsequent cooling before the welding of the next bead was
==PAGE:86==
72
Chapter 9. Veriﬁcation of model
initiated. The cooling period between the welding of the beads is hereafter referred
to as interpasses. After the last bead was deposited, a prolonged cooling time can be
seen, this is to ensure that the plate reaches room temperature. This cooling time is
called the afterpass.
The laptimes were found by establishing the time between each peak in the tem-
perature measurements as seen in Figure 9.6. The peaks are found using a Matlab
script included in the Appendix D. It was concluded that using the temperature
measurements from the sensor placed closest to the weld (sensor 101, see appendix
C) would give the most precise lap times, as this sensor is the least affected by the
thermal properties of the material.
FIGURE 9.6: Peak temperatures from the experimental works exe-
cuted by Andersen and Hassan [2]
Having found the laptimes, the next step was to establishing the welding times.
The welding speed was given in the welding report by Andersen and Hassan [2],
hence, it was only a matter of dividing the speed by the weld length to ﬁnd time
associated with each bead.
By subtracting the welding time per bead from the laptimes, the interpass was
determined. The sequential steps are summarized in Equation (9.1) to Equation (9.3).

9.4
Pass control
With AWI one can efﬁciently build a weld with varying passes, one pass being the
same as a chunk of the bead. The ﬁlm and radiation properties (Table 9.3) were cho-
sen on the basis of the work done by Sommerlund and Ammitzbøl [1] in 2015. Some
later works also included the ﬁlm coefﬁcient as temperature dependent. However,
as mentioned in Section 7.3 the importance of the temperature dependence of the
thermal properties varies and for a welding simulation it can be sufﬁcient to use a
room temperature. This is further supported by the Saupstads’ thesis by in 2016 [37,
p. 113], where he investigated the effects of a lower emissivity and concluded that it
has little inﬂuence one the residual stresses and strains.

The surfaces in Table 9.3 are deﬁned in Appendix B. It can be seen in Table 9.3
that no ﬁlm and radiation properties are included for the the symmetry faces, ﬂux
area, bead area or the base area. This is based upon the fact that at the symmetry
faces, the material would in reality go on, so there would not be a boundary there.
For the last three surfaces, the granular ﬂux is considered to cover the areas, which
means that no heat should in theory be able to dissipate from these surfaces.
Having no heat dissipating through these areas means that the granular ﬂux is
considered to be 100% effective in "locking in" the heat. Is a conservative assumption
and might cause the temperatures from the numerical model to be slightly higher
than what is experimentally measured.
The pass control in AWI requires a speciﬁed increment deﬁnition for all the
passes. The chosen values are presented in Table 9.4, where a try and fail approach
is used to determine the suitable increment size.

9.5. Boundary Conditions
75
The remove step in Table 9.4 represents both the steps where all the beads are
removed in one step in the heat analysis and all the steps in the stress analysis where
the beads are removed one at the time. The insert steps refer to the steps where the
beads are inserted one at a time in both analysis. The load steps are the steps where
the temperature is applied to the interface-surface in the heat analysis and where
the nodal temperatures are applied in the stress analysis. Finally, the steps with the
interpasses are presented. The time periods for the load step and the interpasses are
found in Table 9.1.
In Chapter 7, one can see that these were the thermal properties discussed in
relation to the heat output; conductivity, convection and radiation. The conductivity
is included in the model as a part of the material properties, while the convection
and radiation are implemented as thermal boundary conditions.
9.5
Boundary Conditions
During the welding process the plate was constrained in several ways, as shown
in Figure 9.7. In the welding direction, there were two stiffeners at the end of each
weld, called end plates. These have two functions, ﬁrst of all the arc is lit and have
started to make the welding pool before it reaches the plate. This way, the weld-
ing bead will be as homogeneous as possible throughout the length of the plate.
Secondly, they have a stiffening purpose. Some large tension forces were expected
in the transverse direction relative to the weld, these could potentially cause some
distortion. When examining the welded plate, it can see that there are little to none
angular distortion in the longitudinal direction of the plate. However, the end-plates
show some angular distortion, given that they where mounted parallel to the plate.
The end-plates was evaluated to have little effect on the stress distribution at the
point of interest, which was at the transverse plane at the middle of the plate. This
is due to the placement of the cut in the experimental works.
FIGURE 9.7
==PAGE:90==
76
Chapter 9. Veriﬁcation of model
Two cross-plates was mounted on the main plate by the means of tack weld-
ing. There is little information about the geometry and welding design of the cross-
plates, which made the modeling process somewhat difﬁcult. The known details are
listed below:
• Given dimensions from experimental report: (10x100x1000)mm.
However,
this is not in agreement with the picture-documentation from the experiment,
where all the cross-plates are shorter than the main plate. In correspondence
with Figure 9.7, the dimensions were assumed to be as the following:
H : 100mm
W : 10mm
D : 400mm
• The cross-plates were welded on the main plate before the welding started, and
was present when bead 1-15 and the top bead was welded. Then, the cross-
plates were removed before the bottom bead was welded. Bead deﬁnition can
be seen in Figure 9.4
• The weld design (point or continuous weld) was unknown, but from examin-
ing the plates it was possible to make an estimate of the weld design.
Based on the above details it was assumed that the cross-plates were attached
2cm from the edge and its length of 40 cm was centred on the 50 cm plate, leaving 5
cm on each side.
Due to the fact that the cross-plate were removed during the welding process,
there were some uncertainties regarding the constraints of the model.
The stresses were expected to be of the greatest magnitude near the area where
the last bead was deposited. According to Figure 9.4 the last bead was placed on the
top right side of the weld. On that account, the bottom bead was assumed to be of
minor importance to the maximum tension stresses.
It should be noted that the heat input from the bottom bead is in the range of
twice the magnitude of all the other beads. Therefor it is evident that the bottom
bead can not be neglected as it will have an impact on the total stress distribution of
the plate.
With all the above mentioned facts in mind a proposal for boundary conditions
was developed. In Figure 9.8 the constrains applied to the Abaqus model is illus-
trated. A line constraint in y-direction is implemented as shown in the left picture
to simulate the effect of the cross-plates. Modeling the cross-plates with the cor-
rect stiffness would have given a more visually similar model and also allow small
deformation. However, the deformations would have been modest due to the stiff-
ness about the z-axis of the cross-plate. On that account, a ﬁxed constraint in the
y-direction is assumed to be a valid assumption.
The cross-plate also constrained the plate from large movements in the x-direction.
Therefor the points in the left and middle picture in Figure 9.8 are ﬁxed in the x-
direction. Lastly, the plate is ﬁxed in z-direction to make sure the plate does not
have any translation or rotation. The right picture in Figure 9.8 shows the points
where the z-constraints are applied.
These constraints was expected to give reaction forces where the magnitude of
the line constraint in y-direction was of the greatest magnitude. The x-constraint
was expected to give small reaction forces, as the distortion in the x-direction should
be small compared to the y-direction. The z-constraint should not give any reaction
forces as there are no forces that would imply distortion in that direction.
==PAGE:91==
9.6. Thermal modelling
77
FIGURE 9.8: Boundary conditions implemented in Abaqus.
9.6
Thermal modelling
There were two measurements that could validate the thermal model, the heat input
and the temperature measurement. The heat input was given in the experimental
report by Andersen and Hassan [2] as current, voltage and welding speed. While
the temperature measur ements were given as a separate Excel-ﬁle.
9.6.1
Heat input
To adjust the heat input in the numerical model, one could change the following
parameters:
• Temperature of bulk mass of the beads
• Transverse length of boundary surface
• Holding time for each chunk (time period in Table 9.1)
• Torch temperature (for the boundary surface)
The bulk mass of the bead is known to represent the melted steel and can there-
fore minimum have the melting temperature of the steel, here equal to 1500◦C.
The heat input is represented as energy per length of weld, hence the transverse
length of the boundary surface affects the magnitude of the heat input. This length,
together with the holding time is the only parameters that varies from bead to bead.
All lengths of the chunks are the same, hence there is a linear relationship between
the transverse length of the surface and the total surface area for a chunk. In Figure
9.9 the surface area for each bead together with the temperature output from Abaqus
are shown.
One can see a certain correlation between the surface areas and the experimental
temperature measurements, where the temperature increase more rapidly is where
the surface area is larger. Accordingly, the chosen geometry of the beads, and there-
fore also the transverse length of the surface area is considered to be valid.
The holding time per chunk is calculated to match the total welding time per
bead in the experiment, and is therefore considered to be unalterable. This leaves
the torch temperature as the most likely source of error for the heat input.
==PAGE:92==
78
Chapter 9. Veriﬁcation of model
FIGURE 9.9: Mean value of all the temperature measurements from
the experiment (left axis), together with surface area per chunk in the
different beads (right axis).
Torch temperature
The torch temperature (Ttorch) was ﬁrst set to 2500◦C based on experimental work
in connection to the heat modeling approach - prescribed temperature [1, p. 76]. In
Figure 9.10, the heat input extracted from Abaqus together with the calculated heat
input from the experiment is shown. One can see that the heat input from Abaqus
exceeds the experimental vales by approximately 45% for each bead.
Due to the large shift between the heat input from the numerical model and the
experiment, a lowered torch temperature was tested. A 20% decrease was ﬁrst tried,
yielding a torch temperature of 2000◦C.
FIGURE 9.10: Heat input with a prescribed temperature of 2000◦C
(red), 2500◦C (blue) and the heat input from the experiment (yellow).
The heat input from both torch temperatures is seen in the Figure above. It is
evident that the decrease in torch temperature did not have the wanted impact on
the heat input. A further decrease of the temperature seamed unlikely considering
the welding method used.
The tendency on the other hand, shows good correlation between experiment
and numerical model. This further supports the assumption that the variation be-
tween each bead (transverse length of surface area and holding time per chunk) are
correctly chosen in relation to the experiment.
==PAGE:93==
9.6. Thermal modelling
79
9.6.2
Temperature distribution
The temperature distribution in the numerical model depends on several parameters
that can be modiﬁed to correspond to the experimental measurements:
• Heat input
• Thermal material properties
• Thermal boundary conditions
The heat input is discussed in the section above, and it was concluded that the
main parameters that could be varied was the torch temperature. Hence, the torch
temperature is also essential to the temperature distribution. The thermal material
properties include the speciﬁc heat capacity, density and thermal conductivity. The
thermal boundary conditions in this context, include the heat transfer to the sur-
roundings by convection and radiation. The convection is implemented in Abaqus
as the ﬁlm coefﬁcient with a value of 5.77 W/m2/◦C and the radiation as an emis-
sivity of 0.9.
All of the above parameters could be experimented with to optimize the model.
Zhu and Chao [49] suggest that the temperature dependence of all the thermal ma-
terial properties should have little effect on the residual stresses, with the largest
uncertainty for the thermal conductivity.
As mentioned earlier, Saupstad [37] experimented with implementing different
values for the emissivity and found that it had little effect on the residual stresses,
which is the main objective in this thesis.
In conclusion, the parameters most beneﬁcial to experiment with to optimize the
numerical model are the torch temperature, thermal conductivity and ﬁlm coefﬁ-
cient.
Torch temperature
In Figure 9.11 to 9.13 the temperature distribution for different torch temperatures
are shown. The black graphs represent the experimental measurements, while the
red ones are from the numerical model in Abaqus.
FIGURE 9.11: Torch temperature of 2000◦C

imental values from the above graphs. To make it clearer, all the average values for
each torch temperature was found. In Figure 9.14, the a plot with both the variying
torch temperatures and the experimental values are shown.

9.7
Mesh density
To be able to validate the results found in Abaqus, one has to be certain that the mesh
density is adequate. To this end, a convergence test of the mesh density was carried
out. Starting with a coarse distribution of the elements and gradually increasing it
until the values of interests stagnates.
In the present work, the values of interest are the temperature distribution over
time in the position of the thermo-pins, and the maximum tension at the weld area
after the part has cooled to room temperature.
There were 8 thermo-pins on each side of the weld in the experimental work. To
be able to compare the experimental work to the numerical results from Abaqus, the
temperatures had to be measured at the same points. Thus, there were 16 tempera-
ture graphs for each mesh that needed to be validated. This would make a confusing
presentation, and therefore it is chosen to only compare the maximum temperature
measured for each bead for the thermo-pin placed closest to the weld. This is be-
cause of the fact that higher temperatures cause more expansion and ﬁnally, higher
stresses, which is the main interest of this thesis.
One expects large strains in the heat affected area and decreasingly strains to-
wards the edge of the plate. Hence, stresses are validated by comparing the maxi-
mum values of the strains.
As the highest temperatures and consequently the largest stresses are found at
the weld area, this is where the elements needs to be smallest. To save computational
time, the elements are made larger towards the end of the plate where strains of
minor magnitude was expected.
Before starting the modelling process it was expected that the results would show
an symmetrical tendency around the weld-line. After several simulations had con-
ﬁrmed this, the element size on one side of the weld was grossly increased, and the
results from one side only was considered. This saved some computational time.
The mesh was divided into zones, where the mesh density varied between each
zone. The zones are shown in Appendix A, and the variation for each mesh is shown
in Table 9.6
TABLE 9.6: The different mesh density at the meshing zones for all
the different meshes. All values are in meters.
