Explanation of the meshes:
• Mesh 1: The mesh is quite coarse in all zones.
• Mesh 2: Here the seeding in the bead area is changed, yielding an expectation
of a possible change in both the temperature distribution and maximum stress
value.
• Mesh 3: Here the global mesh is changed, meaning that all elements in the
welding area are effected in the longitudinal direction. Again, its expected
that both values of interest might change.
• Mesh 4: The same change as in mesh 1 and mesh 2 is made, yielding the same
expectations.
• Mesh 5: Now, both the transition zone between the bead-area and the rest of
the plate is changed, together with the longitudinal seed spacing of the area
near the end of the plate. This means that no change should be observed in the
maximum stress value, but the temperature distribution might be affected. The
reason for checking this mesh is related to the large error that can be seen in
Figure 9.16 in the transition zone at approximately 30 mm from the centerline
of the plate.
Due to the excessive computational time of the welding simulation, some simpli-
ﬁcations had to be made in relation to the convergence test. Only two beads with the
corresponding cooling periods were modelled. This means that there were only two
temperature peaks instead of sixteen and that the maximum stresses did not reach
the full magnitude of what a complete simulation would show.
These are both approximations, but considered to be within reason as the same
values are checked for each mesh at the same point in time. In Figure 9.15 a plot of
the convergence for the ﬁrst two temperature peaks are shown. Next, the conver-
gence related to the stress distribution is shown, Figure 9.16 to 9.18.
FIGURE 9.15: Maximum temperature measured for bead 1 and bead
2.
From the ﬁgure above one can see that mesh 4 gave satisfying results, where a
change of 1% and 0.9% can be seen for the two peaks between mesh 4 and mesh 5.
==PAGE:97==
9.7. Mesh density
83
The investigation of the convergence related to the stresses, needed more atten-
tion than the convergence of the heat analysis. In Figure 9.16 one can see that the
average relative error2 is remote for most of the plate, with a large deviation at about
30 mm from the center of the weld. The deviation is seen to be smaller for each re-
ﬁnement of the mesh, ending up at a about 20% for mesh 4. However, looking at
the percentage gives a false impression of the actual change, as can be seen in Figure
9.17 and 9.18. Where, it is evident that there is a good correlation between the stress
levels for the different meshes at the same area that shows large percentage of error
in Figure 9.16. This is likely caused by the fact that the stresses at this area are close
to zero and therefore any change will yield large percentages.

One can see that on the left side, the mesh is extremely coarse. This is due to
the symmetry earlier discussed, where one was to model the whole plate due to the
asymmetric placement of the beads. However, it is only the stiffness of the whole
plate which is relevant to the stresses, not the results from both sides of the weld. As
both the thermal and mechanical analysis showed symmetrical results, some com-
putational time was be saved by using the coarse mesh on one side.
FIGURE 9.20: The ﬁnal mesh used in both the thermal and mechanical

9.8
Chunk length
In the model, each bead is divided into 8 chunks of equal length, yielding a chunk
length of 62.5mm. A model with 10 chunks was also tried, this model did did not
ﬁnish due to severe computational time. In more simple models, it has been tried to
vary the chunk length to optimize the heat propagation. This would also be beneﬁ-
cial for the current work, however it was not prioritized.

==PAGE:101==

Chapter 10
Experimental Works
10.1
Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
10.1.1
Introduction
This section contains a detailed description of the preparation, execution and results
concerning a preliminary experiment of the contour method. The main objective
of this preliminary experiment is to obtain knowledge and experience concerning
the contour method, in order to reduce the mistakes and errors linked to the execu-
tion of the main experiment. The numerical result of this preliminary experiment is
not analysed, as there is no scientiﬁc method to verify or validate the obtained data.
Students and technicians who perform this preliminary experiment had no previous
experience with this method, which emphasizes the importance of a preliminary ex-
periment. As the contour method is a destructive testing method, a test experiment
is even more important as there only one chances of success for each cut. The theory
and concept of the contour method can be seen in Chapter 5.
Only one specimen was used in the current experiment, were only one EDM cut
was conducted, with a subsequent set of CMM measurement. As the numerical re-
sults of the preliminary work were not the main object in this test, the specimen was
made in a rather unscientiﬁc manner. Two steel (S355) plates were welded together,
where none of the welding parameters were carefully thought out.
The cut was made by EDM, then measured by CMM, and then the measurements
was processed using Matlab. The data were thereafter implemented in a FE-model,
in Abaqus, where linear-elastic theory was utilized to calculated the residual stresses
which had manifested in the specimen due to the welding.
10.1.2
Target
The contour method is a destructive test method, which means that there is only one
chance of success for each cut. Depending on the size and geometry of the specimen,
only a handful of cuts can be carried out per specimen. This means that the success
of the experiment heavily depends on the executers understanding and overview of
the difﬁculties and details in the method.
All participants included in the preliminary work had no prior experience with
the contour method. This in itself lead to the decision of conducting a preliminary
experiment. The target, was to obtain knowledge and experience, such that the risk
of mistakes and errors in relation to the execution of the main experiment were min-
imized. The key aspects, which were focused on, and predicted to be challenging,
are listed below.
• Parameters for the EDM cut: The manner of which the cut is executed has large
effect on the result. It is important that the surface is of satisfactory roughness
==PAGE:102==
88
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
and that the cut surface is straight. From literature[5] it is known that a rough-
ness equal to Ra = 3.0 is within reason.
• CMM approach: The focus in this aspect, were to get an overview of the best
orientation and sequence of the measurements. In addition, the time associ-
ated with the measurements was of interest, as it was speculated that the CMM
available was not suitable in relation to the contour method.
• Data processing: Splines can be an advanced method to use in respect to the
mathematics, especially when working in three dimensions. Therefore, it was
important to get an understanding of the theory and how it is executed in
Matlab prior to the main experiment.
• Implementing data in a FE-model: The manner of which the data processed in
Matlab were implemented in the FE-model were unknown in the early stages
of the experiment. After some research, it was found that a Python script was
need for this task. The lack of experience with both Python and Abaqus, made
this aspect prone to challenge. The main question remained; was it feasible
for the authors to create the script within reasonable time or was external help
needed.
With these aspects in mind, the preliminary experiment was planned. A docu-
mentary analysis was conducted, to ensure coherence with earlier works regarding
the contour method. A large extent of the previous work was written by of Michael
B. Prime, which were considered to be the main literature for the present work. More
on the documentary analysis can be found in Chapter 2.
In the next section a description of the test specimen is presented. Here, the shape
and geometry of the specimen will be shown, together with the type of steel used.
10.1.3
Specimen
Although the shape, geometry and steel type is not of great importance in this test
experiment, the information is registered and presented in this section.
The steel used was of the type S355, which is a common structural steel. The
choice of steel was in this case, not of high signiﬁcance. This is due to the fact that the
numerical results would not be validated and the experiment was only conducted
for gaining knowledge and experience.
The specimen consisted of two steel plates, welded together by the means of
fusion welding. These plates were of the dimensions 15mm x 100mm x 400mm,
making the ﬁnished welded specimen approximately:
H : 15mm
W : 200mm
D : 400mm
In Figure 10.1, a picture of the plates prior to the welding is shown. One can see
that the plates were tack welded together.
In the upcoming section the setup of the welding, EDM cutting and CMM mea-
suring is presented. Here, the conditions, equipments and setup used will be shown
and described.
==PAGE:103==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
89
FIGURE 10.1: Picture of specimen tack welded prior to the welding.
10.1.4
Setup
The physical experiment can be looked upon as tripartite; the welding of the speci-
men, the EDM cutting and the CMM measuring. Within this section the three parts
will be discussed separately and chronologically to create a clear picture of how
these parts have been conducted.
Welding the Specimen
The setup of the welding resembles, a to large extent, a regular setup for welding,
where the welded pieces were securely clamped to a sturdy table and tacked to en-
sure constant gap between the plates. The main difference which can be empha-
sized, is the usage of a welding tractor system. This system assures a more con-
sistent welding bead in regards to the welding speed and pattern, compared to a
hand-held system. The welding tractor followed a rail which kept the welding bead
true and straight. A picture of the welding tractor system is shown in Figure 10.2.
The speciﬁcations regarding the welding tractor system can be viewed in Appendix
F.
FIGURE 10.2: Picture of welding tractor system used [47].

90
Chapter 10. Experimental Works

This apparatus consisted of a power supply, a welding gun with a wire feeder and a
shielding gas supplier. The gas used was of the type Argon and shielded the welding
pool from Nitrogen and Oxygen. To complete the circuit needed to generate an arch,
a wire was connected to the workplaces. The welding apparatus is shown in Figure
10.3 and speciﬁcations regarding the welding apparatus can be viewed in Appendix

After the welding, the plate had been distorted asymmetrically, due to inade-
quate clamping at one of the corners. It was observed that only a third of the plate
was severely distortion, it was therefore concluded to discard the affected third of
the plate to simplify the clamping procedure in the EDM cutting.
Making the Cut
The setup for the EDM cutting consisted mainly of the EDM machine. However, the
manner of which the plate was clamped and the settings used for the cut was essen-
tial to obtain a sufﬁcient cut according to the contour method. The EDM machine
was of the type +GF+ AgieCharmilles Cut E 350 and it was operated by technician
Henrik Petersen of the department of mechanical engineering at DTU. The speciﬁ-
cations regarding the EDM machine can be viewed in the manufactures speciﬁcation
data sheet [43]. A picture of the EDM machine is shown in Figure 10.6.
FIGURE 10.6: Picture of the EDM setup, showing the clamping ar-
rangement and placement of the cut.
==PAGE:107==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
93
When clamping the specimen it was important to ensure that the clamps were
placed symmetrically around the cut path. This was to ensure symmetrical deforma-
tion on either side of the cut, to keep the cut as straight as possible and to minimize
the error which can occur during the relaxation of the steel. These errors were dis-
cussed in Section 5.2.4. Also, as the plate was somewhat distorted, it was favourable
to build up supports underneath the plate so that a minimal amount of moment was
added to the plate. The setup in the EDM machine is shown in Figure 10.7. One can
see from the pictures that the plate had already been submerged in the deionized
water, this was to ensure that the clamping was correct in regards of thermal expan-
sion of the plate when submerged. It can also be seen that the cut was not placed at
the centre of the plate. Still, the rule of thumb regarding the effected length due to
the relaxation was upheld, where the depth were at least 3 times the thickness of the
steel to each side of the cut. This is further explained later in this chapter, where the
procedure of making the cut is dicussed.
FIGURE 10.7: Picture of the EDM setup.
The cut in itself had to be programmed in advance. At this stage, the setting for
the cut had to be programed and the wire had to be selected. From literature, it was
evident that the wire should be of brass [38, p.116] [34, p.2].
Low powered setting, also called ﬁrst skim cutting settings, was used. These set-
tings are optimal for achieving a good ﬁnish surface. In contrast to the rough cuts,
which are optimized for speed [38, p.116].
Prior to the cut of the plate in the preliminary experiment, two trial cuts were
executed, on some 5 cm long steel pieces, of the steel type S355. These trial cuts had
the purpose of determining the roughness, Ra, which was to be used further on in
the testing. A roughness of Ra=1.7 and Ra=3.0 were chosen for testing. Although,
the Ra=1.7 had a smoother ﬁnish, the Ra=3.0 had a far better execution time. The
latter with a cutting time of 10 minutes, in contrast to 3 hours and 7 minutes for
former. It was therefore evident that a roughness of Ra=3.0 had to be used. As
a roughness of Ra=1.7 would not be practically feasible, due to the extensive time
use. Further, it was found in a report authored by P. John Bouchard, Peter Ledgard,
Stan Hiller and Foroogh Hosseinzadeh [5], that a roughness around Ra=3.0 should
be sufﬁcient for use the contour method. Below, in Tabel 10.1, 10.2, 10.3 and 10.4 the
numerical values of the settings used are shown.

The setup for measuring the cut surfaces consisted mainly of the CMM. Only a few

The specimen was clamped in the orientation such that the cut surface was horizon-

that the plate should only be clamped at one place. This minimizes the risk of sub-
jecting the plate to unwanted moment, which can alter the stress state and deforma-
tions in the plate.
The probe used for the measuring was of a spherical ruby stylus type, with a
probe diameter of 2mm. This probe size is chosen on the basis of recommendations
in the paper authored of Michael B. Prime and Adrian T. DeWald [31, Section 5.3.4].

10.1.5
Procedure
In this section, the procedures for the welding, EDM cutting and measuring will
be presented. The structure of the section will resembled the structure in the setup
section, where the three aspects of the experiment will be presented separately and
chronologically to create a clear and orderly picture of the procedures.
Welding the Specimen
A chronological list of the procedure executed in regards to the welding of the spec-
imen is listed below.
Welding the Specimen
A chronological list of the procedure executed in regards to the welding of the spec-
imen is listed below.
==PAGE:112==
98
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
1. The plates were aligned in the desired positions and clamped to the sturdy
welding table.
2. Two small plates was tack welded on each short end of the plates to link them
together and to retain the alignment of the plates.
3. The welding tractor system was mounted to the sturdy welding table, by the
means of magnets and clamps.
4. The tacked plates were aligned with welding tractor rail and clamped to the
welding table.
5. The welding gun was attached to the welding tractor.
6. The welding tractor and welding gun were adjusted so that they aligned with
the desired welding path.
7. The welding parameters were entered into the welding apparatus and welding
tractor.
8. The welding tractor system was started so that a bead was deposited.
9. All slag were removed from weld bead after each welding-pass.
10. For each welding bead, step 6. to 9. was repeated.
11. Post welding, the work-piece had to be cooled before handling.
12. The work piece was cleaned before it was transported to the EDM machine.
Making the Cut
It was important to choose a placement for the cut which minimized the inﬂuence
of the free edges on the deformations of the cut-surfaces. Saint venant’s principle
of characteristic length was therefore utilized as a starting point. Further on, a con-
servative formula was used, which was based on Saint Venant’s principle [34]. The
formula states that the distance, l, from each of the parallel sides to the cut path,
must be equal to or larger than three times the plate thickness. Figure 10.11 shows a
sketch of this principle.
FIGURE 10.11: Cross-sectional sketch, showing the principle of Saint
Venant’s characteristic length which is used.
A chronological list of the procedure executed in regards to the EDM cutting of
the specimen is listed below.
1. The plate was managed with care at all times, to ensure minimum disturbance
to the surface.
==PAGE:113==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
99
2. The plate was placed in the EDM machine and ensured that the sides were
parallel with the axis system of the EDM machine.
3. The work tank was ﬁlled with temperature controlled deionized water(20◦C)
and the plate temperature was allowed to reach equilibrium.
4. The work tank was drained and the plate was clamped, as instructed in 10.1.4
and shown in Figure 10.7.
5. The work tank was ﬁlled again with temperature controlled deionized water(20◦C).
6. It was ensured that the correct wire material and diameter was loaded in the
EDM machine. Table 5.1 was used to select the diameter size.
7. The EDM cutting parameters were entered into the system control unit and the
cutting path was programmed.
8. The cutting program was started.
9. The work tank was drained post cutting.
10. The clamps were loosened in a careful manner. Special attention to the cut-
surfaces were given, as contact could have compromised the cut-surfaces.
11. The plate pieces were unloaded one by one, ensuring that no contact between
the cut-surfaces occurred.
12. The plates were dried as soon as possible, to minimize the chance of corrosion.
13. The cut-surfaces were protected before transported to the CMM.
Measuring the Surfaces
The plate was fastened as explained in the setup and then aligned, as accurately as
possible, to the axis of the CMM. This was to ensure that the surface, which were
to be measured, was plane relative to the horizontal axis. These alignments were
not crucial for achieving good measurements, however it ensured a tidiness of the
data and would simplify the data processing later on. In Figure 10.12 the plate with
coordinate system is shown.
FIGURE 10.12: Plane view of set-up sketch, showing the clamp and
coordinate system.
When programming the measuring order, the area which were to be measured
had to be deﬁned. The CMM software which was used for the programming in the
present work did not have the ability to deﬁne measuring areas other than rectan-
gular, square or lines. This lead to a problem, as the plates were deformed, it would
==PAGE:114==
100
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
not be possible to measure the whole surface. Instead, the largest area which would
ﬁt in the perimeter of the cut surface was programmed. A sketch illustrating the
location of the measured area is presented in Figure 10.13.
FIGURE 10.13: Cross-sectional sketch of the location of the measured
area on the cut surface for the preliminary experiment. Note, the scal-
ing of the sketch is not realistic and the deformations of the plate is
magniﬁed to emphasize the location of the measuring area.
The measuring area was therefore of a rectangular shape. Later, in the data pro-
gressing, see Section 10.1.6, an extrapolation of the points missing from the cut sur-
face perimeter was carried out. However, the extrapolation operation lead to adding
an even amount of points around the circumference of the measured area. Resulting
in a rectangular shape of the cut surface. Thus, disregarding the angular distortion
of the plate, and a plate with the theoretical dimensions were obtained. In Figure
10.14, a sketch of the end cut surface shape is presented.
FIGURE 10.14: Cross-section sketch of the location of measured area
on the cut surface for the preliminary experiment after extrapolating.
Note, the scaling of the sketch is not realistic and the extrapolated area
is magniﬁed to emphasize the location of the measuring area and the
extrapolating area.
Nevertheless, the disregard of the angular distortion of the plate is considered
a reasonable solution as the stiffness is the most important. As the main reason
for the extrapolation is to ensure that the modelled plate have the same stiffness
as the physical plate. The need for this approximation is due to the programming
limitations in the CMM software.
A chronological list of the procedure executed in regards to the measuring of the
cut surfaces is listed below.
1. During transportation and management of the plates, the plates were handled
with utmost care to to ensure minimum disturbance to the surface.
2. The ﬁrst half of the original plate was placed in the clamp mounted on the
granite tabletop of the CMM. The plate was fastened with the amount of force
that would ensure that the plate was sturdy and safe, without inducing any
moment.
==PAGE:115==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
101
3. The plate was aligned with the CMMs axis system.
4. The measuring sequence and the desired measuring grid was programmed.
5. The CMMs axis origin was placed in bottom left corner.
6. The measuring program generated in step 4 was started.
7. The measuring data was saved in the preferred ﬁle format, Excel.
8. For the second half of the plate, step 1. through 7. were repeated, with two
alterations in step 2. and 5. As seen in the sub-list below:
• In step 2. for plate two: The plate was mounted mirrored in relation to
the ﬁrst plate. This simpliﬁed the data processing.
• In step 5. for plate two: The CMMs axis origin was placed in the upper
left corner. This simpliﬁed the the data processing.
9. Post measuring, the plates were stored in a secure location and the cut-surface
was protected in case they needed to be remeasured.
10.1.6
Data Processing
In this section the data processing of the preliminary experiment is presented. It
must be noted that the numerical values of the stresses calculated, is of no scientiﬁc
value, as these can not be veriﬁed nor validated. The information which can be taken
from this section is strictly related to the manner of how the processing operations
were approached and executed, and also the gained overview of data processing.
There have been utilized two types of softwares to process the obtained CMM
data; Matlab and Abaqus. Matlab were used for the pretreatment of the data, where
several operation was executed to prepare the CMM measurements for usage in
the FE-model. The list below, show a chronological list of the numerous operations
needed for the data pretreatment.
1. Sorting
2. Aligning
3. Averaging
4. Translation
5. Smoothing
6. Extrapolation
Thereafter, the pretreated data was implemented in Abaqus. Here one half of the
plate was modelled after the the circumference measurements made by the CMM.
The pretreated data was applied as displacement boundary conditions on the cut
surface, together with the initial boundary conditions. The plate was thereafter, in
simple terms, stretched and squeezed according to these displacement boundary
conditions. With a material modelled as linear elastic, the residual stresses perpen-
dicular to the cut surface was determined.
Further on in this section, the implementation method of the operations per-
formed in Matlab and the process of applying the displacement boundary condi-
tions in Abaqus will be reviewed. Areas in need of improvements in relation to the
==PAGE:116==
102
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
main experiment will be accentuated. Scripts produced in Matlab in relation to the
preliminary experiment will not be attached as appendix, as these will be of no use
for further studies. However, the Matlab scripts related to the main experiment can
be seen in Appendixes I,K, L, N, M, O and P.

knowledge of the measuring order was here essential to be able to generate for loops
which would arrange the data in the matrix form needed for the following opera-
tions. An example of the matrix order needed, is shown below.

To simplify the process of sorting the data, it was important to set up the order of
the measurements in a way that eased the sorting process. Most important were the
consistency and regularity of the measurement order. In the preliminary experiment
the measurements were started in a corner, and the following points were measured
in the order shown in the matrices above. This proved to be a sufﬁcient order, all
though it had great potential from improvement. In Figure 10.15, the sorted raw
data is shown.
FIGURE 10.15: Raw sorted data.
==PAGE:117==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
103
The measuring order of the two surface separately, were found to be sufﬁcient.
However, relative to each other, the measurements was not optimal for the data
processing. In an effort to correct this, a plane over the order, which the points were
to be measured was made. This pattern of measuring was laid out in such a way that
an minimum effort was needed to mirror and align the data sets on top of each other
in Matlab. In Figure 10.16, the principal of the measuring order in illustrated. The
order is mirrored symmetrical about the x-axis, so that the only needed execution, is
to mirror the one data sett about the x-axis to align the to data sets on top off each
other. To get a better understanding of the layout, see Figure 10.16 in conjunction
with with Figure 10.34.
FIGURE 10.16: Plane view of the measuring order principal. Notice,
that the order is mirrored symmetrically about the x-axis and N is the
number of measurements per surface.
Aligning
In this operation, there were two steps taken; mirroring and aligning. To be able to
execute the next operation, which was averaging, the data from one of the plates had
to be mirrored, such that the x- and y-coordinates for the two plates coincided with
each other. This was easily done by multiplying the x- or y-matrix from one of the
plates, depending on which axis was desired mirrored around, by minus one.
As these were measured points, the x- and y-coordinates for the two plates would
never perfectly coincide after mirroring. Therefore, the x- and y-values for the two
plates be averaged to create a coinciding grid of x- and y-coordinates. This oper-
ation was called aligning. This averaging of the x- and y- coordinates was of no
signiﬁcance, as the amount the gird was moved is on a negligible level. In Figure
10.17 and 10.18, surface plots show the data after it had been mirrored and x- and
y-coordinates were averaged. It should be noted that there were still two surfaces at
this point, see Figure 10.18. However, both the surfaces were now at the same x- and
y-values.
==PAGE:118==
104
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
FIGURE 10.17: Un-ﬁltered mirrored and aligned data.
FIGURE 10.18: Un-ﬁltered mirrored and aligned data side-view.
Averaging
The execution of this operation might be the easiest of the pretreatment procedures.
Nevertheless, it is one of the key features of the contour method. In this simple
operation the two z-matrices were averaged, which united the to data sets into one
common z-matrix. The function of this averaging operation is to cancel out the shear
stresses in the cut plane and to eliminate errors from anti-symmetric cutting. More
information on the errors from anti- symmetric cutting can be viewed in Section
5.1.2. In Figure 10.19, a surface plot of the averaged date is presented. The outcome
of the operations performed was so far, one single data set containing one x-matrix,
one y-matrix and one z-matrix.
==PAGE:119==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
105
FIGURE 10.19: Un-ﬁltered averaged data. Note that there is now only
one surface.
Filtering
As the data sets were now joined into one data set by the previously performed op-
eration, it was time to ﬁlter out the outliers. This could be done by many means
depending on the severity of the outliers, both in magnitude and quantity. In the
case where the outliers are of a small quantity, the ﬁltering can be done manually.
Here, one goes into the z-matrix and replace the values of outlier with suitable val-
ues. This can be the same as the ones of the neighbouring measurements, or a linear
interpolation of the two neighbouring measurements. In the case of large quantities
of outliers, the manual approach can be tedious and time consuming. Instead, a ﬁl-
tering command can be utilized in Matlab. There are numerous types of these com-
mands in Matlab, and the choice depend on the desired result. However, one com-
mand which can be recommended is, B = ﬁlloutliers(A,ﬁllmethod,movmethod,window)
[24]. Here, A is the matrix which one wants to ﬁlter and B is the ﬁltered matrix. The
threshold for deciding if a measurement is an outlier, can be adjusted by using the
many choices for ﬁllmethod, movmethod and window.
In the case of the preliminary experiment, see Figure 10.19, it was chosen to ﬁlter
manually, as the quantity of outliers were small and they were located conveniently
on end the of plate. The arise of these outlier on the end of the plate, seen in Figure
10.19, were due the fact that the stylus have surpassed the edge of the plate during
the measurements and registered points which were not located on the cut surface.
Therefore, these columns of points could be deleted form the data set. It must be
noted that when deleting points, one must delete either the whole row or the whole
column which the point is located in. These rows or columns must also be deleted
from the the x- and y-matrix, so that the dimensions of the matrices match. This is
way the ﬁltering command previously mentioned so advantageous. The rest of the
surface was seen as regular and contained no more outliers. This can be a result of a
good executed EDM cut, sufﬁcient cleaning of the cut surface prior to measuring and
==PAGE:120==
106
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
a somewhat short duration of the measuring (dust particles have not had the time
to collect on the surface). The ﬁltered surface is shown in Figure10.20 and 10.21.
FIGURE 10.20: Filtered averaged data.
FIGURE 10.21: Side-view of ﬁltered and averaged data.
Translation
Up until this point, the z-values have been relative to the coordinate system of that
made in the CMM. These values are of no signiﬁcance as long as they are located
in this CMM coordinate system. This is due to the fact that the relevant information
which the measurements containes, does not lie in the value relative to CMM coordi-
nate system, but rather the value relative to each other. It is also known that residual
stresses are self-equilibrating[38, p.1-2]. On that account, the stresses which lead to
the deformations upon relaxation of the steel, had to manifest equally around an
equilibration plane. This plane can be represented as a best ﬁt plane in the measure-
ments obtained. This plane was also the reference of the new relevant coordinate
system which the z-values had to be translated to. Thus, making the equilibrium
plane/best ﬁt plane the new xy-plane, with an z elevation equal to zero. In Figure
==PAGE:121==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
107
10.22, the averaged data from Figure 10.20 and 10.21 is plotted together with the
equilibrium plane/best ﬁt plane.
FIGURE 10.22: Averaged data together with equilibrium plane and
centre point.
This best ﬁt plane was generated from the Curve Fitting Toolbox[23] in Matlab1.
Here, the x-, y- and z-matrices were loaded and polynomial was chosen from the
dropdown menu. The degree of the polynomial was selected to be of ﬁrst degree in
both x- and y-direction, creating a linear plane. In the result window, the function
of the linear polynomial is shown, see Equation (10.1), together with its respective
constants2. A picture of the Curve Fitting Toolbox window is located in Appendix
H.13. This is thereafter used to code a simple function of the linear polynomial where
the input values are the x- and y-coordinates. Now, all the z-values for the best ﬁt
plane in relation to the x- and y-coordinates 4, could be obtained.
f(x, y) = p00 + p10x + p01y
(10.1)
To check if the best ﬁt plane is correct, one can extract the centre point of the point
cloud and compare this with a point calculated from the linear polynomial function.
In the polynomial function the input values are then the x- and y-coordinators from
the centre point. The difference between the z-coordinate from the centre point and
the point calculated should be of a very small magnitude, ideally zero. This method
is used since it is known that a centre point of a point cloud must be located on the
best ﬁt plane of the point cloud. The coordinates for the centre point were found by
averaging the x-, y- and z-matrices. The difference between the centre point and the
calculated point were in the present work of a magnitude equal to 3.9e-05, which is
regarded as close enough to zero to assume that the best ﬁt plane is correct. In Figure
10.22, one can see the location of the centre point on the best ﬁt; the red dot.
Lastly the translation of the z-coordinates were be executed. This was easily
done by subtracting the calculated z-coordinates from the best ﬁt plane form the
measured z-coordinates. A surface plot of the translated data is shown in Figure
10.23 and 10.24. Note the change in the z-coordinates in relation to Figure 10.21.
1One can open the Curve Fitting Toolbox by writing cftool in the command window.
2p00, p10 and p01.
3This screenshot is taken from the main experiment.
4Obtained in the aligning operation previously described.

FIGURE 10.23: Translated data.

Smoothing
In this operation, the goal was to smooth out the z-coordinates, so that all the points
were located on one continuous surface. Also, it was necessary to eliminate all the
minor imperfections of the measured z-values so that the resulting contour map was
readable. It was also auspicious to represent the surface as one function or a com-
pound function, so that the z-values could be extracted from the continuous surface
in any desired grid size. To be able to choose the grid size, proved to be advan-
tageous when deciding the mesh in the FE-model, more on this in the upcoming
subsection on the FE-model.
The method used in the present work is of the type B-splines and is chosen due to
recommendation in a paper authored by M. B. Prime et al. [33]. More details on the
theory behind the method is presented in Section 5.4, together with the approach
used in Matlab. One important detail which needed to be highlighted, is that the
grid used as input in the spline command5, the X and Y coordinates, is not allowed
5sp = spap(knotsy, knotsx, [kykx], Y, X, Z)
==PAGE:123==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
109
to be matrices. The solution for this was to use the ﬁrst row in the X matrix and ﬁrst
column in the Y matrix. Making the input grid uniform relative to these vectors. This
was considered not to be of any signiﬁcance, as the shift of the coordinates were of
a magnitude of 1µm to 0.1µm, which is a range that would not effect the obtained
results. Note that this shift in the coordinates are only in the X and Y coordinate.
Thus, not effecting the displacements, Z, which still was in a matrix.
The grid size used for extraction of the z-coordinates was equal to the measured
grid of 0.5x0.5mm. The grid chosen, had to match with the mesh applied in the
FE-model. It was shown that the computational time of this grid/mesh size was
within a reasonable time period6, even though the grid is regarded as very small in
therms of FE-modelling. On that account, this grid will also be used in the main
experiment. The knot density for the splines, had to be controlled to ensure that the
spline did not over or under smooth the data. This was done in the same manner as
presented in the paper authored by M. B. Prime et al.[33, p.179-180], where the knot
density was incrementally increased from a density of 0.1 knot/mm to 1 knot/mm.
However, this was not executed in the preliminary experiment, as this is regarded
as a straightforward procedure which is time consuming and where the numeri-
cal results from the preliminary experiment can not be used as mentioned earlier.
However, this knot density control will be carried out for the main experiment and
a detailed description will be presented in Section10.2.6. In Figure 10.25, a surface
plot of the data prior to the smoothing is shown and in Figure 10.26 a surface plot of
the data post smoothing is shown.
FIGURE 10.25: Prior to smoothening the data.
6Under 45 minutes.
==PAGE:124==
110
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
FIGURE 10.26: Smoothend data.
Extrapolations
As the measured points did not extend all the way out to the edges, as described
in Section 10.1.5 and shown in Figure 10.13 and 10.13, it was needed to extrapolate
points such that the dimensions of the measurements matched the dimensions of
the real plate [38, p.120]. This was mainly done to ensure that the FE-model had the
same stiffness as the physical plate. All the extrapolated values were regarded as
unreliable, and could not be used as results. The extrapolation operation was not
done for the preliminary experiment, being that the numerical results in the pre-
liminary experiment was of no signiﬁcance. Also the execution of the extrapolation
was regarded as straightforward and time consuming. However, this operation is
included for the main experiment and is described in details in Section 10.2.6.
FE-model
In the current work, the plate was modelled without the angular distortion that man-
ifested in the plate due to the localized heat inﬂicted during the welding. Neither
was the extrapolation area included, as the extrapolation procedure was not con-
ducted for the preliminary work. This means that the plate was modelled with a
rectangular shape of the following dimensions:
H : 10.5mm
W : 193mm
D : 150mm
The plate was sectioned into three parts by partitioning, as in Figure 10.27, where
the zone closest to the cut surface had smaller elements and the zone furthest away
had larger elements. This can be seen in Figure 10.27, where the ﬁrst zone extended
over 55mm, the second zone over 50mm and the last zone over 45mm. The smaller
elements were needed due to the large displacements applied to the cut surface.
The mesh on the cut surface is of great importance. The seeding of the mesh had
to be exactly the same as the grid spacing used for extracting the displacement values
7 in the spline. In Figure 10.28 the surface were the nodal displacements where
applied is shown, together with an enhanced picture of the mesh. In the current
7Z-values from the data processing
==PAGE:125==
10.1. Preliminary Experiment - Contour Method
111
work, the grid spacing in the spline were set to 0.5mm, hence the seed spacing in
Abaqus was the same.
FIGURE 10.27: Mesh of the plate in the preliminary experiement
FIGURE 10.28: Meshed surface where the displacement boundary
conditions were to be appleid, with a enhanced view of the element
size.
In Chapter 5, the basis of the contour method was explained. In Figure 5.2 the
super-positioning principle was shown, where the fully relaxed surface (state B) was
forced back to a ﬂat surface (state C), to ﬁnd the original stresses (state A). In the
present work the modelling approach were reversed, meaning that instead of forc-
ing the relaxed surface back to a ﬂat state(B to C), the ﬂat state was forced to the
coordinates of the relaxed state (C to B).
In doing this, the displacements found from the measuring of the relaxed sur-
face, were implemented in Abaqus as displacement boundary conditions. Hence,
the measurements were applied to the surface as nodal displacements on the xy-
plane, from z equal to zero, to z equal the measured displacement.
Note that in the data processing there were three coordinates, x, y and z, where z
was the displacements of the surface in the uniform grid of x and y. The best ﬁt plane
that was found in translating the measurement to an equilibrium state, represents
the ﬂat surface of the cut 8. Hence, all z-values for this plane is equal to zero. The
values previously referred to as z, are in the FE-model called u, and all the new
z-values are equal to zero. The format for the displacement ﬁle is shown here:
8The ﬂat surface here refers to state C in Figure 5.2

z

.
These values were loaded into Abaqus by the means of a python script that can
be seen in Appendix G. The script is written by Stergios Goutianos of the wind in-
stitute at DTU, for the sole purpose of the current work. The python script reads
the displacement ﬁle, locates the nodes in the model matching the xi,
yi
and
zi
and applies the displacement, ui in the z-direction. At the same time as the displace-
ment boundary conditions are applied to the surface, all nodal movements in x- and
y-direction is restrained. This ensures that a pure y-directional displacement for the
entire surface. The rest of the plate on the other hand, were only ﬁxed by three nodal
displacement constraints, to prevent rigid body motions, see Figure 10.29 .
FIGURE 10.29: The structural boundary conditions of the plate
The material of the part was modelled as elastic isotopic, hence only two material
properties were needed; the Young’s modulus and Poisson’s ratio.
10.1.7
Summary
A quick summary is made to get an overview of the ﬁndings and learnings from the
preliminary experiment.
When planing the EDM cut, a test cut was conducted, where two 5cm long steel
plates were cut. One with a roughness of Ra=1.7 and one with Ra=3.0. Due to
time consumption and ﬁndings in literature, it was decided that the an Ra=3.0 was
acceptable. Further on, when deciding on the placement of the cut, Saint Venant‘s
principle of characteristic length was used as the basis and lead to the usage of the
conservative formula, l ≥t3, see Figure 10.11. It should be mentioned that the best
solution is always to centre the cut in the specimen.
The operations sorting, aligning, averaging, ﬁltering and translation were found to
be of a simple extent, as long as a few precautions were followed during the mea-
surements. Some precautions to keep in mind during the measurering are:
• Handel the plate with utmost care at all times and avoid touching the cut sur-
face, except when it is cleaned.
• Align the specimen as good as possible to the CMMs coordinate system. This
gives a tidiness to the data set and will simplify the data processing consider-
ably.
==PAGE:127==
10.2. Main Experiment
113
• Program a simple measuring order, this makes the sorting of the data simpler.
No signiﬁcant changes will be made to above mentioned operations for the fol-
lowing main experiment.
The smoothening operation, were found to be a complex and somewhat time con-
suming process to grasp, both mathematically and in regards of the programming.
In the main experiment, this operation will be extended. There, it will be conducted
a knot density control, with the goal of ﬁnding the optimal knot density, so that the
splines does not over or under smooth the data.
The extrapolation operation was not carried out in the preliminary experiment,
due to time consumption and the simplicity of the task. Nor is this operation neces-
sary to be able to carry out the experiment. Nevertheless, in the main experiment an
analysis of different extrapolation methods will be conducted.
From the preliminary FE modelling, it was found that implementing the mea-
surements as displacement boundary conditions in Abaqus was more challenging
than expected. It was solved by the help of Stergios Goutianos, who wrote the
python script. This script could, without alterations, be used for the main experi-
ment.
10.2
Main Experiment
10.2.1
Introduction
This section is a continuation of the preliminary work, and all aspects of this section
should be seen in conjunction with the previous chapter. In contrary to the prelimi-
nary work, the numerical aspect of the main experiment was of great importance, as
well as the execution and knowledge gained.
When the main experiment was conducted, one had acquired a general overview
of the extent of the procedures involved in the contour method. The main difference
being the massive increase in the size of the main plate compared to the preliminary
plate.
The increase in the plate size gave rise to many challenges, the key factors be-
ing the plate width was longer than the cutting range of the EDM machine and the
limitations of the CMM regarding number of points in one measurement series.
The already welded plate of steel type S355ML was used in this experiment.
It was planned to execute two EDM cuts in the main plate, one in the transverse
plane releasing longitudinal residual stresses and one in the longitudinal direction
releasing transverse stresses. Both were of interest, as the longitudinal stresses were
expected to be in the range of the yield stresses, yet the transverse stresses are more
relevant to the fatigue cracking of the weld zone. However, due to the lasting mea-
suring time, only the transverse cut was executed.
The sequential measuring was done by a CMM and ﬁnally the results were pro-
cessed in Matlab and Abaqus.
10.2.2
Target
The target of the main experiment was to investigate the residual stresses that man-
ifested in a welded plate due to the localized heat input. However, as the contour
method is a fairly new investigation method, it was also of scientiﬁc interest to iden-
tify challenges in the execution of the method and limitations in regards of available
==PAGE:128==
114
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
equipment. In short, the feasibility of using the contour method for residual stress
investigations at DTU.
The execution of the main experiment was heavily built on the knowledge and
experiences obtained in the preliminary experiment. A target was therefore also to
establish if any of the challenges found in the preliminary work, could be optimized
in the main experiment.
10.2.3
Specimen
The plate used for the contour method was a steel plate of the quality S355ML. The
plate is made from two plates of the dimensions (250x500x45)mm (WxDxH) that
were welded together by SAW. The exact measurements of the plate prior to testing
were:
W : 497mm
D : 500mm
H : 45mm
The dimensions in the early stages of the experiment were believed to be (500x500
x40)mm. However, when the plate was measured by using CMM it was clear that
the thickness was notably thicker than expected.
In Figure 10.30 and 10.31 plate is shown after the welding and temperature mea-
surements, yet prior to any procedures involved in the contour method.
FIGURE 10.30: Top view of the plate used in the main experiment.
FIGURE 10.31: Side view of the plate, prior to any cuts made by the
EDM.
In Figure 9.7 it is seen that the plate had am end-plate attached on each side.
This made it possible to start the welding before reaching the plate, yielding a more
evenly distributed welding bead throughout the plate. The end-plates, together with
the welding material from the startup of the bead was removed prior to the current
experiment. In Figure 10.31, a side view of the plate is seen. However, the welding
==PAGE:129==

geometry does not stand out, as the removal of the end-plates are believed to be
removed by a grinder, hence the rough surface.
10.2.4
Setup
The setup can be divided into two parts - mainly the part regarding the cut (EDM)
and the part regarding the measuring (CMM). In the preliminary experiment, there
was also a welding part, but the plate used for the main experiment was already
welded and therefore not a part of the current work. Information regrading welding
of the plat can be seen in the report authored by Andersen and Hassan[2].
The setup for the main experiment greatly resembles the preliminary experiment.
Making the Cut
The +GF+ AgieCharmilles Cut E 350 EDM was used with the same speciﬁcations and
settings as explained for the preliminary experiment. However, there are some key
differences between the two experiments.
Due to the challenges with the cutting range of the EDM, 9 rails were installed
in the EDM machine for the purpose of sliding the plate without inducing to much
error of the sort "asymmetrical cutting errors" 10. The rails can be seen in Figure
10.32.
FIGURE 10.32: Rail system.
The clamping arrangement is as seen in Figure 10.33, where it was focused on
clamping symmetrically around the cut for the same reasons as explained in the
previous chapter. Even though there are only three clamps visible in the picture,
there was also a clamp placed in the top right corner.
FIGURE 10.33: Picture of the plate placed in the EDM with clamping
arrangement.
9This challenge will be discussed on depth in the procedure of the main experiment.
10Asymmetrical cutting errors were discussed in Section 5.1.2
==PAGE:130==
116
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
Measuring the Surfaces
The CMM was executed with a Zeizz OCM 850 machine, with the same equipment
and speciﬁcations as given for the preliminary experiment. Notably different from
the preliminary work, is the arrangement of the plates during the measurements.
In the preliminary experiment, a single clamp was used to fasten the test piece.
This forces the measuring of the cut-surfaces to be executed in two separate runs,
leading to two separate coordinate-systems. As a common starting point or reference
point was not used, it lead to a somewhat problematic sorting and aligning of the
data and increased the chances of error. A sketch of the single clamp set-up is shown
in Figure 10.12.
The new system, with twin-clamps, eliminated the complications related to the
single-clamp set-ut. The twin-clamp set-up insured a common reference point for
the two surfaces. This effectively united all the measurements into a joint coordinate-
system and lead to a simpliﬁcation of the sequential data processing. With a com-
mon reference point established, the measurements could be conducted either in a
single run or multiple runs, whichever is more feasible with the equipment at hand.
A crucial element when performing multiple measuring runs, is that the same refer-
ence point is used in all runs. A sketch of the twin-clamp setup is shown in Figure
10.34.
FIGURE 10.34: Plane view of set-up sketch, showing the principle of
the twin-clampe set-up.
The above pictures shows the intended setup and alignment of the two pieces,
while in Figure 10.35 the actual used setup is shown.
FIGURE 10.35: Twin setup of the plate and a side view of the setup.
==PAGE:131==
10.2. Main Experiment
117
10.2.5
Procedure
In this section the procedures executed in relation to the main experiment is pre-
sented. The procedure for the main experiment greatly resembles the procedure of
the preliminary work. On that account, only the dissimilarities of the two procedure
will be discussed. The main differences were:
• The plate was already welded, hence the process of the welding was not a part
of the present work.
• The cut was centred relative to the plate.
• The plate was to large for the cutting range of the EDM, hence the cut could
not be executed in one pass.
• The measurements had to be divided into ﬁve measuring zones due to the
limitations of the CMM. This altered to a large extent the data processing, es-
pecially the sorting of the measurements.
As in the preliminary work, a sequential presentation of the procedures will be
presented, starting with the cut, then the measuring will be discussed and ﬁnally the
data processing is presented.
Making the Cut
The enumerated list in the preliminary work explained the chronological order of
the procedure of the cut. This list, in its entirety, was also applicable for cutting the
main plate. The same EDM was used, with the same settings as in the preliminary
experiment.
However, in the main experiment there was a challenge regarding the size of the
plate. The machine had a cutting range of 380mm, while the test plate was 497mm
wide. This left two options, either choose another cutting method for making the
plate smaller or making the cut in two passes. The two considered alternatives are
illustrated in Figure 10.36. If choosing to cut the plate, by the means of water cutting
or another method with a minimum cutting range of 500mm, there would be larger
uncertainties related to the relaxation of the stresses. It was also planned to do a cut
in the longitudinal plane, in addition to the cut in the transverse plane 11. Making
the plate smaller would further increase the uncertainties related to the transverse
stresses. With the second option, where the cut is executed in two passes, there was
a chance that the cut line would get an angle in the transition from cut 1 to cut 2. This
is due to the fact that the plate had to be moved between the cuts to be within the
cutting range of the machine. When considering the averaging of the two surfaces
as a part of the data processing, it can be seen that the effects of an anti-symmetrical
cut would equalize in the process of averaging 12.
11see Figure 5.5 for deﬁnitions of plane
12see Figure 5.4 for illustration of principle
==PAGE:132==
118
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
FIGURE 10.36: Left: Option 1, where the plate is made smaller. Right:
Option 2, where the cut is divided into two passes.
Option 2, where the cut is done in two passes, was considered to be the safest
solution with the information available at that time. If the data had proven use-
less after the transition between the cuts, one could have discarded the data on that
side(cut 2) of the centreline and still have valid data on the other side, from cut 1, see
Figure 10.36.
Executing the cut in two passes required some extra considerations. First of all,
the placement of the transition needed to be where it would disrupt the essential
measurement the least. As the maximum stress was expected to be found in the
welding area, a placement as far away from this as possible was desirable. It was
decided that the ﬁrst cut would progress as far as the cutting range allowed, yielding
a length of about 340mm for the ﬁrst cut, and about 160mm for the second cut. When
shifting the plate between the two cuts, a rail was used to minimize the rotation of
the plate. In Figure 10.32, a picture of the plate-rail arrangement can be seen.
Measuring the Surface
As mentioned earlier, the measuring equipment used in the present work, was not
optimal in regards of the contour method, where the number of measurements needed
are of such a large magnitude. This created some issues for the measuring proce-
dure. The planned order of measurements explained in the sorting sub-section of
Section 10.1.6, could not be utilized. Note that the measuring order proposed in the
preliminary work, is still the recommended solution, and that the procedure used in
the main experiment is only applied due to the limitations of the equipment.
Before measuring, a grid spacing had to be chosen. In the main literature re-
garding the contour method, it was recommended to use a spacing of 0.5mm i both
directions. For the plate used in the current work, that grid spacing lead to about 90
000 points per surface, given that the entire surface was measured. The technician
of the CMM, Morten Røn Andersen, found that the machine had a point capacity
of 17 000 points per run measuring series. Thereby, each surface had to be divided
into measuring zones, where one zone represents one series of measurements. The
sectioning is shown in Figure 10.37, where PL1 and PL2 refers to the two halves of
the original plate and the zones are numbered from 1 to 5.
Another limitation of the CMM that caused complications, was the fact that the
CMM software could only measure rectangles. The main plate, as well as the plate
used for the preliminary work, had undergone some angular distortion and the sur-
face was no longer a rectangular area. Due to the difference in clamping and the
plates orientation relative to the coordinate system, the placement of the measured
areas on the surface did not match the one explained in the preliminary work.
As the measurements were divided into zones, it was possible to shift the rect-
angular measuring area, to include more of the surface. The principle is shown in
==PAGE:133==
10.2. Main Experiment
119
Figure 10.38. Note that the scale and dimension in the ﬁgure is not representative
for the actual plate or measuring zones. What can be taken from Figure 10.38 is the
placement of the ﬁrst zones, which were parallel to the one end of the plate. In the
preliminary work, the measuring rectangle was placed relative to the middle part of
the plate, while in the main experiment the measuring zones were placed relative to
one straight side of the plate.
On the surface of plate 1, zone 4 and 5 were shifted, while on the surface of plate
2, only zone 5 was shifted.
FIGURE 10.37: Cross-section sketch illustration the location of the
measuring zones.
FIGURE 10.38: Principal of shifting the measuring zone, to be able to
maximize the measuring area.
All the above procedures for measuring were implemented due to the limitations
in the measuring equipment. These are all approximations, and should be avoided
if possible.
10.2.6
Data Processing
In this section the data processing for the main experiment is presented. This section
must be seen in conjunction with the data processing for the preliminary experiment,
section 10.1.6, as many of the operations are executed in the exact same manner as
for the preliminary experiment. The operations are, sorting, aligning, averaging and
translation. However, comments regarding the results these operation yield for the
main experiment and some additional work done in these steps, will be presented
in this section. Further, the remaining operations will be described in detail, as some
alterations of the processing have been done in these.
The structure of this section resembles that of the data processing section in pre-
liminary experiment. The Matlab script made for the operations executed in this
section is attached in Appendix I,K, L, N, M, O and P.

Filtering
Due to the quantity of outlier in the averaged data, see Figure 10.50, it is decided to
utilize Matlabs built in ﬁltering command, B=ﬁllout(A,ﬁllmethod,movemethod,window),[24].
where B is the ﬁltered data and A is the unﬁltered data. In the bullet point list below
the settings used in ﬁllout is shown.
==PAGE:140==
126
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
• Fillermethod: linear - Fills using linear interpolation of neighboring, non-outlier
values
• Movemethod: movmedian - Outliers are deﬁned as elements more than three
local scaled median absolute deviations from the local median, over a win-
dowlength speciﬁed by window.
• Window: [15 15] - The window used in movmedian, where it looks at 15 values
before and after the value under examination.
The window used ([15 15]), was found to be a sufﬁcient window through per-
forming a convergent test. Here, the window was incrementally increased form 3
to 15. Thereafter, the Root-Mean-Square deviation (RMSD) was calculated from the
difference between a ﬁltered surface and the previous ﬁltered surface, See Equation
(10.2). A plot of the convergent test is shown in Figure 10.51.

Smoothening
The smoothening was done in the same manner as described in the preliminary ex-
periment, where the Matlab command for bivariate tensor B-form splines were uti-
lized, see Footnote 5. It was stated in the preliminary experiment that a knot density
control was to be conducted, with the goal of ﬁnding the optimal knot density for
the stress analysis. Following, a detailed description of this is presented.
The knot density is a measure of how often a knot14 is placed in the data set, both
in the x- and y-direction. This knot density is the main parameter which governs the
14The placement of where the polynomials is to be smoothly joined.
==PAGE:143==
10.2. Main Experiment
129
degree of smoothening and inﬂuence the stresses calculated in the FE-model. If not
dealt with, this can compromise the readability of the stress contour map or yield
higher uncertainties in the stresses calculated. The same method for ﬁnding the
optimal knot density was used in the paper authored by M.B. Prime, R.J. Sebring,
D.J. Hughes and P.J. Webster [33]. It is stated by M. B. Prime et al., That the us-
age of splines and this knot density control is the only objective way to determine
the smoothing, and yield superior results compared with previously used methods,
such as high-order, continuous, bivariate Fourier series.
The knot density was incrementally increased in both x- and y-direction, from
0.05
knots/mm to 1.0
knots/mm, with an interval shown in Table 10.5. There-
after, the displacements implemented was in the FE-model and a FEM-analysis was
carried out for each increment, followed by calculating the RMSD for the whole
stress series. See Equation 10.3 regarding the RMSD.
RMSDσi,j =
sPN
i=1(σi,j −σi−1,j)2
N
(10.3)
In Equation (10.3), σi,j, stand for the nodal stress at i, and j is associated with the
smoothing-spline solution. j −1 is a previous coarser smoothing-spline solution.

Furthermore, the RMSD was calculated between the numerous spline approxi-
mations of the measurements,u, and the measurements before spline usage,U. See
Equation 10.4, where U, is the measurements before spline usage and, u, is the ones
after. i, is the the point numbering. This was done to see how the uncertainty in the
measurements would developed as the knot density increased. The results for the
RMSD for both the stresses and the measurements are plotted in Figure 10.56.
RMSDui =
sPN
i=1(U −ui)2
N
(10.4)
Before commenting on Figure 10.56, it should be mentioned that the RMES for
stresses, with a knot density of 0.7, was presumed to be an outlier. The uncertainty
of the stresses with this knot density was substantially above the other values, at
195MPa. Therefore, this stress series was not included in the plot shown above. This
==PAGE:144==
130
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
FIGURE 10.56: RMSD for Stress and measurements as the knot den-
sity was increased.
lead to a better compliance with the graph presented in the paper authored by M.B.
Prime, R.J. Sebring, D.J. Hughes and P.J. Webster [33, p.180]. The plot included in
this stress series is attached in Figure H.2.
It is seen that when the knot density was increased the uncertainty of the spline
distortion decreased in relation to the measurements before usage of spline. This is
assumed correct as the resolution of knots increase, yielding a better ﬁt to the mea-
sured points. However, the opposite trend is apparent for the stresses, as the data set
is more and more smoothed as the knot density is decreased, yielding small changes
in the spline distortions at low knot density. It si seen, that when the knot density
is equal to 0.1 the uncertainty of the stresses are at a minimal, 16.83MPa. However,
the uncertainties for the distortions are quite high, 1.82µm. This, in addition to the
ﬁnding of an optimal knot density of 0.3 −0.4knots/mm in the paper authored by
M.B. Prime, R.J. Sebring, D.J. Hughes and P.J. Webster [33, p.180], lead to the con-
clusion of using the knot density of 0.3knots/mm. At this point, one can say that
the stress uncertainty is somewhat stable at 32.4MPa, in relation to 0.2knots/mm,
and the uncertainty of the distortions are lower than for 0.1knots/mm, 1.75µm. The
stress result for knot density equal to 0.1knots/mm, will also be shown in the anal-
ysis, Chapter 11, for comparison.
The fact that the uncertainty increased when the knot density was below 0.1knots/mm,
may indicate that at this point, over-smoothing occurred and details of the con-
tour may be lost. Likewise, the opposite can be seen in the interval from 0.4 −
1.0knots/mm, where there may occur under smoothing and to much detail from the
measurements were translated to the spline surface. When this details from the the
high resolute splines in the interval 0.4−1.0knots/mm were loaded as displacement
boundary conditions in the FE-model, the errors from the stresses were ampliﬁed.
The resulting surface which was used in further investigation have a knot density
of 0.3knots/mm and 0.1knots/mm. The surface with knot density 0.3knots/mm is
presented in Figure 10.57 and 10.58.
==PAGE:145==
10.2. Main Experiment
131

of 0.3knots/mm.
FIGURE 10.58: Side view of surface plot for further used spline, with

Extrapolations
The extrapolations of the measurement was conducted ﬁrstly, by the utilization of
a Matlab command for extrapolate15[25] and for-loops. Here the method used was
linear. This, however, did not yield satisfactory result. In Figure 10.59, surface plots
shows the linear extrapolation for the data using knot density of 0.1 knots/mm(A),
0.5 knots/mm(B) and 0.1knots/mm(C).
15vq = interp1(x,v,xq,method,extrapolation)
==PAGE:146==
132
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
FIGURE 10.59: Linear extrapolation of smoothend data. Knot density
of 0.1 knots/mm(A), 0.5 knots/mm(B) and 1.0knots/mm(C).
As one can see, the extrapolation method do not predict the point from the mea-
suring area to the plate edge in the manner which was desired. As this dose not
reﬂect the trends visible in the measuring area. One can also see that extrapolation
becomes worse as the knot density increases. The reason for this is believed to be
that the angular change between the measured point are to steep and result in an am-
pliﬁcation of the variations in the contour at the surface edges. This seems possible
as the variation increase together with the increase of knot density. Therefore, a new
way to get the desired result was thought out. The solution was to copy as many
columns as points needed to be extrapolated, in the x-direction, and adding this to
to the edges of the measuring area. Thereafter, the same was done for the rows in
the y-direction. The yielding result from this method, which will be regarded as the
copy-method, can be seen in Figure 10.60.
FIGURE 10.60: Smoothend data where the copy method is used. Knot
density of 0.1 knots/mm(A), 0.5 knots/mm(B) and 1.0knots/mm(C).
==PAGE:147==
10.2. Main Experiment
133
It can be seen that the tendencies of the surface contour is transferred in a more
desirable manner when the utilizing the copy method. Even though, this is more of
a ﬁtting procedure then a mathematical procedure.
Further, the linear extrapolation data, the copied data and data containing only
the measured area, for a knot density equal to 0.1knots/mm, was used in a stress
analyses. This was done to compare the two methods with each other and to see if
extrapolation was at all needed. In Figure 10.61, a plot of the stresses from the centre
line in each model is shown.
FIGURE 10.61: Stress plot of centre line for Linear extrapolation, copy
method and the use of no extrapolation.
At ﬁrst glance, it seems like the difference in the three stress analyses are of lesser
importance. However, when zooming in on the centre, where the maximum stresses
are localised, see Figure 10.62, a difference is apparent. Where the maximal stress
from the centre line for the copy method yields 662.35MPa, linear extrapolation
yields 663.2MPa and when not using an extrapolation method it yield 669.8. This
slit elevation of the maximal stress when not using an extrapolation method is pre-
sumed to be because there is less stiffness in this plate, due to a smaller cross-section.
The similarity of the linear extrapolation and copy model are as mentioned,
good. However, the knot density used (0.1knots/mm), is the one where the dis-
tortions are most alike. Compare (A) in Figure 10.59 and (A) in Figure 10.60. It is
therefore presumed that the difference will be enlarged when the knot density is
increased.
==PAGE:148==
134
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
FIGURE 10.62: Stress plot of centre line for Linear extrapolation, copy
method and the use of no extrapolation. In the range of 0.25-0.27m
Nevertheless, in Figure 10.63 the interval 0.4 −0.5m from Figure 10.61, one can
also see that the plate model where no extrapolation is used, is greatly inﬂuenced of
the lower stiffness. The two other methods yield similar results. Note that result in
the the interval 0.49 −0.5 is from the extrapolated zone and is not reliable.
FIGURE 10.63: Stress plot of centre line for Linear extrapolation, copy
method and the use of no extrapolation. In the range of 0.04-0.5m
This then result in that the model with no extrapolation were discarded. Fur-
ther, the model with linear extrapolation is also discarded, even though the results
match well with the model utilizing the copy method. This is because the linear
extrapolation model is presumed to show unreliable result when the knot density
is increased, due to the severe changes in the extrapolation area, shown in Figure
10.60. The likelihood that the extrapolated area is more similar to the copy method
is therefore conclude. The matrices generated form the copy method is thus used for
further investigation. In Figure 10.64 and 10.65, the extrapolated surface plots which
is used in the ﬁnal stress analysis is shown.

10.2.7
FE-model main
The measurements of the main plate showed that the plate was 45mm thick, not
40mm as ﬁrst assumed. The surface displacement measurements were divided into
5 measuring zones for both surfaces of the cut. These zones were slightly shifted
relative to each other. However, the data processing aligned the measurements, so
that all measurements where placed within a rectangular shape. Yet, the measure-
ments did not extend all the way to the edge of the plate, and an extrapolation of the
==PAGE:150==
136
Chapter 10. Experimental Works
measurements had to be executed to include the full cross sectional dimensions of
the plate16. This means that the plate was modelled with the following dimensions:
H : 45mm
W : 498mm
D : 250mm
It is worth noting that the results are only valid for the non-extrapolated zone of
the cross section, yielding a result area of:
H : 38mm
W : 489mm
As in the preliminary work, the plate was sectioned into 3 meshing zones for
variations of the seeding in the depth of the plate. The grid selected to extract the
z-values in the spline procedure, was for the main experiment chosen to 0.5mm.
Hence, the surface where the displacements were applied needed to have the same
mesh seeding. If applying the same mesh across the plate it would result in nearly 45
million elements. This would have given a high computational time, which would
have been unnecessary, as that small elements were only needed in the layers close
to the surface where the displacements were applied. The mesh zones were made
smaller relative to the preliminary mesh due to the massive number of elements.
The mesh used in the main experiment can be seen in Figure 10.66.
FIGURE 10.66: Mesh on the main plate, where the ﬁner elements are
close to the relaxation surface.
Other important aspects of the mesh were discussed in Section 10.1.6, which is
just as relevant in the main experiment as it was for the preliminary work.
Due to the measurement order applied for the main experiment, the plate was
modelled parallel to the x-axis, instead of parallel to the y-axis as in the preliminary
work. However, the same constraints were applied to prevent rigid body motions,
see Figure 10.67.
FIGURE 10.67: Structural boundary condition preventing rigid body
motions.
16See Section 10.2.6 for a more in-depth explanation
==PAGE:151==
10.2. Main Experiment
137
The process of importing the displacements from Matlab to Abaqus was done in
the exact same manner as explained for the preliminary experiment.
The material was modelled as elastic isotopic, with the following values:
E = 218GPa
ν = 0.3
The Young’s modules, E was experimentally found by monotonic tensile stress
testing. The testes were conducted by Hetlevik and Vedel [9] on a piece extracted
from the main plate. Poisson’s ratio, ν, which is used, is the most common used
value for steel. The elements used in the model were of the type 8-node linear brick,
with reduced integration (C3D8R).
