Results and discussion
11.1
Thermal Analysis
In this section, the thermal results from the simulation in Abaqus will be presented.
These results will be compared to the works of Andersen and Hassan [2], regarding
the temperature measurement they extracted during the welding process of the plate
PL1_2.
11.1.1
Results from the simulation
Heat ﬂow
The temperature loads were applied as a boundary surface temperature of 2500◦C
and a melting pool of 1500◦C in the volume of the bead. In Figure 11.1 and 11.2 the
heat propagation from these temperature loads is seen.
FIGURE 11.1: The heat propagation from the HAZ from the deposi-
tion of the ﬁrst chunk, t=5.5 s to 32.7 s.
From Figure 11.1 it is seen that the temperature drops from 2500◦C to about
400◦C within 25 seconds. In the ﬁrst picture (at t=5.5 s) one can see that the tem-
perature goes from 2500◦C to 20◦C in just 2 cm. This means that the temperature
gradient associated with the weld area is of great magnitude. This also supports
the assumptions made in the accuracy classiﬁcation, regarding not including rate-
dependent plasticity due to the time associated with temperatures above 0.5Tmelt.
The ﬁrst bead is deposited close to the bottom surface of the plate, which high-
lights the effect of the surface boundary conditions in form of the ﬁlm coefﬁcient
==PAGE:154==
140
Chapter 11. Results and discussion
applied to that boundary. It is seen that a higher temperature is found in the di-
rection of the bottom surface, than in the transverse directions. Meaning that the
boundary condition creates some accumulation of heat in this direction.
The later stages of the cooling period, shown in Figure 11.2 exhibit a far less mag-
nitude in the temperature gradient, where the temperature goes from 300◦C to 20◦C
in about 250 seconds. If looking back to the temperature dependence of the stiff-
ness of the material (Figure 7.5), it is clear that it is greatly reduced for temperatures
above 500◦C. Implying that the residual stresses mostly manifest where T < 500◦C.
From the two ﬁgures, it can be seen that this excludes the gray areas for growth of
residual stresses. However, as time progresses the gray areas shifts to orange, indi-
cating lower temperature where there is large growth of residual stresses. This can
be seen in conjunction with Figure 4.3 in Chapter 4.
FIGURE 11.2: The heat propagation from the HAZ from the deposi-
tion of the ﬁrst chunk, t=43.6 s to 289.9 s.
Fusion Zone and Heat Affected Zone
In Figure 11.3 and 11.4 the fusion zone (FZ) of the ﬁrst bead is shown together with
the heat affected zone (HAZ) for two different torch temperatures.
FIGURE 11.3: Top: FZ for the ﬁrst bead. Bottom: HAZ for the ﬁrst
bead. Both with a torch temperature of 2000◦C
==PAGE:155==
11.1. Thermal Analysis
141
For a torch temperature of 2000◦C the FZ is estimated to extend about 2 mm
beyond the volume of the bead. The HAZ on the other hand, extends a bit further,
estimated to approximately 3-4 mm. For a torch temperature of 2500◦C the same
measurements are estimated to be 3-4 mm and 4-5 mm, respectively.
FIGURE 11.4: Top: FZ for the ﬁrst bead. Bottom: HAZ for the ﬁrst
bead. Both with a torch temperature of 2500◦C
The melting temperature of the steel is set to 1500◦C, meaning that the steel (both
base and ﬁller material) is in liquid state above this temperature, and the two ma-
terials are free to fuse together, hence a FZ. By comparing the FZ for the different
torch temperatures, it is seen that a higher temperature yields a slightly larger FZ
as expected for an increase in temperature. The same tendency is seen for the HAZ,
with a larger accumulation of heat near the boundary surface.
Looking at the temperature in such a small specter, 1500◦C to 2500◦C, one can
determine the different zones explained in Section 3.3.1. Where the red and yellow
areas correspond to the UMZ and PMZ, while the blue and green represents the
THAZ.
11.1.2
Comparison of experiment and numerical model
The comparison of the experimental and numerical results can be seen as a direct
measure of the validity 1 of the thermal aspect of the model. By comparing the
temperature measurements from the experiment and the simulation it can be seen
that there was about 9.33% deviation between the two.
When evaluating the heat input it was found that it was about 45% high in the
simulation, compared to the experiment, see Figure 9.10. However, when seeing this
in conjunction with the temperature measurements, there is no correlation. On the
contrary, the temperature measurements show that the values are slightly low in the
simulation. A chunk length investigation was not conducted in the present work and
it was therefore speculated in if this might be the source of the contradicting aspects
of the thermal analysis. However, Ammbitzbøll and Sommmerlund [1] found that
the temperature dropped when the chunk length was decreased. Hence, would the
deviation between the experimental results and the simulated temperatures further
increased.
1A measure of the correctness of the FE solution in relation to the physical problem one tries to
simulate.
==PAGE:156==
142
Chapter 11. Results and discussion
FIGURE 11.5: Temperature measurements from experiment (black)
and numerical model (red)
The cooling tendency from the simulation show that the cooling rate is a bit to
fast compared to the experimental values. To optimize the model, one could mod-
ify the thermal properties. Lowering the thermal conductivity would most likely
have given a more precise cooling rate. If the cooling rate was slowed down, the
temperature peaks would presumably increased for most of the beads, yielding less
deviation between the two data sets.
It was attempted to vary the torch temperature, as the torch temperature inves-
tigation reviled that its value was to high. However, as the temperatures already
was on the low side of the experimental data, it only gave worse correlation in this
aspect. In addition, it was found that a 20% decrease in the torch temperature, only
gave a few percentage of change in the heat input.
11.2
Mechanical analysis
In this section, the results from the experimental work related to the mechanical
properties are presented. These results will be compared to other residual stress
investigations methods and the results from the numerical model.
The results from the contour method should be seen in conjunction with the pre-
vious chapter, regarding the experimental work. There, a detailed description of the
process of producing these results can be seen. Also the chapter regarding residual
stresses is of importance, as this clariﬁes the growth of residual stresses.
11.2.1
Results from the contour method
In the smoothening process of the raw data, a knot density investigation was con-
ducted to determine at what knot density the smoothened data had the lowest un-
certainty. It was found that at a knot density of 0.1 knots/mm, gave the lowest
average stress uncertainty, at about 17 MPa. However, for that density, the REMS er-
ror in the measurements where quite high, at 1.82 µm. It was also seen that the stress
uncertainty for 0.1 knots/mm did not follow the overall trend for the different knot
densities. On that account, it was concluded that both the measurements of knot
density 0.1 knots/mm and 0.3 knots/mm should be considered. The measurements
processed with a knot density 0.3 knots/mm had a higher stress uncertainty, of 32.4
MPa, however, the measurement uncertainty were lowered to 1.75 µm. A centreline
plot of the longitudinal residual stresses for both densities are shown in Figure 11.6
==PAGE:157==
11.2. Mechanical analysis
143
FIGURE 11.6:
Centreline distribution of the longitudinal resid-
ual stresses in the plate, for a knot density of 0.1knots/mm and
0.1knots/mm.
It is seen that the difference between the stresses mainly lies in the disturbance
seen in the 0.3 knot/mm plot. This was expected as a higher knot density yields
shorter distance between the ﬁtting points, resulting in higher resolution stress plot.
It is also seen that the maximum values of the centreline slightly deviates for the
two densities, with a peak value of 648 MPa for 0.1 knots/mm and 595 for 0.3 knot-
s/mm. With a nearly 50 MPa divergence, it was found that the result of both densi-
ties needed to be discussed further.
In Figure 11.6 a sudden increase in tension is seen on the left side of the plate,
and the contrary is seen on the right side, with a sudden increase in compression.
These are both cutting errors, believed to be caused by the bulge error explained
in Section 5.2.4. These areas are also part of the extrapolated areas which is known
to give unreliable results. In the following it should be noted that the extrapolated
areas are removed from all contour plots.
Knot density of 0.1 knots/mm
One of the advantages of the contour method is the fact that the whole stress contour
is mapped rather than points, as extracted from the hole drilling method and neu-
tron diffraction method. In the following, the contour plots for the highly smoothed
measurements are shown, see Figure 11.7 and 11.8. The coarse knot density means
that some characteristics of the contour may have been lost. At the same time, the ef-
fects of surface roughness, artefacts on the surface and measuring errors are evened
out.
FIGURE 11.7: Full contour plot of the cut surface, where red indicates
large tension and green moderate compression.
==PAGE:158==

Chapter 11. Results and discussion
FIGURE 11.8: Enhanced contour plot of the tension zone. All colours
indicates tension, where red means close to maximum tension (857
MPa) and blue close to zero tension.
Analyzing the plots, it can be seen that the tendencies expected2 are highly present,
with a narrow section of large tension in the welding area and large areas of minor
compression toward the end of the plate. The enhanced plot in Figure 11.8 shows a
maximum tension of 857 MPa in the area of the bottom bead. This is explained by
the fact that in the welding process, the bottom bead 3 was welded lastly. Hence,
partially releasing surrounding stresses. This is also the explanation for the large
stresses near the top surface, where beads were deposited late in the welding pro-
cess.
Knot density of 0.3 knots/mm
Choosing to use a higher knot density in the smoothening of the measurements lead
to a better caption of the actual measurements, however this might not be closer to
the actual stresses in the welded plate. In Figure 11.9 and 11.10 the stress contour of
the plate is seen.

11.2.2
Results from the welding simulation
The numerical model constructed for the present work, is here presented together
with the experimental results. The thermal experimental data where present prior
to the current work, and the model could thereby be optimized in regards of the
thermal properties. The mechanical aspect of the model on the other hand, was not
optimized relative to the experimental work as the stress results were obtained so
late in the working process. The model was also approximated in some regards,
most relevant to the results were the omission of the bottom bead. In Figure 11.12
the longitudinal stress distribution of the welding simulation is seen.
FIGURE 11.12: Longitudinal residual stresses of a centred cross sec-
tion of the numerical model, with the indication of the maximum
stress value and placement.
==PAGE:160==
146
Chapter 11. Results and discussion
From the contour plot above it is seen that the stresses accumulated around the
area of the last beads on the top side of the plate. There are little stress near the
bottom of the cross section. This was expected, as the bottom bead was not included
in the simulation, due to the difﬁculties with modelling a weld across the entire
thickness of the plate.
The maximum longitudinal stress are found to be 723 MPa in the upper half of
the plate thickness. These stresses are not considered to be affected by the omission
of the bottom bead, as they are located that far up in the cross section. If the bottom
bead had been modelled, it would have been expected to see an additional stress
peak in the lower part of the cross section.
To validate the boundary conditions and the general contraction forces in the
model, the displacements in the y-direction were compared to a rough measurement
of the distortion of the plate. In the numerical model, the largest deﬂections were
found at the ends of the plate, where the maximum deﬂection was about 2.8mm.
These deﬂections are shown in Figure 11.13. A rough measurement of the actual
plate showed a deﬂection of 2mm. This was considered to be within acceptable
correspondence.
FIGURE 11.13: Contour plot of the deformations of the numerical
model.
11.2.3
Comparison of experiment and numerical model
The experimental results is here compared to the results from the numerical welding
simulation. The centrelines of both results were compared, knowing that the centre
of the modelled weld might have been affected by the omission of the bottom bead.
In Figure 11.17 and 11.18 the two centrelines are plotted together. It should also be
noted that the numerical results shown here has not yet converged in regards of the
mesh. The mesh convergence test showed that with a more reﬁned mesh, the stress
values manifest closer to the y-axis, creating a more narrow stress distribution.
In the two ﬁgure below it is seen that the numerical model peaks slightly to the
right of the experimental values. The model was created on the assumption that the
weld was perfectly centred between the two welded plates. It is believed that this
assumption is the only reason for the offset, and if shifting the numerical values 12
mm to the right, the peaks align.
Comparing these results, it seen that the shifted simulation values correspond
well to the experimental results. Keeping in mind that a more narrow stress distri-
bution is expected for a ﬁner mesh in the numerical model.
The main effects of changing the boundary conditions were found in the com-
pression zones, when validating the model. As the experimental values were not
present at the time of constructing the model, assumptions had to be made in re-
gards of the stress distribution corresponding to the constraint of the plate during
the welding. However, seeing the results together, it is found that the assumptions
regarding the boundary conditions seams reasonable.

11.2. Mechanical analysis
147
FIGURE 11.14: Centreline distribution of the longitudinal residual

However, as the mesh convergence only included two beads, it difﬁcult to estimate
anything of the peak values from the numerical model. Regarding the chunk length,
Rasmus Buhl Larsen found that a small increase in the peak stress was found for a
shortened chunk length.
Lastly, the contour of the different results are shown in Figure 11.16. From the
plots it is evident that a peak stress near the bottom bead is missing from the nu-
merical results. On the other hand, the tendencies of the upper half of the cross
section, seems to correspond well with the experimental contours. The peak values
are shown to increase from the numerical simulation (723 MPa), to the experimen-
tal result with knot density of 0.1 knots/mm (857 MPa), and reaching a maximum
value for the knot density of 0.3 knots/mm (954) MPa. There is a substantial increase
between the three results, yet the tendencies correlates.
==PAGE:162==
148
Chapter 11. Results and discussion
It is seen that all three results, exceeds the yield limit in some areas of the cross
section, the experiment values more than the numerical simulation. A main dif-
ference is believed to stem from the fact that the material properties are modelled
including plasticity for the welding simulation, and elastic for the experiment. The
hardening process that occurs during the cyclic heating of the specimen, leads to a
change in the working curve of the material and an increased in the yield strength.
This is included in the simulation by implementing material properties found through
monotonic testing, yet including isotopic hardening and a single point of annealing.
In the experimental model the material is modelled as isotropic elastic, hence not
including any effects of plasticity or hardening.
FIGURE 11.16: contours
11.2.4
Comparison of Methods
The Neutron Diffraction (ND) method was used on a different plate in the same
period as the current work was conducted. That plate was of the steel type S335ML
and was also welded by SAW, however it had a different thickness. With ND, three
horizontal lines were investigated, a centreline and two lines near the edges. As
the results from the contour method are known to have unreliable results close to
the edges, it was only the centrelines that could be compared. The ND results were
provided by Thomas Dyhr Hansen.
As can be seen in Figure 11.17 and 11.18 that the maximum longitudinal stress
in the centreline was found to be 639 MPa from ND. The uncertainty of the two
measurement closest to the weld center was reported to be 37 MPa and for the rest
of the measurements it was about 30 MPa.
The maximum stresses in the centreline of the contour method was 648±17 MPa
for a knot density of 0.1 knots/mm and 595±32MPa for a knot density 0.3knots/mm.
Note that there is a large difference in the thickness of the plates used for the
different methods.
From examining the plots of the centrelines, it is believed that the welds of the
two plates where slightly shifted relative to each other

149
FIGURE 11.17: Longitudinal stress in the centreline of the plate for
a 45 mm thick plate investigated by the contour method with a knot

FIGURE 11.18: Longitudinal stress in the centreline of the plate for

A hole drilling experiment was also conducted in the same time period as the
present work, then on a 40 mm thick plate welded by SAW. As this plate resem-

the results from these two experiments. However, in the hole drilling experiment
the residual stresses was found 1 mm from the top-surface of the plate. In the con-
tour method the results in the extrapolated areas are unreliable, in the present work
the extrapolated area extend 3.5mm towards the top surface of the plate. On that
account, the two methods could not be compared.

Chapter 12
Conclusion
In this chapter the conclusions regarding the different aspects in the present work
is presented. The structure of the chapter is constructed in the same manner as the
overall thesis. Firstly, the thermal analysis is concluded. Thereafter, the mechani-
cal analysis will be concluded. Lastly, a conclusion regarding the feasibility of the
contour method at the DTUs facilities is presented.
Thermal Analysis
The thermal results gained from the simulation showed good correspondence with
the thermal time series used for comparison. The average relative difference be-
tween the two was below 10%, even though the simulation showed trends of a
more rapid cooling rate than the experimental results. Measures were taken to opti-
mize the numerical model against the experimental values. A torch temperature of
2000C◦was tested, in contrast to the value of 2500◦that was initially implemented.
This 20% decrease in torch temperature showed a minor difference in the change of
heat input, only a few percent, where a needed 45% decrease was desired. This also
lead to an even higher average relative difference between the simulated thermal
results and the temperature time series. A lowering of the thermal conductivity is
presumed to potentially decrease the average relative difference. As, this is known
to be a parameter with a high inﬂuence on the stress development in such a model
[49, p.975].
Regarding the construction of the model and design of the welding geometry,
it was found that the cross sectional welding geometry had a signiﬁcant impact
on the computation time. This is presumed to stem from the sharp angles in the
cross-sectional bead geometry. The reason for the extensive computation time when
utilizing sharp angels is reasoned by the fact that Abaqus has difﬁculties reaching
convergence in the calculations when the elements used do not resemble a cubic
form. It is therefore advised to simplify the cross-sectional geometry in future stud-
ies and focus more on the establishment of the thermo-mechanical parameters used
as input in the model.
Mechanical Analysis
The results obtained from the contour method are given in two contour plots. There
it can be seen that one plot shows the stresses generated from a knot density of
0.1knots/mm and the other from a knot density of 0.3knots/mm. The two plots
==PAGE:166==
152
Chapter 12. Conclusion
demonstrate similar trends, however, they have a difference of 50MPa in the max-
imum stress. These peak values do not manifest at the same location in the cross-
section. The plot with knot density of 0.3knots/mm, shows a peak at the upper part
of the cross-section of 954±32MPa. The plot with the knot density of 0.1knot/mm,
shows a peak at the lower section of the cross-section, 857 ± 17MPa. This is believed
to be due to the loss of details when low knot density is used. However, it is dif-
ﬁcult to conclude which of the knot densities show the most reliable stresses. It is
therefore reasoned that more research in relation to the uncertainties and errors of
the stresses and relaxation measurements is needed. A another interesting feature
of the results lay in the fact that stress levels are that high, these stresses well ex-
ceeds the yield limit of 501MPa. In the a paper authored by M.B. Prime, R.J.Sebring,
J.M.Edwards, D.J. Hughes and P.J. Webster[33], it is reported that the stresses in the
welding region are highly triaxial, leading to individual residual stress components
to exceed the yield limit. Such high residual stresses are routinely observed in the
contour method and neutron diffraction method. It is therefore also presumed that
triaxiality is a reason for the measuring of high residual stresses in the present work.
The comparison of the obtained experimental result and the results yielded from
the welding simulation show same trends in the stress plots extracted from the cen-
tre line. A small shift in the distribution is shown, but the general shape is looked
upon as correlating. However, the peak value from the simulation is located in
the rang of the yield limit ,548MPa., whereas the value from the contour method
is 648 ± 17MPa for 0.1knots/mm and 595 ± 32MPa for 0.3knots/mm. The reason
for the deviation seen in the peak stress between the simulation and experimental
values, can be due to the fact that in the experimental model only isotropic linear
elastic theory is used, whereas for the simulation, plasticity is included. As both the
contour plots and the graphs display corresponding tendencies, it is concluded that
the overall construction of the numerical model is representative for the stress de-
velopment, however some adjustments are needed to increase the maximum stress
value.
The results obtained from the contour method are also compared with the results
extracted from a neutron diffraction experiment conducted at the same time period.
Even though this ND test is conducted on a different plate thickness, the results
show great similarity, both in peak values and in distribution. The shift that can
be seen in the graphs, is of no signiﬁcances as they are result from two completely
different plates. The only common denominator of the tests is that both plates are
welded by SAW and the base material is of the same steel type(S355ML). The two
stress results are therefore concluded to be incomparable to a certain degree.
Feasibility of the Contour method
The feasibility of conducting an experiment using the contour method at the facilities
available at DTU, have shown to be adequate in regards of the EDM. The equipment
used has been of a modern quality and the efﬁciency of the execution was satisfac-
tory. It should be noted that in future work the specimen must be of a smaller size,
so that it will ﬁt within the cutting range of the machine.
The opposite can be reported in relation to the CMM. Here, the capacity of the
machine was of a poor quality, due to the outdated software and machinery. This
==PAGE:167==
Chapter 12. Conclusion
153
lead to en extremely long execution time for the measurements. The time used for
measuring the two cut surfaces ended up being more than two weeks. In this two
week period the CMM ran over 100 hours continuously. The software related to the
CMM was not able to process more then 17000 points at a time, resulting in the need
to split opp the measurement in zones. This was of course not favourable, as this
introduces a new source of error and complicates the data processing considerably.
The long time period related to the measuring is also not advantageous, as the spec-
imen is more exposed to the temperature changes and collection of dust particles on
the measuring surface. Thus, resulting in more outliers.
The software needed for the method was Matlab (or similar) and Abaqus. Both
were available at DTU.
==PAGE:168==
==PAGE:169==
155
Chapter 13
Future work
In the present work, it was focused on modelling the plate as true to the actual plate
as possible. This modelling approach proved to lead to many complications. In
future work it is recommended that the bead geometry of the plate is modelled as
simple as possible. Starting with a simple model to map the changes related to differ-
ences in bead geometry. An investigation in different values for the thermal conduc-
tivity is also recommended, so that one can optimize the cooling rate of the material.
In the present work it was tried to decrease the torch temperature in the numerical
model to match the heat input given in the welding parameters. This however, did
not give the expected results, as a decrease of 20% in the torch temperature only shift
the heat input a few percentages. While, the difference between the model heat out-
put and the welding heat output were close to 45%. Thereby, no conclusions were
drawn from this investigation. In future work, one should focus on extracting more
accurate heat output values in Abaqus, and relate this to the welding parameters.
The stress distribution from the numerical model seen in the current work, does
not include the high stresses located near the bottom bead in the experimental re-
sults. This is obviously due to the fact that the bottom bead was not modelled. In
future work, it should be focused on ﬁnding a solution for implementing a weld
across the entire thickness of the plate. It is believed that the solution lies in con-
straining the model in the right way, so that Abaqus is able to ﬁnd convergence in
its calculations.
The stress distribution of the experimental work showed values exceeding the
yield limit of the material. This is both due to the fact that the contour method as-
sumes elastic material and that the hardening process of the cyclic loading is not
accounted for. In future work, a link between the numerical results, including plas-
ticity, and the experimental results, elastically calculated, should be focused on.
There are also several aspects of the contour method that needs more investiga-
tion, some are listed below:
• Uncertainties: Many different ways of calculating the uncertainly of the stresses
were found. Just one method was implemeted here, and the subject should be
further investigated.
• Bulge error: There are ways of correcting for this cutting error, however this
requires several cuts being executed.
• Extrapolation: The extrapolation methods applied in the current work, clearly
gave unrealistic values in the present work, and a copying method was used
instead.
• Knot density: In the current work, some inconsistencies were found in the
knot density check in relation to the smoothening of the contour. The reason
for the outliers were not identiﬁed, and further work is needed.
==PAGE:170==
156
Chapter 13. Future work
• Measuring density: As there were some difﬁculties with the measuring equip-
ment in the present work, it was not possible to do a convergence test of the
measuring density, however this might show that a coarse measuring grid
could be applied.
• Roughness of surface: For obtaining lower roughness of the cut surface, the
cutting time increases. However, this might reveal the sufﬁcient surface rough-
ness.
The present work mapped the base principles of the method, however, as seen
from the list above, there is a lot of potential of development regarding the contour
method.

Optimale svejseløsninger
Robust og kraftfuld
Origo™/ Aristo™Mig 5000i samt Aristo™Mig U4000i og 
U5000i er ideelle partnere når det drejer sig om effektiv 
produktion eller præfabrikation af højtlegerede materialer med 
meget høje krav til svejsekvaliteten.
Strømkilderne er kompakte og robuste med et chassis lavet af 
pressede aluminiumsplader - Alutech™ - et materiale, der er 
stærkt nok til at klare en grov behandling. 
Strømkilderne er baseret på IGBT inverterteknologien, som 
betyder pålideligt udstyr med fremragende svejseegenskaber. 
Det CAN-bus baserede kommunikations- og kontrolsystem 
reducerer mængden af kabler og forøger driftssikkerheden.
ESAB LogicPump. Den patenterede ESAB LogicPump ELP, 
starter automatisk maskinens kølevandspumpe når en 
vandkølet svejsepistol tilsluttes trådboksene Aristo™YardFeed 
2000, RoboFeed 3004, Feed 3004 eller Aristo™Feed 4804. 
Dette forhindrer risikoen for overophedning af pistolen. Når en 
egenkølet pistol er i brug, slukker pumpen automatisk, hvilket 
reducerer støjniveauet og sikrer en længere levetid for 
kølepumpen.
Aristo™ Selector er en effektiv løsning ved opgaver, som 
kræver et hurtigt og nemt skift mellem 2 forskellige 
tråddimensioner eller trådtyper. Tilslut 2 x Aristo™Feed til 1 
Aristo™Mig og programvælgeren giver automatisk strøm til 
den trådtype/dimension, der arbejdes med.

Enhederne er optimerede til at arbejde sammen med 
trådboksene Aristo™YardFeed 2000, RoboFeed 3004, Feed 
3004 og Aristo™Feed 4804. Tilslutningskabler på op til 35 m 
giver en meget stor arbejdsradius og gør arbejdet mere 
fleksibelt. 
ESABs TrueArcVoltage System™, kombineret med en ESAB 
PSF™ pistol, garanterer at der svejses med den korrekte 
lysbuespænding, uanset om der forekommer spændingsfald i 
svejsekablerne. Dette betyder, at man opnår den samme 
lysbuespænding og den samme svejsekvalitet, uanset om der 
anvende et kort kabel og en 3 m pistol eller om der anvendes 
35 m kabler.

vælges mellem mere end 200 forprogrammerede synergilinier. 
Svejsning. som er baseret på forprogrammerede data, reducerer 
forberedelserne før svejsestart til et minimum.

svejseparametre øger funktionaliteten yderligere. Desuden giver 
flere knapper med fleksible funktioner direkte adgang til Hot 
Start, kraterfyldning, 2/4-takt osv.

2008-12-10 / ESAB forbeholder sig ret til at ændre specifikationerne uden foregående varsel
Service og support med 
maksimal tilgængelighed 
ESABs engagement og evne til at levere 
den forventede service og support 
begynder straks, efter at ordren er 
bekræftet. Vores mål er at tilbyde vore 
kunder et koncept for eftersalg, som 
passer til den enkelte kundes behov. En 
stærk og kompetent serviceorganisation er 
parat til at tilbyde servicekontrakter og 
vedligeholdelsesaftaler, som omfatter 
kalibrering, validering og opgradering af 
udstyr og software. Serviceorganisationen 
kan tilbyde standardiserede løsninger for 
tilpasning eller modificering af eksisterende 
produkter. 
Slid- og reservedele fremstilles i henhold til 
ESABs koncept for kvalitetssikring. 
Udskiftning af PCB (Printed Circuit Board), 
erstatningsmoduler og -komponenter er til 
rådighed for mange af vore produkter for at 
reducere driftsstop til et absolut minimum.
ESAB uddanner løbende vores 
autoriserede servicepartnere, som leverer 
den lokale service og support. Produkt- og 
procesoplæring til brugere kan tilbydes 
som et tillæg til den samlede løsning.
Spørg altid din lokale ESAB-repræsentant 
eller -distributør til råds angående en 
samlet løsning.
==PAGE:196==
Railtrac B42V
A truly unique and flexible multi-purpose 
system for welding and cutting.
XA00185220
Industry
Ship and Offshore Yards
Ship/Barge Building
Pipeline
Repair and Maintenance
Heavy Fabrication
Structural Steel Fabrication
Tank and Vessel Fabrication
Windtower
Railtrac B42V is a welding tractor system of 
components that can be configured to create the 
optimal solution for your mechanized welding 
application. To minimize problems associated with 
harsh environments, most mechanical parts are 
constructed in aluminum or stainless steel. Railtrac 
B42V is designed for horizontal and vertical up/down 
when applicable. 
The system can be easily connected to most ESAB 
wire feeders with no major modification. Remote 
adapters have to be mounted in feeders Aristo© Feed 
3004/4804 and Warrior™Feed 304. Railtrac B42V 
features different torch holders for torches and 
applications.
42V AC powered from power source or battery 
driven with standard Makita® 18 V system. 
Possible to program a specific welding length with 
auto return to start position. 
Stepper motors both for drive and weaving function 
with extremely high precision. Wide speed range 
available in a single unit.
Remote is programmable for weave patterns and 
travel speed and is capable of controlling voltage 
and wire feed speed in up to 5 standard programs.
Unit can be programmed and run directly from the 
controls on the Railtrac machine if the remote 
control is lost, damaged or not preferred. 
Visit esab.com for more information.
